The Second Doubt: Is the Vegetative Soul a Name or a Substantial Form?
Raises the second doubt about the nature of the vegetative soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second doubt about whether the vegetative soul is merely a name for the vegetative powers or a substantial form that is the source of those powers and also sensation, movement, and intellection in higher beings.

Core Idea
The vegetative soul could be a substantial form underlying all vegetative and sensitive powers.

After the first doubt about the multiplicity of powers, a second question arises: What is the vegetative soul? Is it just a collective name for the powers, or is it a substantial form that serves as the principle for these powers in plants, and for sensation, movement, and even intellection in higher beings? This doubt touches on the metaphysical status of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second doubt
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the two alternatives presented: the vegetative soul as a name or as a substantial form.
Notice how the passage extends the role of the substantial form to include sensation in animals and intellection in humans.
2
Compare the two views
Consider the implications of each view. If it is a name, the soul is not a single entity but a collection. If it is a substantial form, it is a unified principle that manifests different powers in different beings.
3
Reflect on the hierarchy
The substantial form view implies a hierarchy: the same form gives vegetative powers to plants, adds sensation and movement to animals, and adds intellect to humans.
This reflects the idea of the soul as a single reality with multiple faculties.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson reveals that even the lowest vegetative powers are connected to the highest intellectual ones. How might this change the way you view the acts of eating, sleeping, and maintaining your physical health?
?
The passage asks whether the soul is just a name for different powers or a single reality. In your own experience, do you feel fragmented by your different desires and abilities, or can you sense a single self behind them all? What would it take to live from that unified source?

The Third Doubt: Is the Nourishing Power Identical to the Digestive Powers?
Presents the third doubt concerning the identity of the nourishing power with digestive powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt about whether the nourishing power is distinct from the attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive powers, or whether it is identical to them, with some considering them one in essence but differing in consideration.

Core Idea
The nourishing power might be the same as the four digestive powers viewed from different angles.

The third doubt narrows the focus to the nourishing power itself. Scholars question whether it is an independent power distinct from the four digestive sub-powers (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive), or whether these four are just different aspects of the same nourishing power. Galen's remarks and the view that they are one in essence with different actions are examined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third doubt
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the debate about the nourishing power and the four digestive powers.
Note the reference to Galen and the view that the four are one power.
2
Understand the two positions
One position holds that the nourishing power is distinct from the attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive powers. The other holds that it is identical to them, being just a collective term or a single power with different phases.
3
Examine the argument for unity
Some argued that there is a single power whose action is attraction at swallowing, retention after, digestion after retention, and expulsion after digestion. This suggests a temporal sequence of actions from one power.
This view aligns with the idea that the nourishing power is one in essence but multiple in consideration.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the digestive powers are clearly separate from the nourishing power because they have distinct names.
Correction: The passage indicates that many scholars, including Galen, considered the nourishing power to be merely a collective term for these digestive functions, not a separate entity.

Checkpoint
What did some scholars hold about the four digestive powers (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive)?
A) They are four completely distinct and separate powers.
B) They are one in essence but differ in consideration.
C) They are illusions.
D) They are all identical to the generating power.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt questions whether the nourishing power is distinct from the four digestive powers or identical to them, with some viewing them as one essence with sequential actions.

The Fourth Doubt: Is the Nourishing Power One or Three Powers?
Presents the fourth doubt about the unity or multiplicity of the nourishing power's components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth doubt about whether the nourishing power, if distinct from the digestive powers, is a single power with three actions (analysis, assimilation, attachment) or three distinct powers, and why Ibn Sina preferred three.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina held that the nourishing power is actually three distinct powers for analysis, assimilation, and attachment.

The final doubt assumes the nourishing power is distinct from the digestive powers. Even then, is it one power that performs three actions—analysis, assimilation, and attachment—or are these three separate powers? Ibn Sina's inclination toward three distinct powers is mentioned, and the passage supports this by noting that there are no other actions besides these three.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the fourth doubt
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the question about one vs. three powers for the nourishing power's actions.
Note Ibn Sina's view and the reasoning that there are only three actions.
2
Identify the two views
One view: the nourishing power is a single power with three actions (tahlil, tashbih, ilsaq). Another view: these actions are performed by three distinct powers.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's reasoning
Ibn Sina preferred three distinct powers because there are three distinct actions (bringing substitute, assimilation, attachment) and no other actions. Thus, each action likely has its own principle.
This reflects the principle that the effect of the one is only one, unless the agent is one in all respects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحصيل الغذاء وتشبيهه وإلصاقه واحد أو متعدد وتحيروا في كيفية صدور هذه الأفعال المتفقة عن القوى الضعيفة سيما إذا تؤمل ما نشاهد من أنواع الحيوان والنبات من عجائب الصور والأشكال وغرائب النقوش والألوان والتجأوا على ما هو موجب الفطرة السليمة إلى إذن الخالق القدير، وتقدير العزيز العليم. يعني لما كان كلامهم في باب القوى مبنياً على الحدس والتخمين دون القطع واليقين وقع متردداً في عدة مواضع منها : أن الغاذية والنامية والمولدة قوى متعددة متغايرة بحسب الذات، أم بمجرد الاعتبار، ويكون اختلاف الأفعال والآثار راجعاً إلى اختلاف الآلات والاستعدادات. مثلاً تفعل الغاذية النمو، فيما إذا كان الوارد زائداً على المتحلل، والتوليد فيما إذا صار صالحاً، لأن يصير منياً وحاصلاً في الأنثيين، ويعرض لأفعالها قوة أو ضعف في بعض الأحوال، لأسباب عائدة إلى المواد والآلات وزيادة الحرارة الغريزية ونقصانها . وكذا تفاوت في الحدوث، بأن يحدث التوليد بعد التغذية والتنمية، ويبقى التوليد دون التنمية، وتبقى التغذية دون التنمية والتوليد، وما تقرر عندهم من أن أثر الواحد لا يكون إلا واحدا . فإنما هو في الواحد بجميع الجهات، ومنها أن النفس النباتية اسم لهذه القوى في النبات وكذا الحيوانية في الحيوان أم هي صورة جوهرية، مبدأ لهذه القوى في النبات وللحس والحركة أيضاً في الحيوان ولإدراك المعقولات أيضاً في الإنسان، ومنها أن الغاذية هل هي مغايرة بالذات للجاذبة والماسكة والهاضمة والدافعة أم لا بل هي عبارة عنها ، كما يشعر به كلام جالينوس وغيره، وأيضاً ذهب بعضهم إلى أن الأربعة واحدة بالذات متغايرة بالاعتبار بمعنى أن هناك قوة واحدة، فعلها جذب عند الإدرار، إمساك بعد الإدرار، هضم بعد الإمساك، دفع بعد الانهضام. ومنها أن الغاذية على تقدير مغايرتها للبواقي، هل هي قوة واحدة فعلها التحليل والتشبيه والالصاق، أم قوى ثلاث متغايرة بالذات مبادئ للأفعال الثلاثة. وميل ابن سينا إلى الثاني، وهو الظاهر من قواعدهم، ثم إنها نفس القوى الثلاث لا قوة أخرى نستخدمها، لأنه ليس هناك فعل آخر غير إيراد البدل والتشبيه والالصاق
Obtaining nourishment, assimilation, and attachment is one or multiple. And they were perplexed about how these consistent actions come about from weak powers, especially when one considers what we observe of the wonders of forms and shapes and the marvels of designs and colors among the types of animals and plants. And they resorted, according to what sound nature requires, to the permission of the powerful Creator and the decree of the Mighty, the All-Knowing. That is, because their discussion regarding the powers was based on conjecture and estimation rather than certainty and conviction, it wavered in several places, among them: whether the nourishing, growing, and generating powers are multiple and distinct in essence, or merely in consideration, and the difference of actions and effects goes back to the difference of instruments and aptitudes. For example, the nourishing power performs growth when the incoming surplus exceeds the decomposed, and generation when it becomes suitable to become semen and in the testicles. And its actions may be strong or weak in certain states due to causes returning to materials and instruments and increase or decrease of natural heat. Likewise, they differ in occurrence: generation occurs after nutrition and growth, generation remains without growth, nutrition remains without growth and generation. And what is established with them that the effect of the one is only one is (applicable) only to the one in all respects. And among them: whether the vegetative soul is a name for these powers in plants, and likewise the animal soul in animals, or is it a substantial form, a principle for these powers in plants, and for sensation and movement also in animals, and for the perception of intelligibles also in humans. And among them: whether the nourishing power is essentially distinct from the attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive powers, or is it an expression of them, as the speech of Galen and others indicates. And also, some held that the four are one in essence, differing in consideration, meaning there is one power whose action is attraction at the time of swallowing, retention after swallowing, digestion after retention, expulsion after digestion. And among them: supposing the nourishing power is distinct from the rest, is it one power whose action is analysis, assimilation, and attachment, or three distinct powers in essence, principles for the three actions? Ibn Sina inclines to the second, and it is apparent from their rules, and then they are the same three powers, not another power that employs them, because there is no other action besides bringing the substitute, assimilation, and attachment.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of doubts (mutaraddid) concerning the vegetative powers. It begins by noting that the discussion about powers is based on conjecture, leading to several uncertain points. The first doubt: whether the nourishing, growing, and generating powers are multiple and distinct in essence or merely conceptual distinctions, with differences in actions due to instruments and preparations. The author gives examples: the nourishing power causes growth when intake exceeds decomposition, and generation when material becomes suitable. Their actions vary in strength and order. The principle that 'the effect of the one is only one' is qualified to apply only when the agent is one in all respects. The second doubt: whether the vegetative soul is just a name for these powers or a substantial form that is the source of these powers. The third doubt: whether the nourishing power is distinct from the four digestive powers (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) or identical to them, as some including Galen imply. Some hold they are one in essence but different in consideration. The fourth doubt: supposing nourishing power is distinct from the others, is it one power with three actions (analysis, assimilation, attachment) or three distinct powers? Ibn Sina inclines to the latter, which is apparent from their rules. The passage concludes that these three actions are performed by the three powers, and there is no additional power.

Correction: The passage and Ibn Sina's view hold that these actions are performed by three distinct powers, each responsible for one action.

Checkpoint

C) Because Galen said so.
D) Because it is not possible for one power to have three actions.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you sit down to eat, how often do you pause to consider that every bite involves a complex orchestration of analysis, assimilation, and attachment — each a distinct act of divine design? What would it mean for your gratitude if you recognized each of these as a separate gift from Allah?
?
Ibn Sina’s analysis breaks down a single function into three distinct powers. Where else in your life do you tend to overlook the intricate details of Allah’s provision? How might a deeper awareness of these details change your sense of dependence on Him moment by moment?
?
The argument here insists that three distinct actions cannot be performed by a single undivided power. Reflect on the unity of your own self: you perform countless actions daily, yet you experience yourself as one person. What does this tell you about the Creator who is absolutely One, yet the source of all diverse actions? How does this move you to deeper tawḥīd?

The Animal Power and Objections to It
Defines animal power, gives evidence, and presents the first objection with reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physicians' concept of animal power, the evidence for its existence, and the first objection concerning the possibility of latent powers.

Core Idea
A power may exist even when its action is prevented.

Physicians propose a third power—animal power—to explain why paralyzed limbs stay alive. This lesson examines their definition, evidence, and the objection that absence of action does not prove absence of power. The passage also raises a further objection about a single power having multiple effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage fully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the flow from definition to evidence to objection.
Note the technical terms: قوة حيوانية, مفلوج, ذابل.
2
Analyze the definition and evidence
Identify how physicians define animal power and what evidence they use from paralyzed and withered limbs.
The evidence rests on the preservation of life despite loss of other powers.
3
Examine the first objection and reply
Understand why the objector denies the negation of sensation/movement and nutrition, and the reply about specific conditions and obstacles.
The objection shows logical reasoning about causes and effects.
4
Consider the second objection
Notice the beginning of a second objection: a single power cannot be a principle for multiple actions. The passage ends here, so think about possible replies.
This incomplete section prompts reflection on the unity of powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يثبت) قوة أخرى هي مبدأ لها تخص باسم القوة الحيوانية توجد في العضو المفلوج والذابل. يعني أن الأطباء يثبتون جنساً آخر من القوى يسمونها القوة الحيوانية، ويجعلونها مبدأ القوى النفسانية، حيث يفسرونها بالقوة التي إذا حصلت في الأعضاء هيأتها لقبول الحس والحركة وأفعال الحياة كجعل الغذاء بحيث يصلح لتغذية بدن الحيوان وكحركات الانبساط عند الغضب والفرح، والانقباض عند الخوف والغم. ويستدلون على ذلك، بأن في العضو المفلوج أو الذابل، قوة تحفظ عليه الحياة، وتمنعه النقص والفساد. وليست هي قوة الحس والحركة لفقدها في المفلوج، ولا قوة التغذية لفقدها في الذابل، فهي التي نسميها القوة الحيوانية. واعترض عليه من وجهين : أحدهما : أنا لا نسلم انتفاء قوة الحس والحركة في المفلوج، وقوة التغذية في الذابل لجواز أن توجد القوة، ولا يترتب عليها الفعل، لفقد شرط، أو وجود مانع، فإن قيل : لو انتفى الشرط أو وجد المانع، لما ترتب حفظ الحياة . قلنا : يجوز أن يكون لبعض الشروط والموانع اختصاص ببعض الأفعال دون البعض. فإن قيل : القوة الواحدة لا تكون مبدأ إلا لفعل واحد.
He said: 'And there may be established another power that is a principle for them, particularized by the name 'animal power', existing in the paralyzed and withered limb.' That is, physicians establish another genus of powers which they call the animal power, and they make it the principle of the psychic powers, where they define it as the power which, when it occurs in the organs, prepares them for receiving sensation and movement and the actions of life, such as making food suitable to nourish the animal's body, and like movements of expansion upon anger and joy, and contraction upon fear and grief. They adduce evidence for that: in the paralyzed or withered limb there is a power that preserves life for it and prevents it from wasting and corruption. It is neither the power of sensation and movement, because they are absent in the paralyzed limb, nor the power of nutrition, because it is absent in the withered limb. So it is what we call the animal power. And an objection was raised against it from two angles: One: We do not accept the negation of the power of sensation and movement in the paralyzed limb and the power of nutrition in the withered limb, because it is possible that the power exists but the action does not follow from it due to the absence of a condition or the presence of an obstacle. If it is said: If the condition is absent or the obstacle exists, then preservation of life would not follow. We say: It is possible that some conditions and obstacles are specific to some actions and not others. If it is said: The single power is not a principle except for a single action.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage introduces a third power called the 'animal power' (quwwa hayawaniyya) proposed by physicians, distinct from the natural and psychic powers. The author explains that physicians consider it a genus that is a principle for psychic powers, and they define it as the power that prepares organs to receive sensation, movement, and life functions—e.g., making food suitable for nutrition and facilitating bodily movements in emotional states like expansion (anger, joy) and contraction (fear, grief). Their evidence: in a paralyzed (mafluj) or withered (dhabil) limb, there is a power that preserves life and prevents decay, despite the absence of sensation/movement or nutrition. Therefore, it must be a distinct power. Then the author presents the first objection: we do not accept that these powers are absent, because the power might exist but fail to produce its effect due to a missing condition or an obstacle. The reply: it is possible that some conditions and obstacles are specific only to certain actions, not all. Then a further objection is posed: a single power is only a principle for a single action—implying that the animal power cannot be responsible for multiple functions. The text ends without an explicit answer to this second objection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of an action implies absence of the power.
Correction: A power may exist but fail to act due to a missing condition or obstacle, as argued in the first objection.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of physicians for the existence of the animal power?
A) It is necessary for rational thought.
B) It preserves life in paralyzed or withered limbs despite the loss of sensation, movement, or nutrition.
C) It is found only in healthy animals.
D) It is the same as the nutritive power.

Key Takeaway
Physicians argue for a distinct animal power based on life preservation in limbs lacking other powers, but objectors counter that power may exist without manifesting due to conditions or obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a time when you believed you lacked a certain ability because you did not see its results. How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider what hidden powers—spiritual or moral—might still be within you, waiting for the right condition or removal of an obstacle before they can act? What one barrier can you remove today to let that dormant power manifest?
?
The physicians argued for an unseen power (animal power) based on observable preservation of life. In your own life, what 'preservation' or 'survival' you cannot explain by the usual causes points to a hidden divine power sustaining you? Are you acknowledging that power, or are you attributing it to mere chance or your own strength?
?

Will you investigate and supply those conditions, or remain content with latent belief that never moves you?

The Multiplicity of the Sense of Touch according to Ibn Sina
Ibn Sina enumerates four powers of touch and defends their multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the sense of touch consists of multiple distinct powers based on the diversity of oppositions it perceives.

Core Idea
Touch is not one sense but many, united by a shared instrument.

In classical Islamic thought, the nature of the senses was debated. Ibn Sina argued that touch is unique among the senses in perceiving multiple types of opposition (hot/cold, wet/dry, etc.), each requiring a distinct power. This passage from al-Shifa' presents his reasoning and defends it against objections regarding hearing and taste.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from statement to argument to objections.
2
Identify the four powers of touch
The text explicitly lists four types of opposition: hot/cold, wet/dry, hard/soft, rough/smooth. Each is governed by a separate power.
These are the four 'قوى' mentioned.
3
Understand the argument from Ibn Sina
Each opposition is a primary act of sensation, so each requires its own power. The unity of the organ gives an illusion of a single sense.
Ibn Sina uses the analogy of touch and taste spreading throughout the body.
4
Consider the objections and answers
The objection about hearing (which also perceives multiple oppositions) is answered by saying hearing's primary object is sound, and the oppositions are accidental. For taste, the answer is that all tastes share a single type of opposition.
5
Reflect on the key insight
The multiplicity of touch is justified by the fundamental difference in the types of opposition perceived, unlike other senses.
This shows the importance of analysis of sensory perception in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمس قوي كثيرة، بل قوى أربع. وقال في الشفاء : يشبه أن تكون اللامسة عند قوم، لا نوعاً أخيراً ، بل جنساً لقوى أربع ، أو فوقها منبثاً معاً في الجلد كله، إحداها حاكمة في التضاد الذي بين الحار والبارد، والثانية في التضاد، الذي بين الرطب واليابس والثالثة في الذي بين الصلب واللين والرابعة في الذي بين الخشن ،والأملس ، إلا أن اجتماعها في آلة واحدة يوهم تأحدها في الذات. :وقال أيضاً. يشبه أن تكون قوى اللمس قوى كثيرة، تختص كل واحدة منها بمضادة، فيكون ما يدرك به المضادة التي بين الثقيل والخفيف ، غير ما يدرك به المضادة التي بين الحار والبارد . فإن هذه أفعال أولية للحس، يجب أن يكون لكل جنس منها قوة خاصة، إلا أن هذه القوى لما انتشرت في جميع الالات بالسوية، ظنت قوة واحدة، كما لو كان اللمس والذوق منتشرين في البدن كله انتشارهما في اللسان ، لظن مبدؤهما قوة واحدة، فلما تميزا عرف اختلافهما، وليس يجب أن يكون لكل واحدة من هذه القوى آلة تخصها ، بل يجوز أن تكون آلة واحدة مشتركة .لها. ويجوز أن يكون هناك انقسام في الآلات غير محسوس . ثم قال : فإن قيل : فالسمع أيضاً يدرك المضادة التي بين الصوت الثقيل والحاد، والتي بين الصوت الخافت والجهير وغير ذلك ، فلم لم تجعل قوى كثيرة . فالجواب أن محسوسه الأول هو الصوت، وهذه أعراض لها، وتوابع بخلاف اللمس فإن كل واحدة من المتضادات تحس لذاتها، لا بسبب الاخر ولما كان السؤال في الذوق المدرك للطعوم المتضادة ظاهراً. أجاب الإمام بأن الطعوم وإن كثرت فبينها مضادة واحدة، بخلاف الملموسات، فإن بين الحرارة والبرودة نوعاً من التضاد، غير النوع الذي بين الرطوبة واليبوسة .
For the sense of touch there are many powers, rather four powers. And he said in al-Shifa': It appears that the sense of touch according to some people is not a final type, but a genus for four powers, or more, spread together throughout the whole skin: one of them rules over the opposition between hot and cold, the second over the opposition between wet and dry, the third over that between hard and soft, and the fourth over that between rough and smooth, except that their coming together in one instrument gives the illusion of their unity in essence. And he also said: It appears that the powers of touch are many powers, each one being specific to an opposition, so that that by which the opposition between heavy and light is perceived is not that by which the opposition between hot and cold is perceived. For these are primary acts of sensation, and there must be for each genus of them a specific power, except that when these powers are spread equally in all instruments, they are thought to be one power, just as if touch and taste were spread throughout the whole body as they are on the tongue, their principle would be thought to be one power; when they became distinct, their difference would be known. And it is not necessary that each of these powers have an instrument specific to it, but rather it is possible that they share a single instrument. And it is possible that there is a division in the instruments that is not sensed. Then he said: If it is said: Hearing also perceives the opposition between a low-pitched and a high-pitched sound, and between a soft and a loud sound, and other things, so why is it not made into many powers? The answer is that its primary sensible is sound, and these are accidents of it and its concomitants, unlike touch, for each of the opposites is sensed in itself, not by means of the other. And since the question about taste, which perceives opposing tastes, is apparent, the Imam answered that tastes, though many, have a single type of opposition between them, unlike tangible things, for between heat and cold there is a type of opposition different from that between wetness and dryness.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the multiplicity of the sense of touch, drawing on Ibn Sina's views and addressing potential objections. The author first states that touch is not a single power but multiple powers, specifically four, as mentioned in al-Shifa'. The four powers correspond to four types of opposition: hot/cold, wet/dry, hard/soft, rough/smooth. They are spread throughout the skin and appear as one due to the common instrument. Then a second quote from Ibn Sina reinforces that each opposition requires a specific power, using the analogy of touch and taste if they were spread throughout the body. An objection is raised: hearing also perceives multiple oppositions (pitch, loudness), so why not multiple powers? The answer distinguishes hearing: its primary object is sound, and the oppositions are accidents of sound, whereas in touch each opposite is sensed directly. Then an objection about taste: tastes are multiple and opposing, so why not multiple powers? The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) answers that all tastes have a single type of opposition, unlike tangible opposites which have distinct types. Thus the multiplicity of touch is justified by the diversity of types of opposition in tangible qualities.

Key Terms
اللمس
القوى اللامسة
التضاد
المضادة
الحس
الذوق
السمع
الشفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because touch is felt through a single organ (the skin), it must be a single sense.
Correction: The passage argues that multiple distinct powers can share the same instrument, and the apparent unity is an illusion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many powers does the sense of touch consist of?
A) One
B) Four
C) Two
D) Three

Key Takeaway
Touch is unique among the senses in requiring multiple powers because it directly perceives fundamentally different types of opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed something was simple because it appeared unified on the surface, like thinking the skin gives a single feeling when it actually gives many? What might you be oversimplifying in your own understanding of yourself or your faith?
?
Ibn Sina argues from careful observation of experience. When you feel a rough cold object, are you aware that your soul is using two distinct powers? How might paying attention to the complexity of your own sensations deepen your appreciation of Allah's creation?
?
You often rely on your senses without questioning their nature. After studying this passage, what illusion about your own perception might you be living in, and what would it mean to see past it?

The Sense of Taste: Definition and Conditions
Definition of taste with organ, conditions, and comparison to touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the sense of taste, its organ, the conditions for taste perception, and how it differs from touch.

Core Idea
Taste requires moisture and contact; touch needs only contact.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid defines the sense of taste and explains its physiological conditions. The author carefully distinguishes taste from touch, showing that taste perception depends on the mediation of salivary moisture. Let's examine the precise definition, the required conditions, and the comparison with touch.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get a sense of the author's definition and the key terms.
Notice the technical terms: الذوق, الرطوبة اللعابية, المماسة.
2
Identify the organ and conditions for taste
Locate where the author describes the power of taste, its location in the nerve over the tongue, and the three conditions: contact, salivary moisture, and freedom of saliva from similar/opposite tastes.
The conditions are: 1) المماسة (contact), 2) توسط الرطوبة اللعابية (mediation of salivary moisture), 3) خلوها عن المثل والضد (freedom from similarity and opposition).
3
Compare taste with touch
Find the sentences comparing taste and touch. Notice what they agree on (need for contact) and what distinguishes taste (need for saliva).
Touch conveys heat directly upon contact; taste does not—it requires saliva.
4
Understand the benefit of taste
Read the part about taste being second to touch in benefit and its role in appetizing and selecting food.
This shows the purpose of taste in sustaining the body.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that taste is simply a type of touch. How does this passage correct that?
The key difference is the necessary mediation of saliva.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها الذوق) : وهي قوة منبثة في العصب المفروش على جرم اللسان، تدرك بها الطعوم بشرط المماسة، وتوسط الرطوبة اللعابية، وخلوها عن المثل والضد ليتكيف بكيفية الطعوم أو تخالطها أجزاء منه فيغوص] . هو تال اللمس في المنفعة، بحيث يفعل ما به يتقوم البدن، وهو تشهية الغذاء و اختايره، ويوافقه في الاحتياج إلى الملامسة ويفارقه في أن نفس الملامسة، لا تؤدي الطعم، كما أن نفس ملامسة الحار، تؤدي إلى الحرارة ، بل الا بد من توسط الرطوبة اللعابية المنبعثة من الآلة المسماة بالملعبة بشرط خلوها عن طعم .
He said: And among them (the senses) is taste. It is a power spread in the nerve spread over the body of the tongue, by which tastes are perceived under the condition of contact, and the mediation of salivary moisture, and its freedom from similarity and opposition so that it becomes qualified by the quality of the tastes, or parts of it mix with them and penetrate. It follows touch in benefit, such that it does that by which the body is sustained, which is the appetizing and selection of food. And it agrees with touch in the need for contact, but differs in that contact itself does not convey taste, just as contact with a hot thing conveys heat. Rather, there must be the mediation of salivary moisture emanating from the instrument called the salivary gland, under the condition that it is free from taste.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of taste (al-dhawq). The author states that taste is a power spread in the nerve that is spread over the body of the tongue. It perceives tastes with the condition of contact (mumāssa) and the mediation of salivary moisture (ruṭūba luʿābiyya). Additionally, the saliva must be free from any similar or opposite taste so that it can be affected by the taste qualities, or parts of the tasted object mix with it and penetrate. Taste is second to touch in benefit, as it performs what sustains the body: appetizing and selecting food. It agrees with touch in requiring contact, but differs because contact alone does not produce taste; unlike touching something hot which immediately conveys heat. Instead, taste requires the mediation of saliva from the salivary gland, which itself must be tasteless. This explanation clarifies the physiological and necessary conditions for taste perception.

Key Terms
الذوق
الرطوبة اللعابية
المماسة
الملعبة
تشهية الغذاء
اختيار الغذاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taste is simply a form of touch and does not require any medium.
Correction: The author explains that although taste and touch both need contact, taste differs because contact alone does not convey taste; it requires the mediation of saliva that is free from any taste.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between taste and touch?
A) Taste does not require contact
B) Taste requires mediation of saliva, while touch does not
C) Touch requires mediation of saliva
D) They are identical

Key Takeaway
Taste perception requires both contact and the mediation of tasteless saliva, distinguishing it from touch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have tasted countless foods without ever appreciating the intricate conditions Allah placed for taste. How does knowing that your saliva must be tasteless for you to perceive flavor change your awareness of even a single bite of food? Will you now pause and thank Him for the gift of taste?
?
The author says taste does that by which the body is sustained: appetizing and selecting food. Think about a time you ate without any mindfulness, just consuming. This sense is for your benefit. How will you use your sense of taste in a way that fulfills its purpose of sustaining your body and being grateful, rather than indulging recklessly?
?
Touch conveys heat through mere contact, but taste requires a medium. In your spiritual life, have you ever assumed that mere contact with truth (like hearing a verse) is enough to change you, without the mediation of a heart free from opposing desires? How can you apply the lesson of mediation to your own heart's receptivity?

Definition of Hearing and the Role of Intermediate Air
Al-Taftazani defines hearing and describes the intermediate air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of hearing in classical kalam and the nature of the intermediate air that carries sound.

Core Idea
Hearing is the perception of sound through undulating air, whether from percussion or plucking.

Al-Taftazani begins by defining the faculty of hearing and clarifying the medium through which sound travels. He emphasises that the intermediate air is the undulating air that carries sound, regardless of whether the sound is produced by percussion or plucking. This foundation is essential for understanding the subsequent discussion on how sound reaches the ear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the definition and the discussion on intermediate air.
Focus on the definition of hearing as a power in the nerve of the ear canal.
2
Identify the key components of the definition
Note that hearing is defined as a power in the nerve of the interior of the ear canal that perceives sounds, and the intermediate air is the undulating air carrying sound.
Also note the mention of percussion (qar') and plucking (qal').
3
Understand the controversy regarding Avicenna's phrasing
Al-Taftazani points out an apparent contradiction in Avicenna's al-Shifa where he restricts sound cause to percussion despite acknowledging plucking. This sets up the need for clarification.
This is a minor point but shows the scholarly context.
4
Reflect on why the definition is important
This definition establishes the physical basis for sound perception, which is used to refute later objections.
Connect to the overall topic of hearing in kalam psychology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها السمع): وهي قوة في عصب باطن الصماخ يدرك بها الأصوات. قد سبق في بحث الصوت ما يغني عن شرح هذا الموضع، والمراد بالهواء المتوسط، هو المتموج الحامل للصوت، سواء كان معلولاً للقرع، أو للقلع. ومعني توسطه بين القارع والمقروع، كونه بين الجزء الذي يفعل الصدم بعد الصدم، وبين تجويف الصماخ، وهذا ظاهر وإنما الإشكال في عبارة الشفاء. أن السامعة قوة مرتبة في العصب المتفرق في سطح الصماخ يدرك صورة ما يتأدى إليه من تموج الهواء المنضغط بين قارع، ومقروع، مقاوم له انضغاطاً بعنف، يحدث منه صوت، فيتأدى تموجه إلى الهواء المحصور الراكد في تجويف الصماخ، ويحركه بشكل حركته، حيث اقتصر في سبب الصوت على القرع، مع تصريحه بأنه قد يكون بالقلع. قال (ولا يمتنع) أن يقوم الصوت بكل جزء من أجزاء الهواء النافذة في المنافذ الضيقة ويكون السماع مشروطاً بكون الوصول أولاً لعدم الانفعال عن المماثل. إشارة إلى دفع إشكالين: أحدهما أن الهواء المتموج يمتنع أن يبقى على هيئته، من تقصيعات الحروف وتشكيلاتها، عند دخوله في المنافذ الضيقة، ومصادماته للجدران الصلبة. وثانيهما: أن الهواء الحامل للصوت، إن قام الصوت بمجموعه، لزم أن لا يسمعه إلا واحد من الحاضرين، لأنه بمجموعه لا يصل إلا إلى صماخ واحد، وإن قام بكل جزء منه لزم أن يسمعه كل سامع مراراً بعدد بتأدى اليه من أجزاء الهواء المتموج.
He said: "And among them is hearing": it is a power in the nerve of the interior of the ear canal by which sounds are perceived. The discussion on sound has already covered what suffices for explaining this point. What is meant by the intermediate air is the undulating air that carries sound, whether it is caused by percussion or by plucking. The meaning of its intermediacy between the striker and the struck is that it is between the part that repeatedly produces the impact and the cavity of the ear canal. This is obvious; the only difficulty is in the expression of al-Shifa: that the auditory faculty is a power arranged in the nerve distributed over the surface of the ear canal, perceiving the form of what reaches it from the undulation of air compressed between a striker and a struck object that resists it with violent compression, which generates sound, then its undulation reaches the confined stagnant air in the cavity of the ear canal and moves it with its motion, given that he (i.e., Avicenna) restricted the cause of sound to percussion, despite his statement that it can also be by plucking. He said: "And it is not impossible" that sound subsists in every part of the parts of air passing through narrow passages, and hearing is conditioned by the arrival first, because there is no passive influence from the similar [part]. This is an indication to refute two objections: One of them: that the undulating air cannot preserve its form of the inflections of letters and their configurations when it enters narrow passages and collides with solid walls. The second: that the air that carries sound, if the sound subsists in its whole, then it would necessarily be heard only by one of those present, because as a whole it reaches only one ear canal; and if it subsists in every part of it, then every hearer would necessarily hear it multiple times according to the number of parts of the undulating air that reach him.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines the faculty of hearing and then addresses two objections about how sound reaches the ear. First, hearing is defined as a power in the nerve of the inner ear canal by which sounds are perceived. The discussion then refers back to an earlier treatment of sound, clarifying that the intermediate air is the undulating air that carries sound, whether caused by percussion or by plucking. The intermediacy is between the striker and the struck. The author notes a difficulty in Avicenna’s phrasing in al-Shifa, where Avicenna restricts the cause of sound to percussion despite acknowledging plucking. Then the author quotes a statement: 'It is not impossible that sound subsists in every part of the air passing through narrow passages, and hearing is conditioned by the first arrival because there is no passive influence from the similar part.' This is an indication to refute two objections: First, that the undulating air cannot preserve the inflections of letters and configurations when entering narrow passages and colliding with solid walls. The answer implies that sound can subsist in each part and does not require the whole air to preserve the shape. Second, that if sound subsists in the whole air, only one person would hear; if it subsists in each part, each hearer would hear it multiple times. The answer is that hearing is conditioned by the first arrival, so each hearer hears only once from the first part that reaches, thus avoiding multiple hearing. The passage thus defends the possibility of sound propagation such that many can hear the same sound distinctly.

Key Terms
السمع
الصماخ
الهواء المتوسط
القرع
القلع
التموج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'intermediate air' that carries sound?
A) The air inside the ear canal
B) The undulating air that carries sound
C) The air compressed between striker and struck
D) The air that is stagnant in the cavity

Key Takeaway
Hearing is the perception of sound transmitted through undulating air, whether generated by percussion or plucking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given the faculty of hearing through a precise mechanism of undulating air and inner ear nerves. How often do you pause to thank Allah for this gift? Do you use your hearing to listen to the Quran and beneficial knowledge, or do you waste it on gossip, music, and idle talk? Resolve today to guard your ears from what displeases Allah and fill them with remembrance.
?
The intermediate air carries sound from the striker to your ear. Every word you hear travels through this medium. Are the words you listen to drawing you closer to Allah or pulling you away? Think of one specific sound you will avoid for His sake starting today.
?
If hearing is a power in the nerve of the inner ear, then you are responsible for what you expose that nerve to. Have you considered that your hearing will testify for or against you on the Day of Judgment? What change can you make right now to ensure your hearing bears witness to your faith?

Resolving Two Objections about Sound Propagation
Al-Taftazani refutes two objections about sound propagation and multiple hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes two objections regarding the propagation of sound through narrow passages and the possibility of multiple hearings.

Core Idea
Sound subsists in each part of the air, and hearing is conditioned by first arrival, so many can hear the same sound distinctly.

After defining hearing, al-Taftazani turns to two objections that challenge the physical explanation of sound reaching the ear. The first objection concerns how the undulating air can preserve the inflections of speech through narrow passages and collisions. The second objection addresses the problem of multiple hearers hearing the same sound multiple times. This lesson examines these objections and the concise refutations provided.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response section
Start at 'قال (ولا يمتنع)...' and read through the end. Understand the two objections.
The response begins with a statement that sound can subsist in every part of air.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection is that the undulating air cannot preserve the forms of letters and inflections when entering narrow passages and hitting solid walls.
This challenges how we hear distinct speech sounds.
3
Understand the second objection
The second objection is a dilemma: if sound subsists in the whole air, only one person hears; if in each part, each hearer hears multiple times.
This challenges the common experience of many hearing the same sound.
4
Grasp the resolution
Al-Taftazani states that it is not impossible for sound to subsist in each part, and hearing is conditioned by first arrival, so each hearer hears only once from the first part reaching them.
The phrase 'لا انفعال عن المماثل' indicates that similar parts do not cause a new perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Common Sense: Definition and Proofs
Defines the common sense and provides three proofs for its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the common sense is and the three rational proofs that establish its existence as an inner faculty distinct from the external senses.

Core Idea
The common sense is the inner tablet where all sensory images converge.

This passage introduces one of the inner senses: the common sense. After defining it, the author presents three proofs demonstrating its necessity, drawing on our ability to compare different sensory data, the reality of dreams, and visual persistences. The discussion also addresses an objection about the location of senses like taste and touch.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and Greek name
Notice that the common sense is defined as a faculty where images from external senses gather. Its Greek name 'phantasia' means 'tablet of the soul'. This metaphor emphasizes its receptive role.
The definition sets the foundation for the proofs.
2
Understand the objection and response
An objection claims that touch and taste are not located in the brain, which might challenge the common sense's status. The author agrees but explains that these senses are primary instruments, whereas the common sense is a secondary integrating faculty.
This clarifies that the common sense is not a replacement for external senses but an internal unifier.
3
Examine the first proof – cross-modal judgment
We can judge across senses (e.g., 'this yellow is hot') which requires a single faculty where different sensations coexist. This cannot be any external sense, so an internal faculty is necessary.
Think of a scenario where you associate a color with a taste.
4
Examine the second proof – dreams and hallucinations
In sleep or illness, we see images with no external source. Since the external senses are inactive, an internal faculty must be producing these images.
This proof shows the common sense can generate images independently.
5
Examine the third proof – persistence of vision
A falling drop appears as a continuous line because the common sense retains the image briefly, connecting successive visual imprints. The eye alone cannot do this.
This proof relies on the phenomenon of afterimages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما الحواس الباطنة) فمنها الحس المشترك، وهي القوة التي يجتمع فيها صور المحسوسات بتأديها إليها من طرق الحواس، يدل عليها الحكم ببعض المحسوسات على البعض ومشاهدة النائم والمريض ما ليس في الخارج، ومشاهدة الكل القطرة النازلة خطاً، والشعلة الجوالة دائرة، ومبناه على أن صور المحسوسات لا ترتسم في النفس، وإن كانت هي الحاكمة والمدركة، وعلى ضرورة أنه لا يرتسم في البصر إلا المقابل، أو ما هو في حكمه . فإن قيل : كون اللمس أو الذوق ليس بالدماغ قطعي . قلنا : نعم . بمعنى أنه ليس الآلية الأولية المختصة . أيضاً على حسب ما وجدناه . خمس . وإن احتمل إمكان غيرها، وما يقال هي [?] . أما الحس المشترك ويسمى باليونانية «بنطاسيا» أي لوح النفس فهي القوة التي تجتمع فيها صور المحسوسات الظاهرة بالتأدي إليها من طرق الحواس ويدل على وجودها وجوه : الأول : أنا نحكم ببعض المحسوسات الظاهرة على البعض، كما نحكم بأن هذا الأصفر هو هذا الحار، أو هذا الحلو هو هذا المشموم، وكل من الحواس الظاهرة لا يحضر عندها إلا نوع مدركاته، فلا بد من قوة يحضر عندها جميع الأنواع ، ليصح الحكم بينها. الثاني : إن النائم أو المريض كالمبرسم ، يشاهد صوراً جزئية لا تحقق لها في الخارج، ولا في شيء من الحواس الظاهرة، فلا بد من قوة بها المشاهدة. الثالث : إنا نشاهد القطرة النازلة بسرعة خطاً مستقيماً، والشعلة الجوالة بسرعة خطاً مستديراً ، وما ذاك إلا لأن لنا قوة غير البصر ، يرتسم فيها صورة القطرة والشعلة، ويبقى قليلاً على وجه يتصل به الارتسامات البصرية المتتالية بعضها ببعض، بحيث يشاهد خطاً، للقطع بأنه لا ارتسام في البصر عند زوال المقابلة، ومنع ذلك على ما ذكره الإمام مكابرة، وإلى هذا أشار في المتن ما ذكر من صرورة [?].
He said: (As for the inner senses) among them is the common sense, which is the faculty in which images of perceived objects are gathered by being conveyed to it from the pathways of the senses. It is indicated by the judgment of some perceived objects upon others, by the sleeper and the sick person seeing what is not external, and by everyone seeing a falling drop as a line and a moving flame as a circle. Its basis is that images of perceived objects are not imprinted in the soul, even though it is the judging and perceiving agent, and on the necessity that only what is opposite or in its ruling is imprinted in the eye. If it is said: that touch or taste is certainly not in the brain, we say: yes, meaning that it is not the primary specific instrument, also according to what we found, five [?], even if the possibility of others is conceivable, and what is said is [?]. As for the common sense—also called in Greek 'phantasia', i.e., the tablet of the soul—it is the faculty in which images of external perceived objects are gathered by being conveyed to it from the pathways of the senses. Its existence is indicated by several proofs: First: we judge some external perceived objects upon others, as we judge that this yellow is this hot, or this sweet is this fragrant, and each of the external senses has present only its own kind of perceptions, so there must be a faculty that has all kinds present in order for judgment between them to be valid. Second: the sleeper or the sick person, like one with fever, sees particular images that have no reality externally nor in any of the external senses, so there must be a faculty by which the seeing occurs. Third: we see a quickly falling drop as a straight line and a quickly moving flame as a circular line, and that is only because we have a faculty other than the eye in which the image of the drop and the flame is imprinted and remains briefly such that successive visual imprints connect with one another so that a line is seen, because it is certain that there is no imprint in the eye when the opposite has vanished, and to deny that, as the Imam mentioned, is obstinacy; and to this is referred what the text mentioned from ?.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage defines the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) and presents three proofs for its existence. It begins with an introductory statement that among the inner senses is the common sense, described as a faculty where images of perceived objects are collected from the pathways of the senses. Evidence for it includes judging one perceived object against another, dreams and hallucinations, and seeing a falling drop as a line. The basis is that images are not imprinted in the soul directly, and only opposites are imprinted in the eye. An objection is raised: it is certain that touch and taste are not located in the brain. The author answers affirmatively but clarifies that they are not the primary specific instruments; rather, the common sense is distinct. The definition is then repeated with the Greek term 'phantasia' (tablet of the soul). Three proofs follow: (1) We judge across sensory modalities (e.g., identifying the yellow as hot), which requires a single faculty where all sensory types are present. (2) Dreams and hallucination produce images without external reality, indicating an internal faculty. (3) Persistence of vision: a falling drop is seen as a line because the common sense retains successive imprints, unlike the eye which only captures the instantaneous opposite. Denying this, as some have, is mere obstinacy. The passage ends noting that the text referred to this point.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The common sense is the same as the external senses or the rational soul.
Correction: The common sense is an internal faculty distinct from both: it integrates data from external senses but is not itself a sensory organ, and it is not the rational soul which judges.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Common Sense: Definition and Proofs
Defines common sense and gives three proofs for its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and the three rational proofs for its existence as presented in the source.

Core Idea
Common sense is the inner tablet where all sensory images meet.

This passage introduces the concept of common sense, an inner faculty that gathers sensory data from the external senses. The author then provides three distinct arguments to prove that such a faculty exists. These proofs rely on everyday experiences like cross-modal perception, dreams, and the perception of motion. Understanding these proofs helps us see why Islamic philosophers posited an internal sense beyond the five external ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic definition of common sense. Note the Greek borrowing 'بَنْطَاسِيَا' and the phrase 'لوح النفس' (tablet of the soul). Understand that this faculty collects images from all external senses.
2
Identify the first proof
Look for the proof based on cross-modal judgments. We judge that a hot thing is also sweet or smelled, which requires a single faculty where all sense types converge. Each external sense only grasps its own type, so a common sense is needed.
This proof is about the unity of perceptual experience.
3
Explore the second proof
Consider the proof from dreams and illness. A sleeper or patient observes images without external stimuli. Since no external sense is active, there must be an inner faculty generating these perceptions.
This shows perception can occur without current external input.
4
Analyze the third proof
Study the proof from motion perception. A falling droplet appears as a continuous line because successive visual imprints are retained and connected. This requires a faculty other than sight that retains images briefly.
Sight alone cannot retain the imprint after the object disappears.
5
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author ends by stating that denying this proof is obstinacy, citing al-Rāzī. Consider why this proof might be considered especially strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحس المشترك ويسمى باليونانية «بنطاسيا» أي لوح النفس فهي القوة التي تجتمع فيها صور المحسوسات الظاهرة بالتأدي إليها من طرق الحواس ويدل على وجودها وجوه : الأول : أنا نحكم ببعض المحسوسات الظاهرة على البعض، كما نحكم بأن هذا الحار، أو هذا الحلو هو هذا المشموم، وكل من الحواس الظاهرة لا يحضر عندها إلا نوع مدركاته، فلا بد من قوة يحضر عندها جميع الانواع ، ليصح الحكم بينها. الثاني : إن النائم أو المريض كالمبرسم ، يشاهد صوراً جزئية لا تحقق لها في الخارج، ولا في شيء من الحواس الظاهرة، فلا بد من قوة بها المشاهدة. الثالث : إنا نشاهد القطرة النازلة بسرعة خطاً مستقيماً، والشعلة الجوالة بسرعة خطاً مستديراً ، وما ذاك إلا لأن لنا قوة غير البصر ، يرتسم فيها صورة القطرة والشعلة، ويبقى قليلاً على وجه يتصل به الارتسامات البصرية المتتالية بعضها ببعض، بحيث يشاهد خطاً، للقطع بأنه لا ارتسام في البصر عند زوال المقابلة، ومنع ذلك على ما ذكره الإمام مكابرة، وإلى هذا أشار في المتن ما ذكر من صرورة أنه لا يرتسم في البصر إلا المقابل، أو ما هو في حكمه.
As for the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), called in Greek 'phantasia' (baṇṭāsiyā), meaning the tablet of the soul, it is the faculty in which the images of external sensed objects are gathered through being transmitted to it via the external senses. There are proofs for its existence: First, we judge some external sensed objects in relation to others, just as we judge that this hot (thing) or this sweet (thing) is this smelled (thing). Each external sense only apprehends its own type of percepts, so there must be a faculty in which all types are present in order for judgment between them to be correct. Second, the sleeper or the patient, such as one with pleurisy, observes particular images that have no reality in the external world nor in any of the external senses, so there must be a faculty by which the observation occurs. Third, we observe a rapidly falling droplet as a straight line, and a rapidly moving flame as a circular line. This is only because we have a faculty other than sight in which the image of the droplet and flame is imprinted and remains for a short time such that successive visual imprints connect with each other, so that a line is perceived. This is because it is certain that no imprint occurs in sight when the facing object disappears. Denying this, as the Imam (al-Rāzī) mentioned, is obstinacy. And the text (al-matn) referred to this, mentioning that no imprint occurs in sight except from that which is facing or its equivalent.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage defines the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and provides three proofs for its existence. The definition states that common sense is a faculty where images from external senses gather, like a tablet of the soul. The first proof: we make cross-modal judgments (e.g., 'this hot thing is this sweet thing'), which requires a single faculty where all sense data converge. The second proof: dreams and illnesses produce images without external stimuli, implying an internal sensory faculty. The third proof: motion perception (e.g., a falling droplet appearing as a line) requires a faculty that retains successive images; sight alone cannot do this because it only imprints facing objects. The author notes that denying this, as al-Rāzī did, is obstinacy, and that the matn (probably Taftāzānī's text) refers to this point.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common sense is the same as imagination or is located in the brain.
Correction: Common sense is a distinct faculty that collects sensory images; it is not the power of imagination (which manipulates images) and its location is not specified here.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Common sense is the inner faculty that unifies sensory data from different external senses, and its existence is proven by cross-modal judgments, dreams, and motion perception.

?

?
As you read these words, your eyes move across the screen, yet you perceive a continuous flow of meaning. Are you aware of the inner 'tablet' that holds each word in sequence until the sentence is complete? How does this remind you that your soul is constantly recording, retaining, and connecting the moments of your life? What accountability does that bring?

Objection and Response on Particular Images in the Soul
Objection and response on the soul's perception of particulars versus common sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the soul's perception of particulars might negate the need for a common sense, the response that particulars are imprinted in an instrument, and the subsequent consideration that questions whether a common instrument is required.

Core Idea
The soul judges particulars, but they reside in an instrument, not the soul.

This passage presents a nuanced objection: if the soul already perceives particulars, why is a common sense needed? The response defends the common sense but concedes that while the soul perceives both universals and particulars, particular images are imprinted in an instrument. A further consideration, however, suggests that the soul might judge particulars based on their imprint in the eye, challenging the necessity of a single common instrument. This reveals the depth of debate on internal senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection argues that since the soul perceives universals and also judges particulars, particulars could be imprinted in the soul, making common sense unnecessary. Identify the key examples: yellowness, heat, Zayd as a human.
The objection uses the fact that we judge the universal on the particular (e.g., 'this yellowness is a color').
2
Understand the response's structure
The response (taqrīr al-jawāb) admits that the soul perceives both, but asserts that particular images are not imprinted in the soul itself, but in its instrument (āla). Therefore, a common instrument is needed for cross-modal judgments.
Note the key term 'āla' (instrument) – it is a corporeal faculty like the common sense.
3
Examine the further consideration
The text then adds a counterpoint (fīhi naẓar): the soul could judge particulars based on their imprint in the eye, so a separate common instrument might not be needed. However, the final conclusion is that each external sense has an internal sense, implying that external senses alone are insufficient.
This shows the debate is not settled; the author presents a critical analysis of the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : ومبناه أن المحسوسات لا ترتسم في النفس فإشارة الى جواب اعتراض آخر، أنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الارتسام في الباصرة، كونه في قوة أخرى جسمانية، لجواز أن يكون في النفس، وكذا الصور التي يشاهدها المريض والنائم ، وصور المحسوسات المحكوم فيها بالبعض على البعض كهذه الصفرة والحرارة ،وغيرها، ألا ترى أنا نحكم بالكلي على الجزئي، كحكمنا بأن هذه الصفرة ،لون، وزيد ،إنسان مع القطع بأن مدرك الكلي هو النفس، فإذا كان الحكم بين الشيئين مستلزماً لحضورهما عند الحاكم ، كان الجزئي حاضراً عند النفس مرتسماً فيها كالكلي، فلا يثبت الحس المشترك، وتقرير الجواب : أنا معترفون بأن مدرك الكليات والجزئيات جميعاً، والحاكم بينهما هو النفس، لكن الصور الجزئية لا ترتسم فيها لما سيجيء، بل في آلتها، فلا بد في الحكم بين محسوسين من آلة مشتركة. وفيه نظر. لجواز أن يكون حضورهما عند النفس، وحكمها بينها لارتسامهما في العين، كما أن الحكم بين الكلي والجزئي، يكون لارتسام الكلي في النفس، والجزئي في الآلة، فلا تثبت آلة مشتركة غاية الأمر أنه لا يكفي الحواس الظاهرة ليصح الحكم حالتي الغيبة والحضور، بل يكون لكل حسن ظاهر، حسن باطن.
As for his statement: 'Its basis is that sensed objects are not imprinted in the soul' — this points to the answer to another objection: that it does not follow from the absence of imprint in the sight that it must be in another corporeal faculty, because it is possible that it is in the soul. Similarly, the images seen by the sick and the sleeper, and the images of sensed objects judged in relation to each other, like this yellowness and heat, etc. Do you not see that we judge the universal in relation to the particular, as when we judge that this yellowness is a color, and Zayd is a human, while knowing with certainty that the perceiver of universals is the soul? If judgment between two things necessitates their presence before the judge, then the particular would be present in the soul imprinted in it like the universal, thus the common sense would not be established. The summary of the answer: We admit that the perceiver of both universals and particulars, and the judge between them, is the soul, but particular images are not imprinted in it for a reason to come, but rather in its instrument. Therefore, in judgment between two sensed objects, a common instrument is necessary. But there is consideration [objection to this]: It is possible that their presence before the soul and its judgment between them is due to their imprint in the eye, just as the judgment between the universal and particular occurs due to the imprint of the universal in the soul and the particular in the instrument. Thus a common instrument is not established; the only conclusion is that the external senses are not sufficient for correct judgment in states of both absence and presence, but rather each external sense has an internal sense.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response concerning the common sense. The objection argues that since the soul perceives universals and also judges particulars, particulars could be imprinted in the soul, negating the need for a common sense. The response (taqrīr al-jawāb) concedes that the soul perceives both, but particulars are not imprinted in the soul but in its instrument (āla). Therefore, a common instrument is needed for cross-modal judgments. However, the text then notes a further consideration (fīhi naẓar): the soul could judge particulars based on their imprint in the eye, not requiring a separate common instrument. The final conclusion is that external senses alone are insufficient; each external sense has an internal sense (i.e., a corresponding internal faculty), but not necessarily a single common sense. This highlights a nuanced debate on the internal senses.

Correction: The response shows that particulars are imprinted in an instrument, so a common instrument is needed for judgment between sensed objects.

Key Takeaway
The soul judges particulars, but they are not imprinted in it; they are in an instrument, though the necessity of a single common instrument is debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that your soul judges between sensory particulars through an instrument. When you make a judgment based on your senses—like deciding if a sound is coming from left or right—do you ever pause to consider whether your 'internal instrument' is reliable? What would it mean for your life if you discovered it was damaged or clouded?

When you face a deep question about your own mind or soul, do you sit patiently with the uncertainty and continue seeking, or do you rush to settle on an answer? What is at stake for your soul if you choose comfort over truth?
?
The philosopher distinguishes between the soul and its instrument. In your daily life, do you ever confuse your physical sensations (like hunger or anger) with your soul's deeper judgment? How does that confusion affect your choices? Are you willing to examine where your true 'self' resides?

Imagination: Definition and First Proof
Definition of imagination and presentation of the first proof (acceptance-preservation distinction).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of imagination (khayal) as the faculty that preserves sensory forms, and the first argument proving it is distinct from common sense based on the principle that one cause cannot produce two different effects.

Core Idea
Imagination is the memory of forms; its proof rests on the difference between receiving and preserving.

Taftazani opens this section by defining the faculty of imagination, which stores sensory information after the senses have stopped perceiving. He then presents the first of two proofs for its existence as a separate faculty, distinct from common sense. The proof relies on a fundamental axiom in Islamic theology: a single agent cannot be the source of two distinct acts. Let's read the source text and trace the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of imagination
Read Taftazani's definition in Arabic and English. Note that imagination preserves forms after they have left the common sense. Understand that 'common sense' is the faculty that receives sensory input.
The term 'khayal' here is technical, not ordinary fantasy.
2
Identify the two acts: acceptance and preservation
The proof begins by noting that sensory forms undergo two acts: acceptance (qabul) and preservation (hifz). These are different. The author argues they must therefore have different sources.
Acceptance is the receiving of the form; preservation is retaining it.
3
Understand the principle of 'one source, one effect'
The proof invokes the rule that a single cause cannot produce two distinct effects. This principle is used to show that common sense cannot be both acceptor and preserver, so a separate faculty (imagination) must exist.
This principle is central to kalam arguments about divine attributes as well.
4
Reflect on the purpose of preservation
Taftazani explains why preservation is necessary: without it, we could not recognize objects on repeated sight, making it impossible to distinguish beneficial from harmful things.
This shows the teleological reasoning common in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (منها الخيال) وهي التي تحفظ صور المحسوسات بعد غيبتها عن الحس المشترك. استدل على ثبوتها ومغايرتها للحس المشترك بوجهين: الأول: أن لصور المحسوسات قبولاً عندنا وحفظاً، وهما فعلان مختلفان، فلا بد لهما من مبدأين متغايرين، لما تقرر من أن الواحد لا يكون مصدراً لأثرين، ومبدأ القبول هو الحس المشترك، فمبدأ الحفظ هو الخيال، وإنما احتيج إلى الحفظ لئلا يختل نظام العالم، فإنا إذا أبصرنا الشيء ثانياً، فلو لم يعرف أنه هو المبصر أولا لما حصل التمييز بين النافع والضار. واعترض بأن الحفظ مسبوق بالقبول ومشروط به ضرورة. فقد اجتمعا في قوة واحدة سميتموها الخيال، وبأن الحس المشترك مبدأ لإدراكات مختلفة هي أنواع الإحساسات، وبأن النفس تقبل الصور العقلية وتتصرف في البدن، فبطل قولكم الواحد لا يكون مبدأ لأثرين. وأجيب: بأن الخيال لا بد أن يكون في محل جسماني، فيجوز أن يكون قبوله لأجل المادة، وحفظه لقوة الخيال كالأرض تقبل الشكل بمادتها، وتحفظه بصورتها وكيفيتها، أعني اليبوسة، وبأن مبدائية الحس المشترك للإدراكات المختلفة، إنما هي لاختلاف الجهات، أعني طرق التأدية من الحواس الظاهرة، وكذا إدراكات النفس وتصرفاتها من جهة قواها المختلفة، ولا يخفى أن هذا الجواب يدفع أصل الاستدلال لجواز أن لا تكون إلا قوة واحدة، لها القبول والحفظ بحسب اختلاف الجهات، وكذا الجواب بأن القبول والإدراك من قبيل الانفعال دون الفعل. فاجتماع القبول والحفظ وأنواع الإدراكات في شيء واحد، لا يقدح في قولنا الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد.
He said: 'Among them is imagination, which preserves the forms of perceived objects after their absence from the common sense.' He argued for its existence and its distinction from common sense in two ways: First: That perceived objects have acceptance and preservation with us, and they are two different acts; therefore they must have two different principles, because it is established that the one cannot be the source of two effects. The principle of acceptance is common sense, so the principle of preservation is imagination. Preservation is needed lest the order of the world be disrupted; for if we see something a second time and do not know that it is the same as what was seen first, discrimination between beneficial and harmful would not occur. It was objected that preservation is preceded by acceptance and conditioned by it necessarily, so they have both come together in a single faculty which you call imagination; and that common sense is the principle of different perceptions which are the types of sensations; and that the soul accepts intellectual forms and acts in the body; so your statement that the one cannot be the source of two effects is invalidated. The answer: Imagination must be in a bodily substrate; so it is possible that its acceptance is due to matter and its preservation is due to the power of imagination, like the earth accepts shape by its matter and preserves it by its form and quality, namely dryness; and that the principality of common sense for different perceptions is only due to difference of directions, namely the channels of transmission from the external senses; likewise the perceptions of the soul and its actions come from its various powers. It is not hidden that this answer repels the original argument because it allows that there could be only one faculty that has acceptance and preservation according to different directions. Likewise the answer that acceptance and perception are of the type of passion rather than action; so the combination of acceptance, preservation, and types of perceptions in one thing does not harm our statement that the one does not cause only one.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage defines the faculty of imagination (al-khayal) as that which preserves the forms of perceived objects after they are no longer present to the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). It then provides the first proof for its existence as distinct from common sense. The proof relies on the principle that a single agent cannot be the source of two different effects. Since acceptance of sensory forms and their preservation are two different acts, they must have two different principles: common sense for acceptance and imagination for preservation. The need for preservation is argued by the necessity of recognizing objects upon repeated perception to distinguish benefit from harm. An objection is raised: preservation depends on acceptance, so both could inhere in one faculty. Additionally, common sense itself accounts for different perceptions, and the soul both receives intellectual forms and acts in the body, refuting the claim that one cannot produce two effects. The answer distinguishes between the material substrate and the power: imagination is in a bodily substrate, so acceptance is due to matter and preservation due to its specific power, analogous to earth accepting shape by matter and preserving it by dryness. The multiplicity of common sense's perceptions is due to different channels from the external senses, not a single act. The answer also notes that acceptance and perception are passions, not actions, so their combination in one thing does not violate the principle of one effect per cause.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think imagination is just idle fantasy and has no real philosophical or theological basis.
Correction: In kalam, imagination (khayal) is a technical term for the faculty that retains sensory forms, essential for cognition and recognition. The first proof establishes its necessity via rational argument.

Checkpoint
What is the function of imagination according to the passage?
A) To create new images from memory
B) To preserve forms of perceived objects after they are absent from common sense
C) To combine sensory forms into composite images
D) To transmit sensory data to the intellect

Key Takeaway
Imagination is the faculty of preservation distinct from common sense, proven by the principle that one cause cannot produce two different effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a memory that preserves countless experiences. How many of those preserved memories remind you of Allah's favours? Are you actively using that preservation to increase your gratitude and obedience, or do you let them fade?
?
The lesson says without preservation, you could not distinguish beneficial from harmful. In your daily choices, do you draw on preserved knowledge of halal and haram, or do you rely on impulse? What will you do today to revive that knowledge?
?
You are reading this lesson right now—will you preserve its teaching? If not, what is the point? Write down one specific change you must make in your heart or life based on this truth, and commit to it now.

Objection and Answer to the First Proof
Objection to the first proof and the scholars' response, with analogies and distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the first proof for imagination's distinctness and the responses given by Taftazani, including the distinction between matter and power, and the classification of acceptance as passion.

Core Idea
Objection: Could one faculty do both? Answer: Matter handles acceptance, power handles preservation.

The first proof for imagination's distinctness seems clear, but it faces a powerful objection: if preservation depends on acceptance, why can't one faculty do both? Additionally, common sense itself perceives multiple types of sensations, and the soul both receives forms and acts on the body — all seeming to violate the 'one cause, one effect' rule. Taftazani provides answers that refine the principle and introduce a key distinction between material substrate and faculty power. Let's examine this critical part of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection raises three points: (1) preservation is conditioned by acceptance, so they can be in one faculty; (2) common sense perceives multiple types of sensations; (3) the soul both receives intellectual forms and acts in the body. These seem to refute the principle of one cause, one effect.
The objection uses the same principle to challenge the proof.
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
Taftazani answers that imagination is in a bodily substrate; acceptance is due to matter, preservation due to the power of imagination. He gives the analogy of earth: matter receives shape, dryness preserves it.
This distinguishes two aspects: material and formal cause.
3
Understand the second part of the answer
The multiplicity of perceptions from common sense is due to different channels from external senses, not a single act. Similarly, the soul's various actions come from different powers. So the principle remains valid.
This shows that the principle applies to acts from the same aspect, not different aspects.
4
Reflect on the final refinement
Taftazani notes that acceptance and perception are passions (infi'al), not actions. Combining passions in one thing does not violate the principle because they are not distinct actions of the agent.
This is a subtle but crucial point about the nature of these faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
