The Retentive Faculty (al-Hafiza) and Its Relation to al-Wahm
Sanusi defines al-hafiza and its relation to al-wahm via analogy with al-khayal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-hafiza as the power that retains the judgments of al-wahm and how it differs from imagination.

Core Idea
Al-hafiza retains meanings, while imagination retains forms; both serve different mental faculties.

Sanusi continues his enumeration of the internal senses by introducing the retentive faculty, known as al-hafiza. This faculty preserves the judgments made by the estimative faculty (al-wahm). In this passage, he clarifies its function by drawing a crucial analogy and by distinguishing it from the imaginative faculty. Understanding this distinction helps clarify how the mind processes and stores abstract meanings versus sensory forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a sense of the definitions and analogies used.
2
Identify the key analogy
Notice how Sanusi compares al-hafiza to al-wahm and al-khayal to the common sense. Why does he use this parallel?
The analogy shows that each faculty has a retaining counterpart.
3
Understand the distinction between retention of meanings vs. forms
The passage emphasizes that the power of acceptance is different from retention, and that al-hafiza retains meanings, not forms. Think about how this differs from imagination.
4
Consider the alternative names
Al-hafiza is also called al-dhakira (memory) and mutadhakkira. Reflect on why these names are used based on the function described.
The names reflect different aspects: retrieval and recollection after heedlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها الحافظة) لأحكام الوهم وتسمى الذاكرة باعتبار استرجاعها . هي للوهم كالخيال للحس المشترك، ووجه تغايرها أن قوة القبول غير قوة الحفظ ، والحافظة للمعاني غير الحافظ للصور، ويسميها قوم ذاكرة، إذ بها الذكر أعني ملاحظة المحفوظ بعد الذهول عنه (٢) ، ومتذكرة إذ بها التذكر. أعني الاحتيال لاستعراض الصور بعد ما اندرست (٣) . قال ابن ميادة . هلا اشتريت حنطة بالرستاق سمراء مما درس ابن مخراق ويقال : سمي إدريس عليه السلام لكثرة دراسته كتاب الله تعالى واسمه اخنوخ . والدرس : جرب قليل يبقى في البعير قال العجاج : من عرق النضج عصيم الدرس والدرس : الطريق الخفي . والدرس بالكسر : الدريس، وهو الثوب الخلق، والجمع درسان . وقد درس الثوب درساً أي أخلق .
He said: (And among them is the retentive faculty (al-hafiza), for the judgments of the estimative faculty, and it is called the memory (al-dhakira) with respect to retrieval.) It is to the estimative faculty as the imaginative faculty (al-khayal) is to the common sense. The aspect of their difference is that the power of reception is not the power of retention, and the retentive of meanings is not the retentive of forms. Some people call it memory (dhakira), because by it is remembrance (al-dhikr), i.e., noting the preserved after heedlessness of it; and mutadhakkira, because by it is recollection (al-tadhakkur), i.e., the endeavor to review forms after they have become effaced. Ibn Mayyada said: 'Would that you buy wheat from al-Rastaq, dark, from what Ibn Mikhraq threshed.' It is said: Idris, peace be upon him, was named for his much study of the Book of Allah; his name was Akhnukh. Al-dars: a small trace remaining on a camel. Al-'Ajaj said: 'From sweat of ripeness, pure the dars.' Al-dars: the hidden path. Al-dirs (with kasra): the worn garment (al-daris), plural dirsan. And darasa al-thawb darsan: it became worn out.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-hafiza' (the retentive faculty) as the power that preserves the judgments of al-wahm. It is also called 'al-dhakira' (memory) when considering retrieval. The author draws a parallel: al-hafiza stands to al-wahm as al-khayal (the imaginative faculty) stands to the common sense. The difference between these pairs is that acceptance (qabul) is distinct from retention (hifz), and the retention of meanings (al-hafiza) is distinct from the retention of forms (al-khayal). Some scholars call it 'dhakira' because it enables 'dhikr' (remembrance), which is recall after heedlessness, and 'mutadhakkira' because it enables 'tadhakkur' (recollection), which is the effort to bring forms back after they have faded. The passage then includes linguistic notes on the root 'd-r-s' (to thresh, to study, to efface) as part of the commentary on the word 'indarast' (have become effaced).

Key Terms
الحافظة
الوهم
الذاكرة
المتذكرة
الخيال
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-hafiza and al-khayal are identical because both preserve mental content.
Correction: The passage clarifies that al-hafiza preserves meanings (from al-wahm) while al-khayal preserves forms (from common sense).

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between al-hafiza and al-khayal according to the passage?
A) Al-hafiza is the same as al-khayal.
B) Al-hafiza retains forms while al-khayal retains meanings.
C) Al-hafiza is to al-wahm as al-khayal is to the common sense.
D) Al-hafiza operates only when al-khayal is inactive.

Key Takeaway
Al-hafiza is the faculty that retains the abstract judgments of the estimative faculty, distinct from imagination which retains sensory forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson distinguishes between retaining the form of knowledge and retaining its meaning. When you read the Quran or listen to a lecture, are you more focused on memorizing the words (forms) or understanding the message (meanings)? What will you change today to prioritize meaning over form in one area of your learning?
?
Al-hafiza is called memory when it retrieves after heedlessness. When you forget a valuable spiritual insight or a lesson from the Quran, do you make an effort to actively recollect it, or do you let it drift away? What specific practice can you start this week to train your faculty of recollection?
?
The passage notes that the power of reception is not the power of retention. How often do you receive knowledge (through hearing or reading) but fail to retain it because you do not actively work on preservation? What one concrete habit can you adopt to move from passive reception to active retention of what you learn about your faith?

The Compositive Faculty and Its Role
Sanusi defines al-mutasarrafa as the combiner/separator of forms and meanings, with variant names and its role in reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the compositive faculty (al-mutasarrafa) as the active power of combining and separating forms and meanings, and its crucial role in syllogistic reasoning by hunting the middle term.

Core Idea
The compositive faculty is the engine of both imagination and logic.

This passage from Sanusi unpacks a key concept in Islamic epistemology: the faculty responsible for creative synthesis and logical inference. Al-mutasarrafa is not merely a passive storehouse but an active agent that combines sense data and meanings, and it is through this faculty that we construct novel ideas and even discover the middle term of a syllogism. Understanding this faculty helps clarify how the mind moves from perception to reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the translation. Focus on the definition of al-mutasarrafa: it is the power that combines and separates forms and meanings.
The definition is given right after 'قال (ومنها المتصرفة)'.
2
Note the two names based on usage
The passage explains that al-mutasarrafa is called mufakkira (cogitative) when used by the intellect, and mutakhayyila (imaginative) when used by the estimative faculty. Consider how this naming reflects its dual role in logic and imagination.
The same faculty has different names depending on which higher faculty employs it.
3
Understand its role in 'hunting the middle term'
The passage states that through this faculty the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is hunted by reviewing the contents of the retentive faculty. This is a key function in syllogistic reasoning. Reflect on how combining and separating leads to discovering the middle term.
The middle term is the link between the major and minor terms in a syllogism.

قال (ومنها المتصرفة) الصور والمعاني بالتركيب والتفصيل وتسمى باعتبار استعمال العقل إياها مفكرة والوهم متخيلة . أي في الصور المأخوذة عن الحس والمعاني المدركة بالوهم بتركيب بعضها مع بعض، وتفصيل بعضها عن بعض كتصور إنسان له رأسان ، أو لا رأس له، وتصور العدو صديقاً، وبالعكس، وهي دائماً لا تسكن نوماً ولا يقظة، وبها يقتنص الحد الأوسط، باستعراض ما في الحافظة ، وهي المحاكية للمدركات والهيئات (١) في (ب) آخر بدلاً من (هذا) (٢) سقط من (أ) لفظ (عنه) . (٣) درس الرسم يدرس دروساً، أي عفا ودرسته الريح يتعدى ولا يتعدى. ودرست المرأة دروساً أي حاضت وأبودراس: فرج المرأة ودرسوا الحنطة دراساً أي داسوها
He said: (And among them is the compositive faculty (al-mutasarrafa), [which operates on] forms and meanings by combination and separation, and it is called, considering the intellect's use of it, the cogitative faculty (mufakkira), and [considering] the estimative faculty [use of it], the imaginative faculty (mutakhayyila).) That is, in the forms taken from sense and the meanings perceived by the estimative faculty, by combining some with others and separating some from others, such as imagining a human with two heads or with no head, and imagining an enemy as a friend, and vice versa. It is constantly active, neither sleeping nor waking, and through it the middle term is hunted by reviewing what is in the retentive faculty. It is the imitator of perceptions and states. (1) In copy (b): 'other' instead of 'this.' (2) Missing from copy (a) the word 'minhu.' (3) Darasa al-rasm yadrusu durusan: i.e., it effaced, and the wind effaced it (transitive and intransitive). And darasat al-mar'atu durusan: she menstruated; abu diras: the vulva of a woman; and darasu al-hinta dirasan: they threshed wheat.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines 'al-mutasarrafa' (the compositive faculty) as the power that combines and separates forms (from the senses) and meanings (from the estimative faculty). It is called 'mufakkira' (cogitative) when used by the intellect, and 'mutakhayyila' (imaginative) when used by the estimative faculty. Examples include conceiving a human with two heads or no head, or imagining an enemy as a friend. This faculty is never at rest, operating in both sleep and wakefulness. It plays a crucial role in reasoning by scanning the contents of the retentive faculty to 'hunt' the middle term of a syllogism (al-hadd al-awsat). It is described as 'al-muhakiya lil-mudrakat wal-hay'at' (the imitator of perceptions and states). The passage ends with textual notes on variant readings and lexical notes on the root 'd-r-s'.

The Soul as Perceiver and Localization of Internal Senses
States the view that the soul perceives and proposes localizations for five internal senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main views on the perceiver (soul vs. bodily power) and the wisdom-based localization of the five internal senses in the brain.

Core Idea
The soul is the true perceiver; the brain's regions are arranged by wisdom for the internal senses.

This passage tackles a fundamental question in classical psychology: who or what perceives? It presents two positions, critiques one, and then proposes a purposeful arrangement of the five internal senses in the brain. The author uses a method of wisdom to assign locations, showing that the internal faculties are not randomly placed but serve specific functions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's style: he presents two views, raises objections, then provides a positive proposal.
2
Identify the two views on the perceiver
The passage contrasts two views: (1) the soul perceives, bodily powers are instruments; (2) a single bodily power perceives, organs are instruments. Notice the objections raised against each.
The first view is attributed to a group of verifiers; the second is deemed impossible.
3
Analyze the localization method
The author then proposes a method of wisdom to localize the five internal senses: common sense, imagination, estimative, memory, compositive imagination. Note the reasoning for each location in the brain.
The order and proximity are based on function: common sense near external senses, imagination as storehouse, etc.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this classification matters for understanding human perception and the soul's role. How does this relate to the earlier debate on the perceiver?
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and consider the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : كون المدرك هي النفس والقوى الجسمانية آلات لها مذهب جمع من المحققين . إلا أنه يشكل بوجود الإدراكات للحيوانات العجم، وأما كون المدرك قوة واحدة جسمانية ، وهذه المحال آلات ،لها ، فمما لا سبيل إليه، إذ لا يعقل آلية العضو لقوة جسمانية. لا تكون حالة فيه ولا يخفي صعوبة إثبات بعض المقدمات الموردة في المقامين . أعني إثبات تعدد القوى، وتعيين محالها. وقد يقال في تعيين محالها بطريق الحكمة، والغاية أن الحس المشترك ينبغي أن يكون في مقدم الدماغ ، ليكون قريباً من الحواس الظاهرة، فيكون التأدي إليه سهلاً، والخيال خلفه، لأن خزانة الشيء ينبغي أن تكون كذلك. ثم ينبغي أن يكون الوهم بقرب الخيال ، لتكون الصور الجزئية بحذاء معانيها الجزئية، والحافظة ،بعده لأنها خزانته، والمتخيلة في الوسط لتكون قريبة من الصور والمعاني، فيمكنها الأخذ منهما بسهولة.
We say: That the perceiver is the soul and the bodily powers are its instruments is the doctrine of a group of verifiers. However, it is problematic because of the existence of perceptions in dumb animals. As for the view that the perceiver is a single bodily power and these organs are its instruments, it is impossible, for the instrumentality of a limb for a bodily power that does not inhere in it is inconceivable. Nor is the difficulty of proving some premises presented in the two contexts hidden. Namely: proving the multiplicity of powers, and determining their loci. It may be said in determining their loci by the way of wisdom and purpose: that the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) should be in the front part of the brain, so that it may be near the external senses, thus conveyance to it is easy; and the imagination (al-khayal) behind it, because the repository of a thing should be thus. Then the estimative faculty (al-wahm) should be near the imagination, so that the particular images are opposite their particular meanings; and the memory (al-hafiza) after it because it is its repository; and the compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila) in the middle, so that it is near the images and meanings, thus it can take from both easily.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents a position on the nature of perception and then proposes a method for localizing the internal senses. The author begins by stating that a group of verifiers holds that the perceiver is the soul and the bodily powers are its instruments. He then raises an objection: this view is problematic because animals also have perceptions, implying that they would also need a soul as perceiver. Next, he rejects the alternative view that the perceiver is a single bodily power with organs as its instruments, because it is inconceivable for a limb to serve as an instrument for a power that does not inhere in it. He acknowledges the difficulty of proving both the multiplicity of powers and their specific locations. He then proposes a method for assigning locations based on wisdom and purpose: the common sense should be in the front of the brain to be near the external senses, the imagination behind it as a storehouse, the estimative near the imagination so that particular images align with their particular meanings, the memory after it as its repository, and the compositive imagination in the middle to be close to both images and meanings, allowing it to combine them easily.

Correction: The passage does not reject the soul-as-perceiver view; it raises an objection about animal perception but does not conclude the view is false. It then proceeds to a different approach: localizing the internal senses.

Avicenna's Doubt on Internal Senses
Reports Avicenna's doubt on the identity of internal faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Avicenna's hesitation about whether the estimative and compositive faculties are one power or many.

Core Idea
Avicenna doubted whether the estimative faculty is one power or many.

This passage reports Avicenna's uncertainty about whether the estimative faculty (al-wahmiyya) is the same as the compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila) and other internal senses. He presents a plausible suggestion that they might be a single faculty with multiple roles, but he also hesitates about the relationship between memory and recollection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a feel for Avicenna's hesitant tone.
Notice the phrase 'تردد' (hesitation) appears twice.
2
Identify the faculties mentioned
Make a list: al-wahmiyya, al-mutakhayyila, al-mutadhakkara, al-mufakkira, al-hakima, al-hafiza. Note their meanings from the translation.
3
Understand Avicenna's suggestion
Avicenna suggests these might all be the same faculty acting in different ways. Pay attention to how he describes the judging, compositive, and recollective roles.
This is a plausible interpretation, not a definitive claim.
4
Focus on the second hesitation
Avicenna also hesitates about whether memory (hafiza) and recollective (mutadhakkara) are two powers or one. This shows the complexity of internal sense distinction.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters in Islamic psychology and kalam. The internal senses are crucial for understanding human cognition and its relation to the soul.
This lesson is a building block for later discussions on the soul's faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وتردد ابن سينا في تعدد الوهمية والمتخيلة. يشير إلى ما قال في الشفاء، يشبه أن تكون القوة الوهمية هي نفسها المتذكرة والمتخيلة والمفكرة وهي نفسها الحاكمة، فتكون بذاتها حاكمة، و بحركاتها وأفعالها متخيلة ومتذكرة، فتكون متفكرة، بما تعمل في الصور والمعاني، ومتذكرة بما ينتهي إليه عملها، وله تردد أيضاً، في أن الحافظة مع المتذكرة . أعني المسترجعة لما غاب عن الحفظ من مخزونات الوهم قوتان أم قوة واحدة.
He said: And Avicenna hesitated concerning the multiplicity of the estimative (al-wahmiyya) and the compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila). He referred to what he said in al-Shifā': It is plausible that the estimative faculty is the very same as the recollective (al-mutadhakkara), the compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila), and the cogitative (al-mufakkira), and it is itself the judging faculty. Thus it is in its essence judging, and by its movements and actions it is compositive and recollective; thus it is cogitative by what it works upon in the images and meanings, and recollective by what its action ends at. And he also hesitated concerning the memory (al-hāfiẓa) and the recollective (al-mutadhakkara) — meaning the one that retrieves what has disappeared from the memory from the stores of the estimative — whether they are two powers or one power.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage reports Avicenna's hesitation regarding the identity of the estimative and compositive faculties. The author first notes that Avicenna vacillates on whether the estimative (wahmiyya) and the compositive imagination (mutakhayyila) are multiple faculties or a single one. He then cites Avicenna's own words from al-Shifā', where he suggests that the estimative power might be identical with the recollective, compositive, and cogitative faculties, and also with the judging faculty. According to this suggestion, in its essence it is judging, and through its various movements and actions it functions as compositive and recollective. It becomes cogitative by working on images and meanings, and recollective by the outcome of its operation. Avicenna also hesitates about the relationship between the memory (hāfiẓa) and the recollective (mutadhakkara) — the power that retrieves what has been stored in the estimative — questioning whether they are two powers or one. This shows the difficulty in precisely distinguishing the internal senses.

Physicians' Simplified Classification of Internal Senses
Explains why physicians simplified internal senses to three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why physicians simplified the internal senses to three and how they mapped them to the brain ventricles.

Core Idea
Physicians classified internal senses practically, not theoretically.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explains that physicians adopted a simplified classification of internal senses based on medical need rather than philosophical accuracy. They grouped the senses into three, corresponding to the three ventricles of the brain. Let's read the passage to see how they justified this simplification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author justifies the simplification.
2
Understand the reason for simplification
Identify why physicians limited themselves to three senses. The text says they focused on preserving health and correcting dysfunctions, not on theoretical distinctions.
The afflictions of different faculties may be similar, allowing grouping.
3
Note the mapping to brain ventricles
Observe how each of the three senses is assigned to a specific ventricle: front for imagination, middle for cogitative, posterior for memory.
Physicians gave each sense a distinct location for practical treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (واقتصر الأطباء) : على الخيال والمفكرة والذاكرة] 

لما كان نظرهم مقصوراً على حفظ صحة القوى، وإصلاح اختلالها، ولم يحتاجوا إلى معرفة الفرق بين القوى، وتحقيق أنواعها، بل إلى معرفة أفعالها ومواضعها، وكانت الآفات العارضة لها، قد تتجانس اقتصروا على قوة في البطن المقدم من الدماغ سموها الحس المشترك والخيال وأخرى في البطن الأوسط سموها المفكرة وهي الوهم ، وأخرى في البطن المؤخر سموها الحافظة والمتذكرة .
He said: 'And the physicians confined [themselves] to the imagination, the cogitative, and the memory.' Since their view was restricted to preserving the health of the powers and correcting their dysfunctions, and they did not need to know the difference between the powers and the verification of their types, but rather to know their actions and positions, and because the afflictions that befall them may be similar, they confined themselves to a power in the front ventricle of the brain which they called the common sense and imagination; another in the middle ventricle which they called the cogitative, and it is the estimative; and another in the posterior ventricle which they called the memory and the recollective.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage explains the pragmatic classification of internal senses by physicians. The author first quotes the statement that physicians limited themselves to three faculties: imagination, cogitative, and memory. He then provides the reason: physicians focus on preserving the health of the powers and correcting their disorders. They do not need to know the precise differences between the powers or verify their types; they only need to know the actions and locations of the faculties and the diseases that affect them. Since the afflictions of different faculties may be similar, physicians simplify by grouping them into three: a power in the front ventricle of the brain, which they call the common sense and imagination; a power in the middle ventricle, called the cogitative (which is actually the estimative); and a power in the posterior ventricle, called the memory and recollective. This classification is practical, not theoretical.

Key Terms
الخيال
المفكرة
الذاكرة
الحس المشترك
الوهم
الحافظة
المتذكرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The physicians' classification is meant to be theoretically exhaustive and accurate.
Correction: The text explicitly states it is a practical simplification for medical treatment, not a theoretical account.

Checkpoint

C) Because they followed Aristotle's exact classification
D) Because they believed the soul has only three powers

Key Takeaway
Physicians simplified internal senses to three (imagination, cogitative, memory) for practical medical reasons, mapping them to the three brain ventricles.

Motor Faculties: Appetitive and Executive Powers
Classifies motor faculties into appetitive and executive powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of motor faculties in Islamic psychology: appetitive (impelling) and executive (physical movement), and their subdivisions.

Core Idea
Motor powers have two types: appetitive (desire/anger) and executive (muscle movement).

In this passage, the author classifies the motor faculties that drive physical actions. You will learn how the appetitive powers (concupiscible and irascible) impel us toward or away from things, while the executive power actually moves the muscles. This distinction is important for understanding human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's classification come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two main categories
Look for the two broad types of motor powers: appetitive (shawqiyya) and executive (fa'ila). Notice that the appetitive is further divided into concupiscible (shahawiyya) and irascible (ghadabiyya).
The executive power deals with expansion and contraction of muscles.
3
Understand the difference between impelling and executing
The appetitive powers impel action (towards benefit or away from harm), while the executive power physically performs the movement by extending or contracting muscles. This distinction clarifies how desire and anger lead to actual bodily motion.
The author notes that motor includes both impelling and executing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما المحركة) فمنها شوقية باعثة على جذب ما يتصوره نافعاً، وتسمى شهوية، أو دفع ما يتصوره ضاراً، وتسمى غضبية، ومنها فاعلة لتمديد الأعصاب إلى جهة مبدأها كما في القبض، أو إلى خلاف جهته كما في البسط .

لم يبسط الكلام في القوى المحركة بسطه في القوى المدركة، لأن المباحث الكلامية لا تتعلق بهذه تعلقها بتلك، والمراد بالمحركة أعم من الفاعلة للحركة، والباعثة عليها وتسمى شوقية ونزوعية، وتنقسم إلى شهوية وهي الباعثة على الحركة، نحو ما يعتقد أو يظن نافعاً وغضبية وهي الباعثة على الحركة نحو ما يعتقد أو يظن ضاراً، وأما الفاعلة فهي قوة من شأنها أن تبسط العضل بإرخاء الأعصاب إلى خلاف جهة مبدأها لينبسط العضو المتحرك، أي يزداد طولاً، وينتقص عرضاً، أو تقبضه بتمديد الأعصاب إلى جهة مبدأها، لينقبض العضو
He said: 'As for the motor [powers] (al-muḥarrika): among them are appetitive (shawqiyya) that impel towards attracting what is imagined as beneficial, called concupiscible (shahawiyya), or repelling what is imagined as harmful, called irascible (ghaḍabiyya). And among them is an executive [power] (fā‘ila) for extending the nerves towards the direction of their origin, as in contraction, or towards the opposite direction, as in expansion.' He did not elaborate on the motor powers as he elaborated on the perceptual powers, because the theological investigations (al-mabāḥith al-kalāmiyya) are not related to these as they are to those. And what is meant by 'motor' (al-muḥarrika) is more general than the executive of movement and the impeller thereof; it is called appetitive and desiderative (nuzū‘iyya). It is divided into concupiscible, which is the impeller towards movement towards what is believed or presumed beneficial, and irascible, which is the impeller towards movement towards what is believed or presumed harmful. As for the executive, it is a power whose function is to extend the muscle by relaxing the nerves towards the opposite direction of their origin, so that the moving limb expands, i.e., increases in length and decreases in width, or to contract it by stretching the nerves towards the direction of their origin, so that the limb contracts.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage discusses the motor faculties. The author first quotes the statement that motor powers include appetitive (shawqiyya) powers that impel towards attracting what is imagined beneficial (concupiscible, shahawiyya) or repelling what is imagined harmful (irascible, ghaḍabiyya), and an executive (fā‘ila) power that extends nerves towards their origin for contraction or in the opposite direction for expansion. The author then notes that the original author did not elaborate on motor powers as much as perceptual powers because kalam investigations are less concerned with them. He expands the definition: 'motor' includes both the executive (fā‘ila) and the impelling (bā‘itha) powers, which is called appetitive and desiderative (nuzū‘iyya). The appetitive part is divided into concupiscible (impelling towards beneficial) and irascible (impelling away from harmful). The executive power works by relaxing nerves to extend the muscle opposite to their origin, causing the limb to expand (increase length, decrease width), or by stretching nerves towards their origin to contract the limb.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motor powers are only the appetitive (desire and anger) that cause motion.
Correction: There is also the executive power that physically moves the muscles, independent of the appetitive impulse.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between the appetitive (shawqiyya) and the executive (fā‘ila) motor powers?
A) The appetitive is for perception, the executive for movement
B) The appetitive impels towards action, the executive executes the movement by expanding or contracting muscles
C) The appetitive is voluntary, the executive is involuntary

Key Takeaway
Motor faculties consist of appetitive powers that impel action (toward benefit or away from harm) and an executive power that physically moves the limbs through muscle expansion and contraction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where you acted out of sudden anger or desire. Did you pause to consider whether the appetitive impulse (concupiscible or irascible) was pushing you, or did your executive power immediately obey? How can you create a gap between impulse and action today?
?
The passage distinguishes between the impeller and the executor. Are you aware of times when your desire to do good (e.g., pray, help someone) was strong, but your limbs did not move? What does that teach you about the role of your will (irāda) in discharging the executive power?
?
If your appetitive powers constantly drive you toward comfort and away from discomfort, how does that align with the demands of faith (e.g., patience, sacrifice)? Which impulse will you resist next time it arises, and what will you replace it with?

The Principle of Desire: From Perceptive Powers
Al-Taftazani explains that desire originates from perceptive powers and addresses a potential misconception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that desire (shawq) originates from the perceptive powers (imagination or intellection), not from a separate motive power, and that perceptive powers can be absent in some creatures due to constitution or accident.

Core Idea
Desire is born from perception, not from a separate motive force.

This lesson clarifies a key point in Islamic psychology: the source of desire. Al-Taftazani addresses a common misunderstanding that desire is a separate motive power. Instead, he shows that desire arises from our perceptive faculties—imagination or intellection. He then explains that these powers can be absent in some animals or individuals, reinforcing the classification of the soul's faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's words speak before interpreting.
Pay attention to the phrase 'قد يتوهم' which signals a mistaken view.
2
Identify the misconception and its correction
Notice the imagined separate motive power and how al-Taftazani refutes it by stating that desire's principle is from perceptive powers, because it is based on imagination or intellection.
This is the key doctrinal point.
3
Reflect on the examples of missing powers
Consider the examples: scorpions lack sight, butterflies lack imagination, humans may be born blind or lose senses. How do these examples support the claim that powers are perceptive?
Connects theory to observable creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما مبدأ الشوق) فمن القوى المدركة .
قد يتوهم أن من القوى المحركة قوة أخرى هي مبدأ قريب للشوقية يعيد للفاعلة كالقوة التي ينبعث عنها شوق الالف بالشيء إلى مألوفه، وشوق المحبوس إلى خلاصه وشوق النفس إلى الفعل الجميل، فأشار إلى أن ذلك من قبيل القوى المدركة ، لأن مبدأ الشوق والنزوع تخيل أو تعقل .
[قال(ثم بعض هذه القوى) قد يفقد في بعض أنواع الحيوان أو أشخاصه بحسب الخلقة أو العارض].
يعني المدركة والمحركة، وقد تفقد في بعض أنواع الحيوان كالبصر في العقرب ، والخيال في الفراشة أو أشخاصه بحسب الخلقة كالأكمه ، ومن ولد مفقود بعض الحواس أو الحركات أو بحسب العارض كمن أصابه آفة، أخلت ببعض إدراكاته أو حركاته .
He said: 'And as for the principle of desire, it is from the perceptive powers.' It might be imagined that among the motive powers there is another power that is the proximate principle of desire, returning to the active power, like the power from which arises the longing of the familiar for what is familiar, and the longing of the imprisoned for his release, and the longing of the soul for beautiful action. So he indicated that this is of the category of perceptive powers, because the principle of desire and inclination is imagination or intellection. He said: 'Then some of these powers may be absent in some types of animals or individuals, due to constitution or accident.' He means the perceptive and motive powers, and they may be absent in some types of animals, like sight in the scorpion and imagination in the butterfly, or in individuals due to constitution, like the congenitally blind, and one born lacking some senses or movements, or due to accident, like one afflicted with a defect that impairs some of his perceptions or movements.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage clarifies a point in the discussion of the soul's powers. It begins by stating that the principle of desire (shawq) belongs to the perceptive powers, not to the motive powers. Some might mistakenly think there is a separate motive power that is the proximate source of desire, like the power that causes longing for the familiar or the prisoner's longing for release. The author refutes this by asserting that desire and inclination originate from imagination (takhayyul) or intellection (ta'aqqul), both of which are perceptive. Then the passage explains that some of these powers (both perceptive and motive) may be absent in certain animals or individual humans, either by natural constitution or by accident. Examples: scorpions lack sight, butterflies lack imagination; humans may be born blind or lose senses due to injury. This reinforces the classification of the soul's faculties.

Correction: Desire arises from the perceptive powers, specifically imagination or intellection, not from a separate motive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What 'imprisonment' in your life (a habit, a pattern, a relationship) do you desire to escape, and have you examined whether your perception of escape is true or imaginary? Make a concrete plan to seek true intellection before acting.

Definition and Divisions of the Soul
Al-Taftazani defines the soul as first perfection and divides it into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the soul as first perfection and its four main divisions across different orders of life.

Core Idea
The soul is the first perfection of a natural organic body, actualizing its potential for life.

Al-Taftazani begins his discussion of incorporeal substances by defining the soul. He clarifies that the term 'soul' can apply to both incorporeal and material principles, and he provides a precise definition that applies across all types. This lesson unpacks his definition and the classification of souls into vegetative, animal, human, and celestial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Read the passage and note that it opens with the second discourse on incorporeal substances, focusing on the soul in the first chapter.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the definition
Look for the phrase 'first perfection of a natural organic body' and see how it applies to all souls.
This definition is the anchor for all divisions.
3
Trace the divisions
Find the four types of soul and how each is defined by its specific functions: vegetative (nutrition/growth), animal (sensation/movement), human (intellection of universals), celestial (willing circular motion).
4
Examine the objection
Notice the objection about motion being called a first perfection and how the author responds, clarifying that motion is a first perfection only with respect to potentiality.
This nuance distinguishes different senses of 'first perfection'.
5
Synthesize
Summarize in your own words what the soul is and why the definition is comprehensive enough to cover all four types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المقالة الثانية فيما يتعلق بالمجردات وفيها فصلان
الفصل الأول في النفس وفيه مباحث :
المبحث الأول : أنها تنقسم إلى فلكية وإنسانية، وقد تطلق على ما ليس بمجرد كالنفس النباتية لمبدأ آثار النبات والحيوانية لمبدأ آثار الحيوان . فمن ها هنا تفسر بأنها كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي. فمن حيث التغذي والنمو نباتية، ومن حيث الحس والحركة حيوانية، ومن حيث تعقل الكليات إنسانية، ومن حيث إرادة الحركة المستديرة فلكية، إذا جعلنا الكواكب والتداوير ونحوهما بمنزلة الآلات ويزاد لتخصيص الأرضية . قيد ذي حياة بالقوة .
أولهما في النفس، والثاني في العقل ، لما عرفت من أن الجوهر المجرد إن تعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف فنفس وإلا فعقل وقد يطلق لفظ النفس على ما ليس بمجرد ، بل مادي كالنفس النباتية التي هي مبدأ أفاعيله من التغذية والتنمية ، والتوليد . والنفس الحيوانية التي هي مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية، وتجعل النفس الأرضية اسماً لهما، أو للنفس الناطقة الإنسانية، فتفسر بأنها كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة، والمراد بالكمال ما يكمل به النوع في ذاته، ويسمى كمالاً أولاً كهيئة السيف للحديد، أو في صفاته ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً، كسائر ما يتبع النوع من العوارض مثل القطع للسيف والحركة للجسم، والعلم للإنسان فإن قيل : قد سبق أن الحركة كمال أول .
قلنا : نعم بالنظر إلى ما هو بالقوة من حيث هو بالقوة، فإنه أول ما يحصل له
He said: The second discourse on what pertains to incorporeal substances, and it contains two chapters. The first chapter is on the soul, and it contains investigations: The first investigation: that it [the soul] is divided into celestial and human, and it may be applied to what is not incorporeal, like the vegetative soul as the principle of vegetative effects, and the animal soul as the principle of animal effects. Hence it is interpreted as the first perfection of a natural organic body. Thus, with respect to nutrition and growth it is vegetative; with respect to sensation and movement it is animal; with respect to intellection of universals it is human; and with respect to willing circular motion it is celestial, if we consider the planets, epicycles, and the like as instruments, and it is added to specify the terrestrial [soul] - the restriction 'potentially having life'. The first of the two [chapters] is on the soul, and the second is on the intellect, because you know that the incorporeal substance, if it is related to the body with the relation of governance and disposal, it is a soul; otherwise, it is an intellect. And the word 'soul' may be applied to what is not incorporeal, but rather material, like the vegetative soul which is the principle of its actions - nutrition, growth, and generation; and the animal soul which is the principle of sensation and voluntary movement. And the terrestrial soul is made a name for these two, or for the rational human soul. So it is interpreted as the first perfection of a natural organic body potentially having life. And by 'perfection' is meant that by which the species is completed in itself, and it is called the first perfection, like the form of the sword for iron; or in its attributes, and it is called the second perfection, like the rest of the accidents that follow the species, such as cutting for the sword, movement for the body, and knowledge for the human. Now if it is said: 'Movement has already been mentioned as a first perfection.' We say: Yes, with respect to what is in potentiality inasmuch as it is in potentiality, for it is the first thing that occurs to it.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the soul (nafs). It begins by stating that the second discourse (maqala) deals with incorporeal substances and contains two chapters: first on the soul, second on the intellect. The first investigation of the soul chapter divides the soul into celestial and human, but notes that 'soul' can also be applied to non-incorporeal principles like the vegetative and animal souls. The soul is defined as 'the first perfection of a natural organic body' (kamaal awwal li-jism tabii'i aali). This definition is then expanded: the soul is called vegetative in terms of nutrition and growth, animal in terms of sensation and movement, human in terms of intellection of universals, and celestial if it wills circular motion (as in celestial spheres). The passage clarifies that 'perfection' (kamaal) means that by which the species is complete in its essence (first perfection, like the form of a sword) or in its attributes (second perfection, like cutting). An objection is raised: motion was earlier said to be a first perfection. The answer: motion is a first perfection only with respect to the potential being actualized; it is the first thing that occurs to that potential. This nuanced response distinguishes between different types of first perfection.

Correction: The passage emphasizes that the soul can be incorporeal, and the definition applies to both material and immaterial souls.

Defending Soul as Perfection Over Form
Objection and answer: why prefer perfection over form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two main reasons why the definition of the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) is preferred over defining it as 'form' (sura).

Core Idea
Form describes matter, but perfection describes the species—and the soul perfects the species.

In this passage, the author defends the classical definition of the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) against an objection that suggests 'form' (sura) might be more appropriate. He argues that form is limited to material inherence, while perfection relates to the species that the soul completes. Let's see how he justifies this preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully, which argues that the soul is also form and power, so why prefer perfection?
2
Identify the first reason
The first reason: form does not apply to the separate human soul except metaphorically.
3
Identify the second reason
The second reason: form relates to matter, while perfection relates to the species. The soul perfects the body, so the definition should refer to the species.
4
Examine the supporting discussion
The author summarizes Avicenna and Imam al-Razi to further support relating the soul to the species, and clarifies a potential misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس للنفوس السماوية اختلاف أفعال وآلات على أنه أيضاً موضع نظر لما ذكر في الشفاء من أن النفس اسم لمبدأ صدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة، عادمة للإرادة ولا خفاء في أنه معنى شامل لهما صالح لتعريفهما به على المذهبين، لأن فعل النفس السماوية أيضاً، ليس على نهج واحد، عادم للإرادة، بل على أنهاج مختلفة على رأي، وعلى نهج واحد مع الإرادة على الصحيح.

فإن قيل : النفس كما أنها كمال للجسم من حيث أنه بها يتم ويتحصل نوعاً كذلك هي صورة له ، من حيث أنها تقارن المادة، فيحصل جوهر نباتي أو حيواني، وقوة له من حيث أنها مبدأ صدور أفعاله، فلم أوثر في تعريفهما الكمال على الصورة والقوة. وما ذكروا من أنا نجد بعض الأجسام يختص بصدور آثار مختلفة عنها، فيقطع بأن ذلك ليس بجسميتة المشتركة، بل لمبادىء خاصة نسميها نفساً، ربما يشعر بأن الأولى ذكر القوة .

قلنا : أما إيثاره على الصورة فلأنها بالحقيقة اسم لما يحل المادة، فلا يتناول النفس الإنسانية المجردة، إلا بتجوز أو تجديد اصطلاح، ولأنها تقاس إلى المادة والكمال إلى النوع . ففي تعريف المعنى الذي به يتحصل الجسم فيصير أحد الأنواع ومصدر الأفعال، يكون المقيس إلى أمر هو نفس ذلك المتحصل أولى من المقيس إلى أمر بعيد لا يكون هو معه إلا بالقوة، ولا ينتسب إليه شيء من الأفاعيل.

هذا ملخص كلام الشفاء. وتقدير الإمام أن المقيس إلى النوع أولى، لأن ما الدلالة على النوع دلالة على المادة، لكونها جزءاً منه من غير عكس، ولأن النوع أقرب إلى الطبيعة الجنسية من المادة. وكان معناه أن النفس تقاس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية المبهمة الناقصة التي إنما تتحصل وتتم نوعاً، لما ينضاف إليها من الفصل بل النفس فتعريفها بالكمال المقيس إلى النوع الذي هو أقرب إلى الجنس من حيث أنهما متحدان في الجود لا يتمايزان إلا في العقل، بأن أخذ هذا مبهماً، وذاك متحصلاً، يكون أولى، هذا وقد يتوهم مما ذكره الإمام، أن النفس كمال بالقياس إلى أن الطبيعة الجنسية كانت ناقصة، وبالضياف الفصل إليها، كمل النوع ، إن الكمال يكون بالقياس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية على ما صرح به المواقف. وحينئذ يكون توسيط النوع وكونه أقرب إلى طبيعة الجنس وهو فاسد على ما لا يخفى. وأما إيثاره على القوة، فلأنها لفظ مشترك مبدأ الفعل كالتحريك ، ومبدأ القبول والانفعال كالإحساس، وكلاهما معتبر في العقل ، وفي الاقتصار على أحدهما، مع أنه إخلال بما هو مدلول النفس، استعمال للمشترك في التعريف، وكذا في اعتبارهما جميعاً ، ولأن الشيء إنما يكون نفساً بكونه مبدأ الاثار، ومكمل النوع ، ولفظ القوة لا يدل إلا على الأول، بخلاف لفظ الكمال ، ولا شك أن تعريف الشيء بما ينبيء عن جميع الجهات المعتبرة فيه، يكون أولى. ففي الجملة لما أمكن تفسير النفس بما يعم السماويات والارضيات ، ثم تمييز كل بما يخصها، وكان ذلك أقرب إلى الضبط آثره في المتن.
And the celestial souls do not have different actions and instruments; although this is also a point of inquiry because of what was mentioned in the Shifa, that 'soul' is a name for the principle of issuing actions that are not uniform, lacking will, and it is not hidden that it is a meaning inclusive of both [earthly and celestial] suitable for defining both according to the two schools, because the action of the celestial soul also is not uniform, lacking will, but rather on different courses according to one opinion, and on a uniform course with will according to the correct [view].

If it is said: The soul, just as it is a perfection (kamal) for the body in that through it the body becomes complete and attains its species, so too it is a form (sura) for it in that it accompanies matter, so a vegetal or animal substance results, and a power (quwwa) for it in that it is the principle of issuing its actions. Why then was preference given in defining both [earthly and celestial] to perfection (kamal) over form and power? And what they mentioned that we find some bodies distinguished by issuing diverse effects from them, so we judge that this is not due to their common corporeality, but rather to special principles which we call 'soul' – perhaps this suggests that power (quwwa) is [more] appropriate.

We say: As for preferring it over form (sura), it is because form in reality is a name for what inheres in matter, so it does not encompass the separate human soul except through metaphor or technical innovation. And because it is related to matter, whereas perfection (kamal) is related to the species. So in defining the meaning by which the body becomes complete and becomes one of the species and source of actions, what is related to a matter that is itself that which becomes complete is more appropriate than what is related to a distant matter that is only with it in potentiality and to which none of the actions are attributed.

This is the summary of the discourse of the Shifa. And the Imam's estimation is that what is related to the species is more appropriate, because indicating the species indicates the matter, since it is part of it, not conversely, and because the species is closer to the generic nature than matter. And its meaning is that the soul is related to the vague, incomplete generic nature which only becomes complete and attains its species by what is added to it from the differentia. But the soul—so defining it by perfection related to the species which is closer to the genus, in that they are united in existence and only differ in the intellect, by taking this as vague and that as determinate, is more appropriate. This is what he mentioned. And one might mistakenly understand from what the Imam mentioned that the soul is perfection in relation to the generic nature being incomplete, and by the attachment of the differentia to it, the species became complete – that perfection is in relation to the generic nature, as stated in al-Mawaqif. In that case, the intermediation of the species and its being closer to the generic nature would be invalid, as is clear. 

As for preferring it over power (quwwa), it is because it is a homonym: it may mean the principle of action like movement, and the principle of reception and passivity like sensation, and both are considered in the intellect. Restricting to one of them, while that is a deficiency in that which is the meaning of the soul, is using a homonym in definition; likewise considering both together. And because a thing is only a soul by being the principle of effects and perfecter of the species, and the word power (quwwa) signifies only the first, unlike the word perfection (kamal). There is no doubt that defining a thing by what indicates all the aspects considered in it is more appropriate. In summary, since it is possible to interpret 'soul' by what encompasses celestial and earthly [souls], then distinguishing each by what is specific to it, and that was closer to precision, he preferred it in the text.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the definition of the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) against two alternative definitions: 'form' (sura) and 'power' (quwwa). The author begins by noting that the definition of the soul as 'the principle of issuing actions that are not uniform and lacking will' also applies to celestial souls, so the definition is general. Then an objector raises the question: why is the soul defined as perfection (kamal) rather than as form (sura) or power (quwwa), given that the soul also has functions that correspond to these? The objector also points out that the argument that some bodies have diverse effects attributable to a special principle called 'soul' might suggest that 'power' is more appropriate.

The author answers in two parts. First, he explains why perfection (kamal) is preferred over form (sura): form literally means what inheres in matter, so it cannot encompass the separate human soul except metaphorically. Moreover, form relates to matter while perfection relates to the species. Since the soul perfects the body and makes it a member of a species, the definition should be in terms of that which achieves this perfection, which is the species, not matter.

Second, he explains why perfection is preferred over power (quwwa): power is ambiguous; it can mean the principle of action (like movement) or the principle of reception (like sensation). Both are essential aspects of the soul, but the word power only captures one of them. The word perfection, on the other hand, indicates all aspects: the soul is both the principle of actions and that which perfects the species. Defining the soul by what indicates all its considered aspects is more appropriate.

The author then summarizes the position of Avicenna's 'al-Shifa' and the interpretation of Imam al-Razi (referred to as 'al-Imam'), who argued that relating the soul to the species is more appropriate because indicating the species implies indicating the matter (since matter is part of the species). He also clarifies a potential misunderstanding regarding whether the soul is perfection relative to the generic nature.

Finally, the author concludes that since it is possible to define the soul in a way that encompasses both celestial and earthly souls, and then distinguish each by its specific attributes, this method is more precise, and that is why this definition was adopted.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs)
كمال (kamal)
صورة (sura)
نوع (naw')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul is perfection relative to the generic nature, so the intermediation of the species is unnecessary.
Correction: The author clarifies that this is false; the soul's perfection is related to the species, not directly to the generic nature.

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) preferred over definition as 'form' (sura)?
A) Because form is ambiguous and has multiple meanings.
B) Because form does not apply to the separate human soul except metaphorically.
C) Because form relates to matter while perfection relates to the species.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The soul is better defined as perfection (kamal) because form is limited to matter and cannot encompass the separate soul, while perfection relates to the species that the soul completes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the soul is defined by its perfection, not by its form. In your own life, do you tend to measure your identity by your outward appearance and material status (form) or by your inner growth in knowledge and virtue (perfection)? What concrete step can you take today to prioritize the latter?
?
The passage warns against mistakenly thinking the soul is perfection relative to a vague generic nature rather than the species. Is there an area in your understanding of yourself where you settle for a vague or incomplete definition (e.g., 'I'm just human') instead of striving to become a specific kind of person Allah wants you to be? What is one specific perfection (kamal) you need to pursue right now?
?
If the separate human soul cannot be fully captured by the concept of form because it transcends matter, how should this affect your attachment to physical things and your pursuit of spiritual realities? Are you willing to let go of a material identification to embrace the perfection of your soul?

Defending Soul as Perfection Over Power
Explanation of why perfection is preferred over power in defining the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) is preferred over defining it as 'power' (quwwa).

Core Idea
Power is ambiguous; perfection captures both action and perfection of species.

Having addressed the alternative definition of form, the author now turns to power (quwwa). He argues that power is homonymous and only indicates the principle of action, not the full range of what the soul does. Perfection, on the other hand, encompasses both action and the soul's role in perfecting the species. Let's explore this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on power
Read the passage carefully. Note that the author first points out that 'power' is a homonym with two meanings.
2
Understand the problem
The problem with using 'power' is that it is ambiguous; it can mean either the principle of action or the principle of reception. Both are essential to the soul.
3
Evaluate the deficiency
If you restrict to one meaning, the definition is incomplete. If you consider both, it's using a homonym in definition, which is problematic.
4
Recognize the superiority of perfection
Perfection indicates both the principle of effects and the perfecter of the species, covering all aspects. The author concludes that perfection is more appropriate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس للنفوس السماوية اختلاف أفعال وآلات على أنه أيضاً موضع نظر لما ذكر في الشفاء من أن النفس اسم لمبدأ صدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة، عادمة للإرادة ولا خفاء في أنه معنى شامل لهما صالح لتعريفهما به على المذهبين، لأن فعل النفس السماوية أيضاً، ليس على نهج واحد، عادم للإرادة، بل على أنهاج مختلفة على رأي، وعلى نهج واحد مع الإرادة على الصحيح.

فإن قيل : النفس كما أنها كمال للجسم من حيث أنه بها يتم ويتحصل نوعاً كذلك هي صورة له ، من حيث أنها تقارن المادة، فيحصل جوهر نباتي أو حيواني، وقوة له من حيث أنها مبدأ صدور أفعاله، فلم أوثر في تعريفهما الكمال على الصورة والقوة. وما ذكروا من أنا نجد بعض الأجسام يختص بصدور آثار مختلفة عنها، فيقطع بأن ذلك ليس بجسميتة المشتركة، بل لمبادىء خاصة نسميها نفساً، ربما يشعر بأن الأولى ذكر القوة .

قلنا : أما إيثاره على الصورة فلأنها بالحقيقة اسم لما يحل المادة، فلا يتناول النفس الإنسانية المجردة، إلا بتجوز أو تجديد اصطلاح، ولأنها تقاس إلى المادة والكمال إلى النوع . ففي تعريف المعنى الذي به يتحصل الجسم فيصير أحد الأنواع ومصدر الأفعال، يكون المقيس إلى أمر هو نفس ذلك المتحصل أولى من المقيس إلى أمر بعيد لا يكون هو معه إلا بالقوة، ولا ينتسب إليه شيء من الأفاعيل.

هذا ملخص كلام الشفاء. وتقدير الإمام أن المقيس إلى النوع أولى، لأن ما الدلالة على النوع دلالة على المادة، لكونها جزءاً منه من غير عكس، ولأن النوع أقرب إلى الطبيعة الجنسية من المادة. وكان معناه أن النفس تقاس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية المبهمة الناقصة التي إنما تتحصل وتتم نوعاً، لما ينضاف إليها من الفصل بل النفس فتعريفها بالكمال المقيس إلى النوع الذي هو أقرب إلى الجنس من حيث أنهما متحدان في الجود لا يتمايزان إلا في العقل، بأن أخذ هذا مبهماً، وذاك متحصلاً، يكون أولى، هذا وقد يتوهم مما ذكره الإمام، أن النفس كمال بالقياس إلى أن الطبيعة الجنسية كانت ناقصة، وبالضياف الفصل إليها، كمل النوع ، إن الكمال يكون بالقياس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية على ما صرح به المواقف. وحينئذ يكون توسيط النوع وكونه أقرب إلى طبيعة الجنس وهو فاسد على ما لا يخفى. وأما إيثاره على القوة، فلأنها لفظ مشترك مبدأ الفعل كالتحريك ، ومبدأ القبول والانفعال كالإحساس، وكلاهما معتبر في العقل ، وفي الاقتصار على أحدهما، مع أنه إخلال بما هو مدلول النفس، استعمال للمشترك في التعريف، وكذا في اعتبارهما جميعاً ، ولأن الشيء إنما يكون نفساً بكونه مبدأ الاثار، ومكمل النوع ، ولفظ القوة لا يدل إلا على الأول، بخلاف لفظ الكمال ، ولا شك أن تعريف الشيء بما ينبيء عن جميع الجهات المعتبرة فيه، يكون أولى. ففي الجملة لما أمكن تفسير النفس بما يعم السماويات والارضيات ، ثم تمييز كل بما يخصها، وكان ذلك أقرب إلى الضبط آثره في المتن.
And the celestial souls do not have different actions and instruments; although this is also a point of inquiry because of what was mentioned in the Shifa, that 'soul' is a name for the principle of issuing actions that are not uniform, lacking will, and it is not hidden that it is a meaning inclusive of both [earthly and celestial] suitable for defining both according to the two schools, because the action of the celestial soul also is not uniform, lacking will, but rather on different courses according to one opinion, and on a uniform course with will according to the correct [view].

If it is said: The soul, just as it is a perfection (kamal) for the body in that through it the body becomes complete and attains its species, so too it is a form (sura) for it in that it accompanies matter, so a vegetal or animal substance results, and a power (quwwa) for it in that it is the principle of issuing its actions. Why then was preference given in defining both [earthly and celestial] to perfection (kamal) over form and power? And what they mentioned that we find some bodies distinguished by issuing diverse effects from them, so we judge that this is not due to their common corporeality, but rather to special principles which we call 'soul' – perhaps this suggests that power (quwwa) is [more] appropriate.

We say: As for preferring it over form (sura), it is because form in reality is a name for what inheres in matter, so it does not encompass the separate human soul except through metaphor or technical innovation. And because it is related to matter, whereas perfection (kamal) is related to the species. So in defining the meaning by which the body becomes complete and becomes one of the species and source of actions, what is related to a matter that is itself that which becomes complete is more appropriate than what is related to a distant matter that is only with it in potentiality and to which none of the actions are attributed.

This is the summary of the discourse of the Shifa. And the Imam's estimation is that what is related to the species is more appropriate, because indicating the species indicates the matter, since it is part of it, not conversely, and because the species is closer to the generic nature than matter. And its meaning is that the soul is related to the vague, incomplete generic nature which only becomes complete and attains its species by what is added to it from the differentia. But the soul—so defining it by perfection related to the species which is closer to the genus, in that they are united in existence and only differ in the intellect, by taking this as vague and that as determinate, is more appropriate. This is what he mentioned. And one might mistakenly understand from what the Imam mentioned that the soul is perfection in relation to the generic nature being incomplete, and by the attachment of the differentia to it, the species became complete – that perfection is in relation to the generic nature, as stated in al-Mawaqif. In that case, the intermediation of the species and its being closer to the generic nature would be invalid, as is clear. 

As for preferring it over power (quwwa), it is because it is a homonym: it may mean the principle of action like movement, and the principle of reception and passivity like sensation, and both are considered in the intellect. Restricting to one of them, while that is a deficiency in that which is the meaning of the soul, is using a homonym in definition; likewise considering both together. And because a thing is only a soul by being the principle of effects and perfecter of the species, and the word power (quwwa) signifies only the first, unlike the word perfection (kamal). There is no doubt that defining a thing by what indicates all the aspects considered in it is more appropriate. In summary, since it is possible to interpret 'soul' by what encompasses celestial and earthly [souls], then distinguishing each by what is specific to it, and that was closer to precision, he preferred it in the text.

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the definition of the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) against two alternative definitions: 'form' (sura) and 'power' (quwwa). Why the Soul is Defined as Perfection Relative to the Species
Argument for defining soul as perfection relative to species

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining the soul as perfection (kamaal) relative to the species is logically superior, based on the relationship between species, genus, and matter.

Core Idea
The soul's perfection is measured against the species, which is the true subject of completion.

This lesson unpacks the commentator's initial reasoning for preferring 'kamaal' as the definition of the soul. He examines how indicating the species indirectly indicates matter, and argues that the species is closer to the generic nature than matter. A potential misunderstanding from al-Razi is addressed to clarify the correct perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator uses logical terms like 'species', 'genus', 'matter'.
Pay attention to the structure: first a general statement, then a clarification.
2
Identify the logical terms
Find terms like 'naw' (species), 'jins' (genus), 'madda' (matter), and 'fasl' (specific difference). Understand how they relate.
3
Understand the correction of the misunderstanding
The commentator addresses a possible misinterpretation from the Imam (al-Razi). Why does he say that position would be false? What is the correct relationship between species, genus, and perfection?
This step clarifies the intended meaning.

الدلالة على النوع دلالة على المادة، لكونها جزءاً منه من غير عكس، ولأن النوع أقرب إلى الطبيعة الجنسية من المادة. وكان معناه أن النفس تقاس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية المبهمة الناقصة التي إنما تتحصل وتتم نوعاً، لما ينضاف إليها من الفصل بل النفس فتعريفها بالكمال المقيس إلى النوع الذي هو أقرب إلى الجنس من حيث أنهما متحدان في الجود لا يتمايزان إلا في العقل، بأن أخذ هذا مبهماً، وذاك متحصلاً، يكون أولى، هذا وقد يتوهم مما ذكره الإمام، أن النفس كمال بالقياس إلى أن الطبيعة الجنسية كانت ناقصة، وبإضافة الفصل إليها، كمل النوع، إن الكمال يكون بالقياس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية على ما صرح به المواقف. وحينئذ يكون توسيط النوع وكونه أقرب إلى طبيعة الجنس وهو فاسد على ما لا يخفى. وأما إيثاره على القوة، فلأنها لفظ مشترك مبدأ الفعل كالتحريك، ومبدأ القبول والانفعال كالإحساس، وكلاهما معتبر في العقل، وفي الاقتصار على أحدهما، مع أنه إخلال بما هو مدلول النفس، استعمال للمشترك في التعريف، وكذا في اعتبارهما جميعاً، ولأن الشيء إنما يكون نفساً بكونه مبدأ الآثار، ومكملاً للنوع، ولفظ القوة لا يدل إلا على الأول، بخلاف لفظ الكمال، ولا شك أن تعريف الشيء بما ينبئ عن جميع الجهات المعتبرة فيه، يكون أولى. ففي الجملة لما أمكن تفسير النفس بما يعم السماويات والأرضيات، ثم تمييز كل بما يخصها، وكان ذلك أقرب إلى الضبط آثره في المتن.
Indication of the species is an indication of the matter, because it is part of it without the converse, and because the species is closer to the generic nature than the matter. And its meaning was that the soul is measured against the vague deficient generic nature which only becomes concrete and complete as a species by what is added to it of the specific difference. Rather, the soul—so defining it by the perfection measured against the species, which is closer to the genus in that they are united in existence and are not distinguished except in the intellect, by taking this as vague and that as determinate—is more appropriate. This [must be said] despite a possible misunderstanding from what the Imam mentioned, that the soul is a perfection relative to the generic nature being deficient, and by adding the specific difference to it, the species becomes perfect, so that perfection is relative to the generic nature as stated by al-Mawaqif. If so, then the intermediation of the species and its being closer to the nature of the genus would be corrupt, as is not hidden. As for preferring it over 'quwwa' (potentiality), it is because 'quwwa' is a homonym: it can mean principle of action like movement, and principle of reception and passion like sensation, and both are considered in the soul. Restricting to one, while being a deficiency in what is connoted by the soul, is using a homonym in the definition, and likewise in considering both together. And because a thing is only a soul by being the principle of effects and the completer of the species, while the word 'quwwa' only indicates the former, unlike the word 'kamaal' (perfection). And there is no doubt that defining a thing by what indicates all the relevant aspects is more appropriate. In summary, since it is possible to interpret the soul in a way that encompasses celestial and terrestrial things, then to distinguish each by what is specific to it, and that being closer to precision, he preferred it in the text.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument. The commentator explains why the soul (nafs) should be defined as 'perfection' (kamaal) rather than 'potentiality' (quwwa). He begins by noting that indicating the species (naw') indirectly indicates matter, since the species includes matter as a part, and the species is closer to the generic nature than matter is. The generic nature is vague and deficient; it only becomes concrete as a species through the addition of a specific difference (fasl). Therefore, defining the soul by perfection relative to the species is better, because the species and the genus are united in existence and differ only conceptually (one vague, one determinate). A potential misunderstanding from the Imam (likely al-Razi) is addressed: if one thinks that perfection is relative to the generic nature alone, then the role of the species as intermediary would be faulty, but this is not the intended meaning. Then the reason for preferring 'kamaal' over 'quwwa' is detailed: 'quwwa' is a homonym (mushtarak) meaning both the principle of action (e.g., movement) and the principle of reception/passion (e.g., sensation). Both meanings are relevant to the soul. Using only one meaning in a definition is deficient, and using the homonym in a definition is problematic. Moreover, the soul is defined by being the source of effects (mabda' al-āthār) and the completer (mukammil) of the species. 'Quwwa' only covers the first aspect, while 'kamaal' covers both. Thus defining by 'kamaal' is more comprehensive and precise. The commentator concludes that since it is possible to define the soul generically for both celestial and terrestrial beings and then specify each, that approach is preferable, and this is why the text adopts it.

كمال
نوع
جنس
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perfection is defined relative to the generic nature alone.
Correction: Perfection is relative to the species, not the generic nature, because the species is the subject that becomes perfect. The generic nature is merely vague potential; perfection actualizes the species.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is defining the soul as perfection relative to the species better than defining it relative to matter?
A) Because the species is closer to the genus than matter is.
B) Because matter is not part of the species.
C) Because the species is more concrete than matter.
D) Because the genus is the same as matter.

Key Takeaway
The soul is best defined as perfection relative to the species because the species is the direct subject that perfection completes, while the genus is only vague potential.

The passage warns against misunderstanding that perfection is relative to the generic nature only. Preferring 'Kamaal' over 'Quwwa': The Problem of Homonymy and Incomplete Coverage
Critique of 'quwwa' as a definition and preference for 'kamaal'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'quwwa' (potentiality) is inadequate for defining the soul due to its homonymy and its failure to indicate the soul's role as the completer of the species.

Core Idea
'Potentiality' fails as a definition because it hides two meanings and misses the soul's perfection role.

This lesson focuses on the commentator's detailed critique of using 'quwwa' (potentiality) to define the soul. He argues that 'quwwa' is a homonym, meaning both principle of action and principle of reception, yet it only conveys the first. Moreover, the soul is defined as the principle of effects and the completer of the species, so 'kamaal' (perfection) is more comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against 'quwwa'
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما إيثاره على القوة' to the end. Notice the two main reasons given.
The argument is structured: first reason (homonymy), second reason (incomplete coverage), then a summary.
2
Understand the homonymy problem
The word 'quwwa' has two meanings: principle of action and principle of reception. Both are relevant to the soul, but using only one in a definition is deficient.
The commentator says both meanings are considered in the soul (al-‘aql). So using 'quwwa' alone fails to specify which meaning.
3
Understand the incompleteness
The soul is defined by being the principle of effects (mabda' al-athar) and the completer of the species (mukammil li'l-naw'). 'Quwwa' only indicates the former, while 'kamaal' indicates both.
Therefore, 'kamaal' is more comprehensive and precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدلالة على النوع دلالة على المادة، لكونها جزءاً منه من غير عكس، ولأن النوع أقرب إلى الطبيعة الجنسية من المادة. وكان معناه أن النفس تقاس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية المبهمة الناقصة التي إنما تتحصل وتتم نوعاً، لما ينضاف إليها من الفصل بل النفس فتعريفها بالكمال المقيس إلى النوع الذي هو أقرب إلى الجنس من حيث أنهما متحدان في الجود لا يتمايزان إلا في العقل، بأن أخذ هذا مبهماً، وذاك متحصلاً، يكون أولى، هذا وقد يتوهم مما ذكره الإمام، أن النفس كمال بالقياس إلى أن الطبيعة الجنسية كانت ناقصة، وبإضافة الفصل إليها، كمل النوع، إن الكمال يكون بالقياس إلى الطبيعة الجنسية على ما صرح به المواقف. وحينئذ يكون توسيط النوع وكونه أقرب إلى طبيعة الجنس وهو فاسد على ما لا يخفى. وأما إيثاره على القوة، فلأنها لفظ مشترك مبدأ الفعل كالتحريك، ومبدأ القبول والانفعال كالإحساس، وكلاهما معتبر في العقل، وفي الاقتصار على أحدهما، مع أنه إخلال بما هو مدلول النفس، استعمال للمشترك في التعريف، وكذا في اعتبارهما جميعاً، ولأن الشيء إنما يكون نفساً بكونه مبدأ الآثار، ومكملاً للنوع، ولفظ القوة لا يدل إلا على الأول، بخلاف لفظ الكمال، ولا شك أن تعريف الشيء بما ينبئ عن جميع الجهات المعتبرة فيه، يكون أولى. ففي الجملة لما أمكن تفسير النفس بما يعم السماويات والأرضيات، ثم تمييز كل بما يخصها، وكان ذلك أقرب إلى الضبط آثره في المتن.
Indication of the species is an indication of the matter, because it is part of it without the converse, and because the species is closer to the generic nature than the matter. And its meaning was that the soul is measured against the vague deficient generic nature which only becomes concrete and complete as a species by what is added to it of the specific difference. Rather, the soul—so defining it by the perfection measured against the species, which is closer to the genus in that they are united in existence and are not distinguished except in the intellect, by taking this as vague and that as determinate—is more appropriate. This [must be said] despite a possible misunderstanding from what the Imam mentioned, that the soul is a perfection relative to the generic nature being deficient, and by adding the specific difference to it, the species becomes perfect, so that perfection is relative to the generic nature as stated by al-Mawaqif. If so, then the intermediation of the species and its being closer to the nature of the genus would be corrupt, as is not hidden. As for preferring it over 'quwwa' (potentiality), it is because 'quwwa' is a homonym: it can mean principle of action like movement, and principle of reception and passion like sensation, and both are considered in the soul. Restricting to one, while being a deficiency in what is connoted by the soul, is using a homonym in the definition, and likewise in considering both together. And because a thing is only a soul by being the principle of effects and the completer of the species, while the word 'quwwa' only indicates the former, unlike the word 'kamaal' (perfection). And there is no doubt that defining a thing by what indicates all the relevant aspects is more appropriate. In summary, since it is possible to interpret the soul in a way that encompasses celestial and terrestrial things, then to distinguish each by what is specific to it, and that being closer to precision, he preferred it in the text.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument. The commentator explains why the soul (nafs) should be defined as 'perfection' (kamaal) rather than 'potentiality' (quwwa). He begins by noting that indicating the species (naw') indirectly indicates matter, since the species includes matter as a part, and the species is closer to the generic nature than matter is. The generic nature is vague and deficient; it only becomes concrete as a species through the addition of a specific difference (fasl). Therefore, defining the soul by perfection relative to the species is better, because the species and the genus are united in existence and differ only conceptually (one vague, one determinate). A potential misunderstanding from the Imam (likely al-Razi) is addressed: if one thinks that perfection is relative to the generic nature alone, then the role of the species as intermediary would be faulty, but this is not the intended meaning. Then the reason for preferring 'kamaal' over 'quwwa' is detailed: 'quwwa' is a homonym (mushtarak) meaning both the principle of action (e.g., movement) and the principle of reception/passion (e.g., sensation). Both meanings are relevant to the soul. Using only one meaning in a definition is deficient, and using the homonym in a definition is problematic. Moreover, the soul is defined by being the source of effects (mabda' al-āthār) and the completer (mukammil) of the species. 'Quwwa' only covers the first aspect, while 'kamaal' covers both. Thus defining by 'kamaal' is more comprehensive and precise. The commentator concludes that since it is possible to define the soul generically for both celestial and terrestrial beings and then specify each, that approach is preferable, and this is why the text adopts it.

مبدأ الآثار

Correction: 'Quwwa' is ambiguous (homonym) and only indicates the soul's role as principle of action, not its role as completer of the species. 'Kamaal' is more comprehensive.

Key Takeaway
