Distinguishing Celestial and Animal Cognition
Answering objection on celestial cognition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer distinguishes the type of sensation and intellection in celestial bodies from that in animals, preserving the differentiators for animality and humanity.

Core Idea
Not all sensation and intellection are alike; celestial bodies have a different kind.

The objection claims that celestial bodies also have sensation and intellection, so these cannot differentiate animal/human souls. The commentator responds by citing Avicenna's al-Shifa to clarify the specific meanings of these terms. This lesson examines that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: the objector claims that celestial bodies also have sensation and intellection, so these cannot differentiate animal/human souls.
Notice the logic: if X and Y share an attribute, that attribute cannot distinguish them.
2
Identify the distinction in the answer
The answer, citing Avicenna's al-Shifa, specifies that sensation here means passive imprinting of forms, and intellection means potential intellect. These are not present in celestial bodies.
The key terms are infi'aal, irtisaam al-mithaal, al-'aql al-hayulani, and al-'aql bi al-malaka.
3
Reflect on the implication
Understanding this distinction preserves the role of sensation and intellection as differentiators for animal and human souls.
This is a typical kalam move: clarifying terms to resolve an objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد ذكروا أن للسمويات حسا وحركة وتعقلاً كلياً، فعلى هذا لا يصلح ذلك مميزاً للحيوانية والإنسانية. قلنا : ذكر في الشفاء أن المراد بالحس ههنا ما يكون على طريق الانفعال، وارتسام المثال، وبالتعقل ما هو شأن العقل الهيولاني، والعقل بالملكة، وأمر السمويات ليس كذلك.
For it might be said: They have mentioned that the celestial bodies have sensation, motion, and universal intellection; accordingly, these [attributes] are not suitable as differentiators for animality and humanity. We answer: It is mentioned in al-Shifa that by sensation here is meant that which is in the way of passion (infi'aal) and imprinting of the form (irtisaam al-mithaal), and by intellection [is meant] that which is the concern of the material intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani) and the intellect in habitu (al-'aql bi al-malaka); but the case of celestial bodies is not like that.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector says that philosophers have attributed sensation, motion, and universal intellection to the celestial bodies (al-samawiyyat). If so, then these attributes cannot serve as differentiators (mumayyiz) for animal and human souls, since celestial bodies share them. The answer, citing Avicenna's al-Shifa, distinguishes the meanings: by 'sensation' here is meant the kind that occurs through passion (infi'aal) and the imprinting of forms (irtisaam al-mithaal)—i.e., a passive reception. By 'intellection' is meant that which is the domain of the material intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani) and the intellect in habitu (al-'aql bi al-malaka)—i.e., an intellectual capacity that develops from potentiality to actuality. The celestial bodies do not have this kind of passive or potential cognition; their knowledge is different (e.g., intuitive or always actual). Therefore, the distinction stands.

حس
تعقل
انفعال
ارتسام المثال
العقل الهيولاني
العقل بالملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensation and intellection in celestial bodies are the same as in animals.
Correction: The answer shows that celestial bodies have a different, non-passive, non-potential kind of cognition.

Checkpoint

D) Sensation by way of direct perception and intellection by way of eternal knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Celestial bodies have a different mode of cognition that is not passive or potential, thus animal/human cognition remains distinctive.

The celestial bodies have immediate, actual knowledge without effort. In your own life, what distractions or attachments keep your soul from attaining a more direct and purified understanding of the truth? Be specific: what will you change starting today?
?

Why Humans Have One Soul, Not Three
Explaining why philosophers reject multiple souls in one body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers reject the idea of three separate souls in a human body, and how a single rational soul encompasses the functions of vegetative and animal souls.

Core Idea
Higher souls include the powers of lower souls—no need for three souls in one body.

If every soul is defined by its effects, a human body seems to require three souls: vegetative, animal, and rational. But the philosophers clarify that each composite body has one soul that performs all the functions of its level, with higher souls incorporating the powers of lower ones. This passage explains that hierarchy and resolves the apparent implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the initial statement of implication and the response that the matter is not so.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the apparent implication
The commentator explains that if each soul has specific effects and each body has a specific form, then a human would seem to have three souls. Find that argument in the text.
This is the 'mawjab' (implication) referenced.
3
Understand the resolution: hierarchy of forms
The resolution from Sharh al-Isharat describes four levels of composites: mineral, vegetative, animal, and rational. Each higher form includes the functions of the lower ones. Trace how a rational soul alone does everything.
Notice that the rational soul is 'abstract' and performs all acts.
4
Reflect on the philosophical point
Why do philosophers prefer a single soul over multiple? Consider simplicity and the unity of the human person. The passage shows that a single principle can produce diverse effects.
This is a key insight for understanding Islamic psychology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم مقتضى قواعدهم أن يكون في الإنسان مثلاً نفس إنسانية، وأخرى حيوانية، وأخرى نباتية، لكن ذكروا أن ليس الأمر كذلك، بل يصدر عن النباتية ما يصدر عن القوة المعدنية، وعن الحيوانية ما يصدر عنها، وعن الإنسانية ما يصدر عن الكل). يعني أن مقتضى ما ذكروا من أن كل نفس مبدأ لآثار مخصوصة، وأن لكل نوع من الأجسام صورة نوعية هي جوهر حال في المادة وأن البدن الإنساني يتم جسماً خاصاً، ثم تتعلق به النفس الناطقة، يقتضي أن يكون في الإنسان نفس مبدأ تعقل الكليات، وكذا في كل حيوان بخواصه، وأخرى مبدأ الحركات والإحساسات، وأخرى مبدأ التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل. لكن ذكر في شرح الإشارات وغيره أن ليس الأمر كذلك، بل المركبات منها ما له صورة معدنية، يقتصر فعلها على حفظ المواد المجتمعة المتضادة بكيفياتها المتداعية إلى الانفكاك لاختلاف ميولها إلى أمكنتها المختلفة، ومنها ما له صورة تسمى نفساً نباتية يصدر عنها مع الحفظ المذكور جمع أجزاء أخر من الأسطقسات، وإضافتها إلى مواد المركب وصرفها في وجوه التغذية والإنماء والتوليد، ومنها ما له صورة تسمى نفساً حيوانية، يصدر عنها مع الأفعال النباتية والحفظ المذكور الحس والحركة الإرادية، ومنها ما له نفس مجردة، يصدر عنها مع الأفعال السابقة كلها النطق وما يتبعه.
He said: (Then the implication of their principles is that in a human there would be, for example, a human soul, another animal soul, and another vegetative soul, but they mentioned that the matter is not so. Rather, from the vegetative soul emanates what emanates from the mineral power, from the animal soul what emanates from it, and from the human soul what emanates from all.) That is, the implication of their statement that every soul is a principle of specific effects, and that for every type of body there is a specific form that is a substance inherent in matter, and that the human body becomes a particular body, then the rational soul attaches to it, would necessitate that in a human there be a soul that is the principle of universal intellection, likewise in every animal with its properties, and another soul that is the principle of motions and sensations, and another that is the principle of nutrition, growth, and generation of similars. However, it was mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat and elsewhere that the matter is not so. Rather, among composites are those that have a mineral form, whose action is limited to preserving the collected, opposing materials—due to their qualities that tend toward dissolution because of the difference of their inclinations to their various places. And among them are those that have a form called a vegetative soul, from which emanates, along with the mentioned preservation, the collection of other parts from the elements, their addition to the materials of the composite, and their distribution in the ways of nutrition, growth, and generation. And among them are those that have a form called an animal soul, from which emanates, along with the vegetative acts and the mentioned preservation, sensation and voluntary motion. And among them are those that have an abstract soul, from which emanates, along with all the previous acts, reason and what follows it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage explains and resolves an implication of the philosophers' principles. The commentator quotes the original text: if every soul is the principle of specific effects and every body has a specific substantial form, then in a human there would seem to be three souls—vegetative, animal, and rational. However, the philosophers state this is not the case; instead, the vegetative soul produces what the mineral power produces, the animal soul produces its own effects, and the human soul produces effects of all. The commentator then expands: the implication would be that in a human there is a soul for intellection, one for motion/sensation, and one for nutrition/growth. But in reality, according to Sharh al-Isharat, composite bodies are of four types: (1) those with a mineral form, which only preserves the collected opposing materials from disintegration; (2) those with a vegetative soul, which additionally gathers elements for nutrition, growth, and reproduction; (3) those with an animal soul, which adds sensation and voluntary motion; (4) those with an abstract (rational) soul, which adds reason and its concomitants. Thus, each composite has a single soul—the higher soul includes the powers of the lower ones. In a human, the rational soul itself performs all functions: it is the principle of nutrition (through its vegetative aspect), sensation (through its animal aspect), and reason. There are not three separate souls; rather, one soul at different levels of manifestation.

جوهر حال في المادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers believed in three separate souls (vegetative, animal, rational) coexisting in a human body.
Correction: The philosophers hold that a human has one rational soul which alone performs all functions of the lower souls by virtue of its comprehensive power.

Checkpoint

B) Two: animal and rational.

Key Takeaway
A human has one rational soul that performs all functions of vegetative, animal, and rational levels; there are not three separate souls in one body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Philosophers' Hierarchy of Forms
Taftazani explains the philosophers' view on the three souls and the hierarchy of forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view on the hierarchy of forms and why they do not posit multiple souls in one body.

Core Idea
A single form can produce multiple effects, not multiple souls.

Taftazani presents the philosophers' account of how forms operate in composite bodies. While their rules might suggest multiple souls in one being, they actually argue that a single form at a higher level encompasses the functions of lower forms. This passage outlines a clear hierarchy from mineral to rational soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first an implication, then a correction, then an enumeration of forms.
2
Identify the problem stated
Find where Taftazani says 'the requirement of their rules is that there would be...' and understand why multiple souls seem necessary.
3
Understand the correction
Notice the phrase 'but they mentioned that it is not so' and the subsequent explanation of how effects actually issue from one form.
4
Examine the hierarchy of forms
List the four levels: mineral, plant, animal, rational. Note what each adds beyond preservation.
Mineral: only preservation. Plant: adds nutrition, growth, reproduction. Animal: adds sensation and voluntary movement. Rational: adds speech and intellection.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this hierarchy matters: it shows a unified view of the soul where higher forms include lower ones, avoiding the need for multiple souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم مقتضى قواعدهم أن يكون في الإنسان مثلاً نفس إنسانية، وأخرى حيوانية، وأخرى نباتية، لكن ذكروا أن ليس الأمر كذلك، بل يصدر عن النباتية ما يصدر عن القوة المعدنية، وعن الحيوانية ما يصدر عنها، وعن الإنسانية ما يصدر عن الكل). يعني أن مقتضى ما ذكروا من أن كل نفس مبدأ لآثار مخصوصة، وأن لكل نوع من الأجسام صورة نوعية هي جوهر حال في المادة وأن البدن الإنساني يتم جسماً خاصاً، ثم تتعلق به النفس الناطقة، يقتضي أن يكون في الإنسان نفس مبدأ تعقل الكليات، وكذا في كل حيوان بخواصه، وأخرى مبدأ الحركات والإحساسات، وأخرى مبدأ التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل. هي من الأسطقسات لكن ذكر في شرح الإشارات وغيره أن ليس الأمر كذلك، بل المركبات منها ما له صورة معدنية، يقتصر فعلها على حفظ المواد المجتمعة المتضادة بكيفياتها المتداعية إلى الانفكاك لاختلاف ميولها إلى أمكنتها المختلفة، ومنها ما له صورة تسمى نفساً نباتية يصدر عنها مع الحفظ المذكور جمع أجزاء أخر من الأسطقسات، وإضافتها إلى مواد المركب وصرفها في وجوه التغذية والإنماء والتوليد، ومنها ما له صورة تسمى نفساً حيوانية، يصدر عنها مع الأفعال النباتية والحفظ المذكور الحس والحركة الإرادية، ومنها ما له نفس مجردة، يصدر عنها مع الأفعال السابقة كلها النطق وما يتبعه.
He said: 'Then the requirement of their rules is that there would be in man, for example, a human soul, another animal soul, and another vegetative soul, but they mentioned that it is not so. Rather, from the vegetative soul issues what issues from the mineral power, from the animal soul what issues from it, and from the human soul what issues from the whole.' That is, the requirement of what they mentioned—that every soul is a principle for specific effects, and that every type of body has a specific form which is a substance subsisting in matter, and that the human body becomes a specific body then the rational soul attaches to it—requires that there be in man a soul that is a principle for intellection of universals, and likewise in every animal according to its properties, and another principle for movements and sensations, and another principle for nutrition, growth, and reproduction of similars. They are from the elements. But it was mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat and elsewhere that it is not so. Rather, among composites: some have a mineral form whose action is limited to preserving the assembled materials that are opposite in their qualities, inclined to disassembly due to differing tendencies toward their different places; some have a form called a vegetative soul, from which, along with the mentioned preservation, issues the collection of other parts from the elements and their addition to the materials of the composite, and their direction in the ways of nutrition, growth, and reproduction; some have a form called an animal soul, from which, along with the vegetative acts and the mentioned preservation, issue sensation and voluntary movement; and some have a simple soul, from which, along with all the previous acts, issue speech and what follows it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the implication of the philosophers' rules: that there would be three souls in humans (rational, animal, vegetative). However, they clarify that this is not the case. Instead, the effects of the vegetative soul include what the mineral power does, etc. The explanation then describes the hierarchy of forms: mineral form (just preserves), plant form (adds nutrition, growth, reproduction), animal form (adds sensation and voluntary movement), and rational soul (adds speech and its consequences). This shows that different levels of forms produce increasingly complex effects.

Key Terms

صورة

نفس حيوانية
نفس ناطقة
أسطقسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers believe there are multiple souls in a single human body (e.g., a separate animal soul and vegetative soul).
Correction: They actually hold that one soul (the rational) encompasses all lower functions, and the effects attributed to lower souls are produced by the same higher soul.

Checkpoint
According to the explanation from Sharh al-Isharat, what is the function of the plant soul (النفس النباتية)?
A) It only preserves the composite material.
B) It adds sensation and voluntary movement to preservation.
C) It adds nutrition, growth, and reproduction to preservation.
D) It adds rational thought and speech.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' hierarchy of forms shows that each higher level subsumes the functions of lower levels, so a single rational soul in humans accounts for all life functions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This hierarchy shows that the rational soul includes the animal and vegetative functions. In your daily life, do you find that your reason governs your animal drives and bodily needs, or do you often act from the lower levels alone? What would it look like for you to truly let your rational soul—the capacity to think and speak truth—lead every action, even the most mundane?
?
Consider the mineral form: it only preserves, without growth or sensation. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you are merely 'preserving'—going through the motions without growth or feeling? What would it take for your faith to move from a static 'mineral' state to a dynamic 'vegetative' or even 'animal' state of eager responsiveness, and ultimately to a 'rational' consciousness of God?
?
The philosophers argue that a single rational soul accounts for all life functions, unifying the person. Do you experience yourself as fragmented—sometimes driven by appetite, sometimes by emotion, sometimes by intellect—or as integrated under a single purpose? What concrete step could you take today to live more from that unified, rational self that seeks truth and goodness?

Early Opinions on the Soul and the View of Many Mutakallimun
Sanusi lists early materialistic opinions and the view of many mutakallimun on the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of historical opinions on the soul, from materialistic theories to the view that it consists of the original remaining parts of the body.

Core Idea
The soul was described as fire, air, or temperament before mutakallimun refined it.

Sanusi begins by cataloguing a series of ancient and medieval opinions on the nature of the soul. These range from fire and air to the four elements and humors, and even to the idea that each person's soul is their unique temperament. He then presents the view of many early mutakallimun that the soul is the original remaining parts (al-ajzāʾ al-aṣliyya al-bāqiya) from birth to death, interpreting earlier corporeal descriptions as referring to the sensible structure that perceives. This lesson guides you through these opinions to see the development of thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the series of 'قيل' opinions
Read through the list of early opinions introduced by 'قيل' (it was said). Notice the progression from simple elements to more complex mixtures like humors and temperament.
These represent pre-Islamic and early philosophical views.
2
Note the indivisible part view
One of the opinions states the soul is an indivisible part (جزء لا يتجزأ) located in the heart. This is a precursor to atomistic views.
3
Understand the view of many mutakallimun
The passage says 'كثير من المتكلمين' hold that the soul is the original remaining parts that persist throughout life. This is a corporeal but enduring substance.
They interpreted the earlier 'sensible structure' as that which perceives, not the physical body itself.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Ask yourself how this view preserves the soul's identity across time while still being a body. This prepares for the more refined majority view in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل هي النار السارية في الهيكل المحسوس ، وقيل هي (٣) الهواء، وقيل الماء، وقيل العناصر الأربعة والمحبة والغلبة أي الشهوة والغضب، وقيل الأخلاط الأربعة، وقيل نفس كل شخص مزاجه الخاص، وقيل جزء لا يتجزأ في القلب . وكثير من المتكلمين على أنها الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره، وكان هذا مراد من قال : هي هذا الهيكل المحسوس، والبنية المحسوسة. أي التي من شأنها أن يحس بها وجمهورهم على أنها جسم مخالف بالماهية للجسم الذي يتولد منه الأعضاء نوراني علوي خفيف حي لذاته، نافذ من جواهر الأعضاء، سار فيها سريان ماء الورد في الورد والنار في الفحم، لا يتطرق إليه تبدل ولا انحلال، بقاؤه في الأعضاء حياة، وانتقاله عنها إلى عالم الأرواح موت، وقيل إنها أجسام لطيفة متكونة في القلب، سارية في الأعضاء من طريق الشرايين أي العروق الضاربة، أو متكونة في الدماغ ، نافذة في الأعصاب النابتة منه إلى جملة البدن، واختيار المحققين من الفلاسفة، وأهل الإسلام أنها جوهر مجرد في ذاته، متعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف ومتعلقه أولا هو ما ذكره المتكلمون من الروح القلبي المتكون في جوفه الأيسر من بخار الغذاء ولطيفه، ويفيل قوة بها تسري في جميع (۱)
It was said it is the fire flowing in the sensible structure, and it was said it is air (3), and it was said water, and it was said the four elements and love and dominance i.e., desire and anger, and it was said the four humors, and it was said the soul of each person is his specific temperament, and it was said an indivisible part in the heart. And many of the mutakallimun hold that it is the original remaining parts from the beginning of life to its end, and this was the meaning of those who said: it is this sensible structure and sensible constitution, i.e., that which perceives. And the majority of them hold that it is a body different in quiddity from the body from which limbs are generated, luminous, elevated, light, alive by itself, penetrating the essences of the limbs, flowing in them like the flow of rosewater in the rose and fire in coal, not subject to change or dissolution; its remaining in the limbs is life, and its departure from them to the world of spirits is death. And it was said they are subtle bodies formed in the heart, flowing in the limbs via the arteries i.e., the beating veins, or formed in the brain, penetrating the nerves sprouting from it to the whole body. And the choice of the investigators among the philosophers and the people of Islam is that it is an immaterial substance in itself, attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition, and its primary attachment is what the mutakallimun mentioned of the cardiac spirit formed in the left cavity from the vapor and subtlety of nourishment, and it pours forth a power by which it flows through all (1)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage catalogues various historical opinions on the nature of the soul. It begins with ancient materialistic views: fire, air, water, the four elements, love and dominance (desire and anger), the four humors, temperament, and an indivisible part in the heart. Then it presents the view of many mutakallimun that the soul is the original remaining parts (al-ajzāʾ al-aṣliyya al-bāqiya) from birth to death, interpreting the earlier phrase 'sensible structure' as that which perceives. The majority of mutakallimun, however, hold a more refined view: the soul is a body different in quiddity from the physical body, luminous, elevated, light, living by itself, penetrating the limbs like rosewater in a rose or fire in coal, unchanging, and its departure is death. A secondary view among some is that it consists of subtle bodies formed in the heart or brain and flowing through arteries or nerves. Finally, the author states the preferred view of the investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) among philosophers and Muslims: the soul is an immaterial substance (jawhar mujarrad) that manages and disposes the body, with its primary attachment being a cardiac spirit formed in the left ventricle from the vapor of nourishment, which pours forth power throughout the body.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الأصلية الباقية (al-ajzāʾ al-aṣliyya al-bāqiya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume all mutakallimun agreed the soul is a simple indivisible substance; the passage shows a substantial group held it to be composite of enduring parts.
Correction: The passage attributes to 'many mutakallimun' the view that the soul consists of parts that persist, not an indivisible atom.

Checkpoint
What does 'many mutakallimun' according to the passage hold the soul to be?
A) A luminous body
B) An immaterial substance
C) The original remaining parts
D) The four humors

Key Takeaway
Early mutakallimun commonly described the soul as the original remaining parts that persist throughout life, an interpretation of the perceiving sensible structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the many different opinions people have held about the soul, do you realize how easy it is to be led astray by mere speculation? Have you ever anchored your belief about the soul on anything other than revelation? What would it take for you to prioritize divine guidance over your own reasoning when it comes to matters of the unseen?
?
The mutakallimun described the soul as 'the original remaining parts' that persist from the beginning of life to its end. If your soul is the part of you that remains unchanged, does your identity depend on your body or on something deeper? How does this affect the way you view your own mortality and accountability?
?
Sanusi lists several materialistic theories about the soul that were once held. Are there any modern materialistic views about the soul or consciousness that you have unconsciously accepted without scrutiny? What would it mean for your faith if the soul is simply a product of chemical processes?

The Luminous Body and Immaterial Substance: Majority and Preferred Views
Sanusi presents the majority mutakallimun view, a minority subtle bodies view, and the preferred immaterial substance view of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority mutakallimun view of the soul as a luminous body and the philosopher's view as an immaterial substance, along with an intermediate subtle bodies opinion.

Core Idea
Most theologians said the soul is a luminous body; philosophers said it is an immaterial substance.

After mentioning the view of many mutakallimun, Sanusi presents the position of the majority (جمهورهم) who describe the soul as a luminous, elevated body that permeates the limbs like fire in coal. He then notes a secondary opinion that the soul consists of subtle bodies in the heart or brain. Finally, he states the choice of the investigators among philosophers and Muslims: the soul is an immaterial substance (جوهر مجرد) that manages the body, with its primary attachment being the cardiac spirit. This lesson unpacks these layered views.

Guided Path
1
Read the majority mutakallimun view
Read from 'وجمهورهم على أنها جسم مخالف' to 'إلى عالم الأرواح موت'. Notice the detailed description: luminous, light, alive by itself, flowing like rosewater.
This view distinguishes the soul from the physical body while still being a body of a different quiddity.
2
Examine the subtle bodies opinion
Read the phrase 'وقيل إنها أجسام لطيفة متكونة في القلب...' and understand that some posited souls as subtle bodies formed in the heart or brain and flowing through arteries or nerves.
This is a minority view within the corporealist tradition.
3
Understand the preferred immaterial substance view
Read the final part: 'واختيار المحققين... أنها جوهر مجرد في ذاته...' and note that it is a non-corporeal substance that manages the body, with its first attachment being the cardiac spirit.
'Immaterial substance' means it is not composed of matter and is not located in space.
4
Connect the cardiac spirit
Notice that the immaterial soul's primary attachment is the cardiac spirit mentioned by the mutakallimun. This spirit is a fine vapor in the left ventricle, serving as the link between the immaterial soul and the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And many of the mutakallimun hold that it is the original remaining parts from the beginning of life to its end, and this was the meaning of those who said: it is this sensible structure and sensible constitution, i.e., that which perceives. And the majority of them hold that it is a body different in quiddity from the body from which limbs are generated, luminous, elevated, light, alive by itself, penetrating the essences of the limbs, flowing in them like the flow of rosewater in the rose and fire in coal, not subject to change or dissolution; its remaining in the limbs is life, and its departure from them to the world of spirits is death. The soul is ultimately understood as an immaterial substance that manages the body, using the cardiac spirit as its first instrument.

?

Do you live as if your body is just a machine, or do you recognize each beat as a call from your soul? What will you do today to honour that connection?
?

Objection and Refutation on the Soul's Immateriality
Refutation of the argument from lack of evidence for immateriality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that absence of evidence for the soul's immateriality implies its materiality.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence; the same logic can be turned against the materialist.

In theological debates, opponents sometimes argue that if there is no proof for a claim, the opposite must be true. Here, the author presents such an objection against the immateriality of the soul and shows its weakness by applying the same reasoning to the opposite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that since there is no evidence for immateriality, the soul must be material. Note that this relies on the principle that a thing is only established by evidence.
2
Identify the refutation
The author points out that the same principle can be applied to the materialist position: there is no evidence the soul is a body either, so it cannot be considered a body. Notice the symmetry.
3
Reflect on the logical strength
Ask yourself why the objection fails. The author calls the principle 'weak' because it proves too much; it would lead to contradictions. Consider how the burden of proof is shared.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل بأنها لا دليل على تجردها، فيجب أن لا تكون مجردة، لأن الشيء إنما يثبت بدليل، وهو مع ابتنائه على القاعدة الواهية يعارض بأنه لا دليل على كونها جسماً أو جسمانياً، فيجب أن لا تكون كذلك.
And one may argue that there is no proof for its immateriality, therefore it must not be immaterial, because a thing is only established by evidence. This argument, while relying on a weak principle, is countered by [saying]: there is no proof for its being a body or corporeal, therefore it must not be such either.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the immateriality of the soul and its refutation. The objector says: there is no evidence that the soul is immaterial; therefore it should be considered material, since a thing is only established by evidence. The author responds that this argument is based on a weak principle (al-qa'ida al-wahiya) and can be countered symmetrically: there is also no evidence that the soul is a body or corporeal, so it must not be such either. Thus the objection fails to prove materiality.

Key Terms
تجرد
جسم
جسماني
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That absence of evidence is evidence of absence – if we can't prove immateriality, the soul must be material.
Correction: The same logic would force us to deny materiality for lack of evidence, showing the argument is inconclusive.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that there is no evidence for the soul's immateriality?
A) He accepts the objection and argues the soul must be material.
B) He denies that evidence is needed.
C) He points out that there is also no evidence for the soul's being a body.
D) He cites a revealed text.

Key Takeaway
The objection that lack of evidence for immateriality proves materiality is invalid because lack of evidence for materiality would similarly prove immateriality, showing the argument's weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the nature of your own soul—do you unconsciously assume it is material until proven otherwise? What if you assumed immateriality until proven otherwise? Which assumption feels more natural, and what does that reveal about your starting point?
?
The objector demands proof for immateriality but offers none for materiality. In your life, are there truths you reject simply because you lack 'proof', while accepting alternatives that are equally unproven? Can you name one such area?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Why did you believe the soul was material?' What would you say if your only answer is 'because I saw no evidence to the contrary'? How does this passage challenge you to reexamine your approach to unseen realities?

First Proof for the Soul's Immateriality: Perception of Immaterial Entities
The author presents the first proof for the soul's immateriality through its conception of immaterial entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the soul's immateriality based on its ability to conceive abstract, universal, and indivisible concepts.

Core Idea
The soul must be immaterial because it conceives what matter cannot contain.

Al-Sanusi presents the first of several proofs for the soul's immateriality. The argument begins with a simple observation: the human intellect can think about things that are not material — like God, justice, or unity. This passage explains why such thoughts are impossible for a material brain, and what this reveals about the soul's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic philosophical text once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the structure: a brief matn followed by a longer commentary.
2
Identify the three categories of immaterial concepts
The passage mentions three types of concepts that cannot inhere in matter: abstract entities (like the Necessary), universals (like humanity), and indivisibles (like unity). Underline each type as you find them.
Also note the additional argument about opposites coexisting in the mind.
3
Understand the inherence principle
The proof relies on the idea that intellection occurs by the form of the conceived object inhering in the intellect. If the form is immaterial, the intellect must also be immaterial.
This is the key logical link: like can only inhere in like.
4
Trace the conclusion
Follow how the impossibility of material inherence leads to the necessary conclusion that the soul is not a body but a simple, indivisible substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (احتجوا) بوجوه الأول: أنها بتعلقها تكون محلاً لما ليس بمادي كالمجردات، ولما يمتنع اختصاصه لوضع ومقدار كالكليات ولما لا يقبل الانقسام كالوجود، والوحدة والنقطة، وسائر البسائط التي إليها تنتهي المركبات ولا يمتنع اجتماعه في جسم كالضدين، بل الصور والأشكال المختلفة دون مجرد، إذ لا تزاحم فيه بين الصور ولو من الضدين أو النقيضين، ومبناه على أن كون التعقل بحصول الصورة، وعلى نفي ذي وضع غير منقسم، وعلى تساوي الصورة وذي الصورة في التجرد وفي الوضع والمقدار، وفي قبول الانقسام، وفي التضاد وإعدامها، وعلى استلزام انقسام المحل انقسام الحال فيما يكون الحلول لذات المحل، لا لطبيعة تلحقه كالنقطة في الخط المتناهي. أي احتج القائلون بتجرد النفس بوجوه : الأول: أنها تكون محلاً لأمور يمتنع حلولها في الماديات، وكل ما هو كذلك يكون مجرداً بالضرورة. أما بيان كونها محلاً لأمور هذا شأنها، فلأنها تتعقلها وقد سبق أن التعقل إنما يكون بحلول الصورة وانطباع المثال، والمادي لا يكون صورة لغير المادي، ومثالاً له. وأما بين تلك الأمور وامتناع حلولها في المادة، فهو أن من جملة معقولاتها الواجب وأن لم تعقله بالكنه، والجواهر المجردة وإن لم نقل بوجودها في الخارج، إذ ربما يعقل المعنى فيحكم بأنه موجود، أو ليس بموجود، ولا خفاء في امتناع حلول صورة المجرد في المادي، ومنها المعاني الكلية التي لا يمنع نفس تصورها الشركة كالإنسانية المتناولة بزيد وعمرو، فإنها يمتنع اختصاصها بشيء من المقادير والأوضاع والكيفيات وغير ذلك مما لا ينفك عنه الشيء المادي في الخارج، بل يجب تجردها عن جميع ذلك، وإلا لم تكن متناولة لما ليس له ذلك . والحاصل أن الحلول في المادي، يستلزم الاختصاص بشيء من المقادير، والأوضاع والكيفيات وغير ذلك . والكلية تنافي ذلك. فلو لم تكن النفس مجردة لم تكن محلاً للصورة الكلية، عاقلة لها، واللازم باطل. ومنها المعاني التي لا تقبل الانقسام، كالوجود والوحدة، والنقطة وغير ذلك. وإلا لكان كل معقول مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل وهو محال . ومع ذلك، فالمطلوب وهو وجود ما لا ينقسم أصلاً ، حاصل ، لأن الكثرة عبارة عن الوحدات ، وإذا كان من العقولات ما هو واحد غير منقسم لزم أن يكون محله العاقل له غير جسم ، بل مجرداً ، لأن الجسم والجسماني منقسم. وانقسام المحل مستلزم لانقسام الحال فيما يكون الحلول لذات المحل، كحلول السواد والحركة والمقدار في الجسم ، لا لطبيعة تلحقه كحلول النقطة في الخط لتناهيه وكحلول الشكل في السطح، لكونه ذا نهاية واحدة أو أكثر، وكحلول المحاذاة في الجسم ، من حيث وجود جسم آخر على وضع ما فيه ، وكحلول الوحدة في الاجزاء من حيث هي مجموع ، ومنها المعاني التي لا يمكن اجتماعها إلا في المجردات دون الجسم كالضدين وكعدة من الصور والأشكال، فإنه لا تزاحم بينها في العقل بل نتصورها ونحكم فيما بينها ، بامتناع الاجتماع في محل واحد من المواد الخارجية حكماً ضرورياً.
He said: They argued by evidence. First: That by its relation it becomes a locus for what is not material, like abstract entities, and for what cannot be specified by position and quantity, like universals, and for what does not accept division, like existence, unity, the point, and the rest of the simples to which composites reduce; and it is not impossible for it to gather in a body, like the two opposites, but rather the different forms and shapes without an abstract [locus], for there is no mutual obstruction in it between forms even if they are opposites or contradictories. This is based on the fact that intellection is by acquisition of the form, and on the denial of a positioned indivisible entity, and on the equality of the form and the possessor of the form in abstraction and in position and quantity, and in acceptance of division, and in opposition and its elimination, and on the requirement that division of the locus necessitates division of the accident in what the inherence is in the essence of the locus, not in a nature attaching to it, like the inherence of the point in the finite line. That is, those who affirm the abstraction of the soul argued by evidences: First: That it is a locus for things whose inherence in material things is impossible, and everything that is such is abstract by necessity. As for the demonstration that it is a locus for things of this nature, it is because it conceives them, and it has preceded that intellection is only by the inherence of the form and the impression of the image, and the material cannot be a form for the immaterial or an image for it. As for [the demonstration] of those things and the impossibility of their inherence in matter, it is that among its intellections is the Necessary [Existent] even if it does not conceive it essentially, and the abstract substances even if we do not affirm their existence externally, for sometimes it conceives a meaning and judges that it exists or does not exist, and there is no concealment of the impossibility of the inherence of the form of the abstract in the material; and among them are universal meanings which their very conception does not prevent participation, like humanity which encompasses Zayd and 'Amr, for it is impossible for it to be specified with any of the quantities, positions, qualities, and other things from which the material thing cannot be free externally; rather, it must be abstract from all that, otherwise it would not encompass what does not have that. The sum is that inherence in the material necessitates specification with some quantity, position, quality, and other things; universality opposes that. So if the soul were not abstract, it would not be a locus for the universal form, conceiving it, and the consequent is false. And among them are meanings that do not accept division, like existence, unity, the point, and other such. Otherwise, every intellection would be composed of actually infinite parts, which is absurd. Moreover, the required [exist], which is the existence of what is not divisible at all, obtains, because multiplicity is [composed of] units, and since among intellections there is what is one indivisible, its locus the intellector must be non-body, but abstract, because the body and corporeal are divisible. And division of the locus necessitates division of the accident in what inherence is in the essence of the locus, like the inherence of blackness, motion, and quantity in the body, not in a nature attaching to it, like the inherence of the point in the line due to its finiteness, and like the inherence of shape in the surface due to its having one or more ends, and like the inherence of adjacency in the body due to the existence of another body in a certain position in it, and like the inherence of unity in the parts as a whole; and among them are meanings that cannot be gathered except in abstracts, not in the body, like the two opposites and several forms and shapes, for there is no mutual obstruction between them in the intellect; rather, we conceive them and judge between them of the impossibility of their gathering in one locus of external materials as a necessary judgment.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the soul's immateriality (tajarrud al-nafs). The author first quotes the matn (original text) summarizing the argument: the soul is a locus for things that are not material — abstract entities, universals, indivisible concepts, and even opposites — and this requires it to be immaterial. The commentary then unpacks this. The proof rests on the principle that intellection occurs by the inherence of the form in the intellect. The soul conceives three types of things that cannot inhere in matter: (1) the Necessary Existent (al-wajib) and abstract substances, whose forms are immaterial; (2) universal meanings like 'humanity', which cannot be specified with particular quantities or positions; (3) indivisible concepts like existence, unity, and the point. Additionally, opposites (like whiteness and blackness) can coexist in the intellect but not in a material subject. If the soul were material, it would be divisible, and the accident inhering in it would also be divisible. But we conceive indivisible concepts, so the intellect must be indivisible. Also, if the soul were material, the universal form would become particularized, which contradicts its universality. Therefore, the soul must be immaterial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the soul's ability to conceive abstract concepts is merely an effect of the brain; this argument shows that the very nature of those concepts prevents material inherence.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that the immaterial nature of the concepts themselves (universality, indivisibility, etc.) requires an immaterial locus. A material brain cannot host forms that are not subject to material constraints.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, which of the following is NOT given as an example of a concept that cannot inhere in matter?
A) The Necessary Existent
B) Universals like humanity
C) Indivisible concepts like unity
D) Sensible qualities like colors

Key Takeaway
The soul must be immaterial because it can conceive abstract, universal, and indivisible concepts that a material substrate cannot host.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is immaterial and can grasp abstract, universal, and indivisible truths, why do you still live as if only material pleasures and bodily comfort are real?
?
You conceive of unity and existence—concepts that transcend matter. How does this awareness of the immaterial call you to prioritize the pursuit of the One who is Pure Existence?
?
Your mind holds opposites without conflict, yet your heart is torn by doubts and contradictions. If the soul was made to host truth clearly, what inner hypocrisy are you allowing to divide it?

The Four Proofs of the Soul's Immateriality
Sanusi enumerates four proofs of soul's immateriality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational proofs for the soul's immateriality based on its ability to conceive immaterial substances, universals, indivisibles, and incompatibles.

Core Idea
The soul must be immaterial because it conceives what cannot exist in matter.

Sanusi presents a compelling argument: the soul's conceptual capacities reveal its true nature. He shows that the soul can conceive immaterial substances, universals, indivisible concepts, and incompatibles—things that cannot inhere in a material substrate. This lesson unpacks these four proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the four concepts
The passage presents four types of concepts that the soul conceives: immaterial substances, universals, indivisibles, and incompatibles. Identify each one and notice how Sanusi argues that each cannot inhere in matter.
3
Trace the logical link
For each concept, ask yourself why its conception requires the soul to be immaterial. The key is that these concepts cannot be particularized or divided, as material things are.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بين تلك الأمور وامتناع حلولها في المادة، فهو أن من جملة معقولاتها الواجب وأن لم تعقله بالكنه، والجواهر المجردة وإن لم نقل بوجودها في الخارج، إذ ربما يعقل المعنى فيحكم بأنه موجود، أو ليس بموجود، ولا خفاء في امتناع حلول صورة المجرد في المادي، ومنها المعاني الكلية التي لا يمنع نفس تصورها الشركة كالإنسانية المتناولة بزيد وعمرو، فإنها يمتنع اختصاصها بشيء من المقادير والأوضاع والكيفيات وغير ذلك مما لا ينفك عنه الشيء المادي في الخارج، بل يجب تجردها عن جميع ذلك، وإلا لم تكن متناولة لما ليس له ذلك. والحاصل أن الحلول في المادي، يستلزم الاختصاص بشيء من المقادير، والأوضاع والكيفيات وغير ذلك. والكلية تنافي ذلك. فلو لم تكن النفس مجردة لم تكن محلاً للصورة الكلية، عاقلة لها، واللازم باطل. ومنها المعاني التي لا تقبل الانقسام، كالوجود والوحدة، والنقطة وغير ذلك. وإلا لكان كل معقول مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل وهو محال. ومع ذلك، فالمطلوب وهو وجود ما لا ينقسم أصلاً، حاصل، لأن الكثرة عبارة عن الوحدات، وإذا كان من العقولات ما هو واحد غير منقسم لزم أن يكون محله العاقل له غير جسم، بل مجرداً، لأن الجسم والجسماني منقسم. وانقسام المحل مستلزم لانقسام الحال فيما يكون الحلول لذات المحل، كحلول السواد والحركة والمقدار في الجسم، لا لطبيعة تلحقه كحلول النقطة في الخط لتناهيه وكحلول الشكل في السطح، لكونه ذا نهاية واحدة أو أكثر، وكحلول المحاذاة في الجسم، من حيث وجود جسم آخر على وضع ما فيه، وكحلول الوحدة في الاجزاء من حيث هي مجموع، ومنها المعاني التي لا يمكن اجتماعها إلا في المجردات دون الجسم كالضدين وكعدة من الصور والأشكال، فإنه لا تزاحم بينها في العقل بل نتصورها ونحكم فيما بينها، بامتناع الاجتماع في محل واحد من المواد الخارجية حكماً ضرورياً. وهذا الوجه من الاحتجاج يمكن أن يجعل وجوهاً أربعة: بأن يقال: لو كانت النفس جسماً، لما كانت عاقلة للمجردات أو للكليات، أو للبسائط، أو للممانعات. والجواب: أن هذا مبني على مقدمات غير مسلمة عند الخصم، منها أن تعقل الشيء، يكون بحلول صورته في العاقل، لا بمجرد إضافة بين العاقل والمعقول. ومنها: أن النفس لو لم تكن مجردة لكانت منقسمة، ولم لم يجز أن تكون جوهراً وضعياً غير منقسم، كالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ؟ ومنها: أن الشيء إذا كان مجرداً كانت صورته الإدراكية مجردة يمتنع حلولها في المادي، ولم لم يجز أن تكون حالة في جسم عاقل؟ لكنها إذا وجدت في الخارج، كانت ذلك الشيء المجرد. ومنها: أن صورة الشيء إذا اختصت بوضع ومقدار وكيفية لحلولها في جسم كذلك، كان الشيء أيضاً مختص بذلك، ولم لم يجز أن يكون في ذاته غير مختص بشيء من الأوضاع، والكيفيات والمقادير. ومنها أن الشيء إذا لم يقبل الانقسام كانت صورته الحاصلة في العاقل كذلك، ولم لم يجز أن تكون منقسمة بانقسام المحل العاقل، مع كون الشيء غير منقسم لذاته، ولو الحلوله في منقسم. ومنها: أن الشيئين إذا كانا بحيث يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل كالسواد والبياض كانت الصورتان الحاصلتان منهما في الجوهر العاقل كذلك. وقد سبق أن صورة الشيء قد تخالفه في كثير من الأحكام. ومنها: أن اجتماعهما في العاقل لا يجوز أن يكون لقيام كل منهما بجزء منه.
As for the relationship between those things and the impossibility of their indwelling in matter, it is that among the things it conceives are the Necessary Being, even if not in its essence, and immaterial substances even if we do not affirm their existence externally, for perhaps the intellect apprehends a meaning and judges it to exist or not exist, and there is no concealment in the impossibility of the indwelling of the form of the immaterial in the material. And among them are universal meanings whose very conception does not prevent sharing, like humanity applying to Zayd and 'Amr, for it is impossible for it to be particularized by any of the magnitudes, positions, qualities, or other things from which the material thing in external existence is not free; rather it must be abstracted from all that, otherwise it would not apply to what does not have that. The conclusion is that indwelling in the material entails particularization by some magnitude, position, quality, etc., and universality contradicts that. So if the soul were not immaterial, it would not be a locus for the universal form, apprehending it, and the consequent is false. And among them are meanings that do not admit division, like existence, unity, the point, etc. Otherwise every concept would be composed of infinite actual parts, which is impossible. Moreover, what is sought, which is the existence of something indivisible at all, is obtained, because multiplicity consists of units, and if among the concepts there is that which is one and indivisible, then it necessarily follows that its locus, the intellect that apprehends it, is not a body but rather immaterial, because the body and the corporeal are divisible. And the division of the locus necessitates the division of the indwelling thing when the indwelling is in the essence of the locus, like the indwelling of blackness, motion, and magnitude in the body—not for a nature that attaches to it, like the indwelling of the point in the line due to its finitude, and like the indwelling of the shape in the surface because it has one or more limits, and like the indwelling of the alignment in the body, from the aspect of another body existing in a certain position in it, and like the indwelling of unity in the parts from the aspect of the whole. And among them are meanings that can only coexist in immaterial things, not in the body, like the two opposites and several forms and shapes, for there is no conflict between them in the intellect; rather we conceive them and judge between them, with a necessary judgment, that it is impossible for them to coexist in one locus among external materials. This aspect of proof can be made into four aspects: by saying: If the soul were a body, it would not be capable of conceiving immaterial things, or universals, or simples, or incompatibles. The answer: that this is based on premises not granted by the opponent, among them: that the apprehension of a thing occurs by the indwelling of its form in the intellect, not merely by a relation between the intellect and the intelligible. And among them: that if the soul were not immaterial, it would be divisible—why could it not be a positional substance that is indivisible, like the indivisible part? And among them: that if a thing is immaterial, its perceptual form would be immaterial and impossible to inhere in the material—why could it not be in a corporeal intellect? But when it exists externally, it is that immaterial thing. And among them: that if the form of a thing is particularized by position, magnitude, and quality due to its indwelling in a body like that, the thing itself would also be particularized by that—why could it not be in its essence not particularized by any positions, qualities, and magnitudes? And among them: that if a thing does not admit division, its form occurring in the intellect would be likewise—why could it not be divided by the division of the intellectual locus, while the thing is indivisible in itself, even if it inheres in a divisible thing? And among them: that if two things are such that they cannot coexist in a locus, like blackness and whiteness, the two forms occurring from them in the intellectual substance would be likewise. And it has preceded that the form of a thing may differ from it in many judgments. And among them: that their coexistence in the intellect cannot be because each of them subsists in a part of it.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the immateriality of the soul based on its capacity to conceive certain types of concepts, and then addresses objections. The author first argues that the soul must be immaterial because it conceives immaterial substances, universals, indivisible concepts, and incompatibles—all of which cannot inhere in matter. He then summarizes this into four forms: the soul conceives immaterial things, universals, simples (indivisibles), and incompatibles. He then presents an opponent's objection: that all this rests on premises the opponent does not accept. The author lists seven specific premises that are contested and implicitly answers them by questioning why the opponent rejects them. The structure is a concise dialectical exchange: the author gives his proof, the opponent challenges the premises, and the author counters by questioning the opponent's rejection. The commentary follows the author's order: first the original argument, then the fourfold summary, then the answer refuting each contested premise.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four types of concepts used to prove the soul's immateriality?
A) Immaterial substances
B) Universals
C) Composite material objects
D) Indivisible concepts

Key Takeaway
The soul's ability to conceive universals, indivisibles, and immaterial things proves that it itself must be immaterial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You conceive of the Necessary Being, yet you often live as if He is distant. What would change if you truly believed your soul is immaterial and directly capable of knowing Him?
?
Your soul grasps universals like justice and mercy, but you rarely embody them. Why does your material body so often override what your immaterial soul knows is true?
?
You can hold opposites like fear and hope in your mind simultaneously for your own state. Are you using this capacity to reflect on your own standing with Allah, or are you avoiding the tension?

Refuting Objections to the Soul's Immateriality
Sanusi presents and refutes seven objections to the proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the immateriality proofs and how Sanusi responds by questioning the opponent's rejection of the premises.

Core Idea
The opponent's objections are based on arbitrary denials of obvious premises.

Having presented four proofs, Sanusi now anticipates the opponent's challenges. The opponent argues that each proof relies on unproven assumptions. Sanusi's response is not to prove the premises directly but to turn the tables: he questions why the opponent would deny these seemingly intuitive principles. This lesson explores the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that after summarizing the four proofs, Sanusi lists several premises the opponent denies.
2
Identify the seven contested premises
The passage lists seven 'منها' statements, each a premise the opponent rejects. Identify each one and understand why the opponent might deny it.
3
Analyze Sanusi's counter-questions
For each rejected premise, Sanusi responds with 'لم لم يجز' (why is it not permissible?). This rhetorical question challenges the opponent to justify their denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بين تلك الأمور وامتناع حلولها في المادة، فهو أن من جملة معقولاتها الواجب وأن لم تعقله بالكنه، والجواهر المجردة وإن لم نقل بوجودها في الخارج، إذ ربما يعقل المعنى فيحكم بأنه موجود، أو ليس بموجود، السطح، لكونه ذا نهاية واحدة أو أكثر، وكحلول المحاذاة في الجسم، من حيث وجود جسم آخر على وضع ما فيه، وكحلول الوحدة في الاجزاء من حيث هي مجموع، ومنها المعاني التي لا يمكن اجتماعها إلا في المجردات دون الجسم كالضدين وكعدة من الصور والأشكال، فإنه لا تزاحم بينها في العقل بل نتصورها ونحكم فيما بينها، بامتناع الاجتماع في محل واحد من المواد الخارجية حكماً ضرورياً. وهذا الوجه من الاحتجاج يمكن أن يجعل وجوهاً أربعة: بأن يقال: لو كانت النفس جسماً، لما كانت عاقلة للمجردات أو للكليات، أو للبسائط، أو للممانعات. والجواب: أن هذا مبني على مقدمات غير مسلمة عند الخصم، منها أن تعقل الشيء، يكون بحلول صورته في العاقل، لا بمجرد إضافة بين العاقل والمعقول. ومنها: أن النفس لو لم تكن مجردة لكانت منقسمة، ولم لم يجز أن تكون جوهراً وضعياً غير منقسم، كالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ؟ ومنها: أن الشيء إذا كان مجرداً كانت صورته الإدراكية مجردة يمتنع حلولها في المادي، ولم لم يجز أن تكون حالة في جسم عاقل؟ لكنها إذا وجدت في الخارج، كانت ذلك الشيء المجرد. ومنها: أن صورة الشيء إذا اختصت بوضع ومقدار وكيفية لحلولها في جسم كذلك، كان الشيء أيضاً مختص بذلك، ولم لم يجز أن يكون في ذاته غير مختص بشيء من الأوضاع، والكيفيات والمقادير. ومنها أن الشيء إذا لم يقبل الانقسام كانت صورته الحاصلة في العاقل كذلك، ولم لم يجز أن تكون منقسمة بانقسام المحل العاقل، مع كون الشيء غير منقسم لذاته، ولو الحلوله في منقسم. ومنها: أن الشيئين إذا كانا بحيث يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل كالسواد والبياض كانت الصورتان الحاصلتان منهما في الجوهر العاقل كذلك. وقد سبق أن صورة الشيء قد تخالفه في كثير من الأحكام. ومنها: أن اجتماعهما في العاقل لا يجوز أن يكون لقيام كل منهما بجزء منه.
As for the relationship between those things and the impossibility of their indwelling in matter, it is that among the things it conceives are the Necessary Being, even if not in its essence, and immaterial substances even if we do not affirm their existence externally, for perhaps the intellect apprehends a meaning and judges it to exist or not exist, and there is no concealment in the impossibility of the indwelling of the form of the immaterial in the material. And among them are universal meanings whose very conception does not prevent sharing, like humanity applying to Zayd and 'Amr, for it is impossible for it to be particularized by any of the magnitudes, positions, qualities, or other things from which the material thing in external existence is not free; rather it must be abstracted from all that, otherwise it would not apply to what does not have that. The conclusion is that indwelling in the material entails particularization by some magnitude, position, quality, etc., and universality contradicts that. So if the soul were not immaterial, it would not be a locus for the universal form, apprehending it, and the consequent is false. And among them are meanings that do not admit division, like existence, unity, the point, etc. Otherwise every concept would be composed of infinite actual parts, which is impossible. Moreover, what is sought, which is the existence of something indivisible at all, is obtained, because multiplicity consists of units, and if among the concepts there is that which is one and indivisible, then it necessarily follows that its locus, the intellect that apprehends it, is not a body but rather immaterial, because the body and the corporeal are divisible. And the division of the locus necessitates the division of the indwelling thing when the indwelling is in the essence of the locus, like the indwelling of blackness, motion, and magnitude in the body—not for a nature that attaches to it, like the indwelling of the point in the line due to its finitude, and like the indwelling of the shape in the surface because it has one or more limits, and like the indwelling of the alignment in the body, from the aspect of another body existing in a certain position in it, and like the indwelling of unity in the parts from the aspect of the whole. And among them are meanings that can only coexist in immaterial things, not in the body, like the two opposites and several forms and shapes, for there is no conflict between them in the intellect; rather we conceive them and judge between them, with a necessary judgment, that it is impossible for them to coexist in one locus among external materials. This aspect of proof can be made into four aspects: by saying: If the soul were a body, it would not be capable of conceiving immaterial things, or universals, or simples, or incompatibles. The answer: that this is based on premises not granted by the opponent, among them: that the apprehension of a thing occurs by the indwelling of its form in the intellect, not merely by a relation between the intellect and the intelligible. And among them: that if the soul were not immaterial, it would be divisible—why could it not be a positional substance that is indivisible, like the indivisible part? And among them: that if a thing is immaterial, its perceptual form would be immaterial and impossible to inhere in the material—why could it not be in a corporeal intellect? But when it exists externally, it is that immaterial thing. And among them: that if the form of a thing is particularized by position, magnitude, and quality due to its indwelling in a body like that, the thing itself would also be particularized by that—why could it not be in its essence not particularized by any positions, qualities, and magnitudes? And among them: that if a thing does not admit division, its form occurring in the intellect would be likewise—why could it not be divided by the division of the intellectual locus, while the thing is indivisible in itself, even if it inheres in a divisible thing? And among them: that if two things are such that they cannot coexist in a locus, like blackness and whiteness, the two forms occurring from them in the intellectual substance would be likewise. And it has preceded that the form of a thing may differ from it in many judgments. And among them: that their coexistence in the intellect cannot be because each of them subsists in a part of it.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the immateriality of the soul based on its capacity to conceive certain types of concepts, and then addresses objections. The author first argues that the soul must be immaterial because it conceives immaterial substances, universals, indivisible concepts, and incompatibles—all of which cannot inhere in matter. He then summarizes this into four forms: the soul conceives immaterial things, universals, simples (indivisibles), and incompatibles. He then presents an opponent's objection: that all this rests on premises the opponent does not accept. The author lists seven specific premises that are contested and implicitly answers them by questioning why the opponent rejects them. The structure is a concise dialectical exchange: the author gives his proof, the opponent challenges the premises, and the author counters by questioning the opponent's rejection. The commentary follows the author's order: first the original argument, then the fourfold summary, then the answer refuting each contested premise.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objections are unanswerable and the proofs are weak.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the objections rely on arbitrary denials of plausible premises, and thus the proofs remain valid.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise that the opponent denies, according to Sanusi?
A) The soul is immaterial
B) Apprehension occurs by indwelling of form
C) The soul is indivisible
D) Opposites cannot coexist

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent in this passage denies premises without reason. In your own life, when you encounter a command from Allah or a teaching of the Prophet that challenges your desires, do you honestly ask 'why not' to the possibility of your obedience? Or do you reject it without full investigation? Will you commit today to questioning your own arbitrary denials?
?
Sanusi's 'why not' is a question that forces the opponent to justify their denial. What truth about yourself—a sin, a weakness, a false belief—have you been denying without justification? Will you allow yourself to ask 'why not' and let the answer change how you live starting now?

Proof That the Soul Is Not a Pervading Substance
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that the rational soul is a substance pervading the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational soul cannot be a substance pervading the body, based on a reductio argument involving the impossibility of constant intellection.

Core Idea
The soul's intermittent self-awareness refutes its supposed material pervasion of the body.

This lesson dives into a powerful proof that the rational soul is not a material substance spread throughout the body. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the soul pervaded the body, it would always be aware of it, yet we know we are not always conscious of our bodies. The reasoning explores two possible explanations for intellection and shows both lead to contradictions. By the end, you'll see why the soul must be immaterial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage silently, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow of the proof.
2
Identify the basic argument structure
The argument is a conditional with a false consequent. Find the initial conditional and the evidence that rejects its result.
The consequent is 'perpetual intellection or none at all'.
3
Follow the two sub-branches
The author considers two ways intellection could relate to bodily presence: either mere presence suffices, or an image is needed. Trace each branch to its contradiction.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
The argument relies on the idea that perception requires a mental image (form) of the perceived. Notice how this principle is used to reject the material soul theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو كانت النفس الناطقة جوهراً سارياً في جسم، أو عرضاً حالاً فيه لزم أن يكون تعقلها لذلك الجسم، سواء كان تمام البدن أو بعض أعضائه كالقلب والدماغ دائماً، أو غير واقع أصلاً. واللازم باطل. لأن البدن وأعضاءه مما يعقل تارة، ويغفل عنه أخرى بحكم الوجدان. وجه اللزوم أنه إما أن يكفي في تعقل ذلك الجسم حضوره بنفسه عند القوة العاقلة، أو لا بل يتوقف على حضور الصورة منه، كإدراك الأمور الخارجة. فإن كان الأول لزم الأول، لوجوب وجود الحكم عند تمام العلة كإدراك النفس لذاتها، وصفاتها الحاصلة لها، لا بالمقايسة إلى الغير ككونها مدركة لذاتها، بخلاف ما يكون حصولها للنفس بعد المقايسة إلى الأشياء المغايرة لها ككونها مجردة عن المادة، غير حاصلة في الموضوع، فإنها لا تدركها دائماً، بل حال المقايسة فقط. وإن كان الثالث لزم الثاني، لأنه لو حصل لها تعقل ذلك الجسم في وقت دون وقت، كان ذلك لحصول صورته لها بعد ما لم تكن. وإذ قد فرضنا النفس مادية حاصلة في ذلك الجسم، لزم كون تلك الصورة حاصلة فيه، فلزم في مادة معينة اجتماع صورتين لشيء واحد. أعني الصورة المستمرة الوجود لذلك الجسم حالتي التعقل وعدمه، والصورة المتجددة التي تحصل له حال تعقل النفس إياه، وذلك محال، لأن الصورتين متغايرتان ضرورة والأشخاص المتجددة الماهية يمتنع أن تتغاير من غير تغاير المواد، وما يجري مجراها. ومبنى هذا الاحتجاج، على أن ليس الإدراك مجرد إضافة مخصوصة بين المدرك والمدرك، بل لا بد من حضور صورة من المدرك عند المدرك، وإلا لجاز أن لا يكون حصول الصورة العينية لذلك الجسم كافياً في تعقله، ومع هذا لا يحتاج إلى انتزاع الصورة، بل إلى حصول شرائط تلك الإضافة المخصوصة. وأيضاً لا تماثل بين الصورتين، لأن المنتزعة حالة في النفس، والأصلية في الجسم، بل في مادته. ولو جعلنا مثلين من جهة كونهما صورة لشيء واحد من غير اختلاف إلا في كون إحداهما منتزعة قائمة بالنفس والأخرى أصلية قائمة بالمادة، فاجتماع المثلين إنما يمتنع من جهة ارتفاع التمايز على ما سبق. وههنا الامتياز باق، وإن جعلا قائمين لشيء واحد، لأن قيام المنتزعة بواسطة النفس بخلاف الأصلية على أن الحق إن قيامها بمادة الجسم، وقيام المنتزعة بالجسم نفسه، وإن ذلك إنما يلزم لو كان حلول النفس في ذلك الجسم حلول العرض في محله لا بطريق مداخلة الأجزاء.
If the rational soul were a substance pervading a body, or an accident inhering in it, then necessarily its intellection of that body would be either perpetual (whether of the whole body or some parts like heart and brain) or not occurring at all. The consequent is false, because the body and its parts are sometimes intellected and sometimes overlooked, as experience testifies. The connection is that either the presence of the body itself before the intellective power suffices for its intellection, or it depends on the presence of an image of it, like the perception of external things. If the first, then the first [perpetual intellection] follows, because the judgment must exist when the cause is complete, like the soul's intellection of itself and its attributes that belong to it without relation to others (such as its being aware of itself), unlike what occurs after relation to other things (such as its being abstract from matter, not subsisting in a subject), which it does not always apprehend but only at the moment of relation. If the third [case—i.e., depending on an image], then the second [non-occurrence always?] follows, because if it has intellection of that body at some times and not others, that would be due to the acquisition of its image after it was not. And since we have assumed the soul to be material and subsisting in that body, that image must subsist in it, so in a given matter there would be a conjunction of two images of one thing: the continually existing image of that body in both states of intellection and non-intellection, and the renewed image that occurs for it when the soul intellets it. That is impossible because the two images are necessarily distinct, and individual essences that are renewed cannot be distinguished without distinction of matters and what resembles them. The basis of this argument is that perception is not merely a specific relation between perceiver and perceived, but there must be a presence of an image of the perceived with the perceiver; otherwise the presence of the actual image of that body could be sufficient for its intellection, and then it would not need to extract an image, but rather to obtain the conditions of that specific relation. Also there is no likeness between the two images, because the extracted one inheres in the soul, while the original is in the body—rather, in its matter. If we make them two likes as images of one thing with no difference except that one is extracted and subsists in the soul and the other is original and subsists in matter, then the conjunction of likes is only impossible due to the removal of distinction as previously. Here distinction remains, even if they are made to subsist for one thing, because the subsistence of the extracted via the soul is unlike the original—rather, the truth is that it subsists in the matter of the body, while the extracted subsists in the body itself; and that only follows if the soul's indwelling in that body is like the indwelling of an accident in its locus, not by way of interpenetration of parts.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that the rational soul is a substance pervading the body (or an accident in it). The author presents a proof by contradiction. He argues: If the soul were a substance pervading the body, then its intellection of the body would be either constant (always perceiving the body) or never at all. But we observe by experience that we sometimes perceive our body and sometimes are heedless of it. Therefore the consequent is false. The connection is analyzed: either the body's own presence before the intellective faculty is sufficient for intellection, or it requires an image. If mere presence sufficed, intellection would be perpetual (since the body is always present to the soul that pervades it), just as the soul always knows itself and its own attributes. But if an image is needed, then the soul would acquire the image at the moment of intellection, implying two images of the same body in the same matter: the body's own continuous form and the acquired image. This is impossible because two distinct images cannot coexist in one matter without distinction of matter. The argument rests on the principle that perception requires the presence of an image (form) of the perceived, not merely a relation. Additionally, the two images are not similar: the acquired image is in the soul, the original is in the body. Even if considered similar, their conjunction would require removal of distinction, but here distinction remains because they inhere in different substrates. Thus the initial assumption—that the soul is a substance pervading the body—is false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul is a material substance that fills the body or an accident in it.
Correction: If the soul were material or an accident in the body, its intellection of the body would be constant or never, but we experience intermittent intellection, so the soul cannot be material or accident in that sense.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the rational soul were a substance pervading the body, what would necessarily follow about its intellection of the body?
A) It would perceive the body sometimes and not others.
B) It would never perceive the body.
C) It would perceive the body constantly or not at all.
D) It would perceive the body only through sensory organs.

Key Takeaway
The argument proves that the rational soul cannot be a substance pervading the body because that would require constant or non-existent intellection, contrary to experience, and any attempt to explain occasional intellection leads to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience moments when you are acutely aware of your own body and other times when you are completely heedless of it. If your soul were material and pervading your body, you would have no such heedlessness. What does this intermittent awareness reveal about the true nature of your soul, and how does it challenge the way you live each day as if your self were just your physical body?
?
This argument shows that intellection requires a mental image. When you think about your own existence, do you rely on images or concepts? What would it mean to be aware of yourself without any such image? Are you willing to pursue that kind of direct self-awareness, or do you prefer the comfort of mental pictures?
?
Your heedlessness of your own body is not a minor lapse; it is a sign that your soul is not bound to matter. How does this truth shape your responsibility to be mindful of Allah and your own soul? Will you let this argument transform your daily practice of self-reflection and remembrance, or will you go back to living as if this argument were just theoretical?
