The Soul's Natural Attachment to the Body
Sanusi presents and answers the third objection about soul-body attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's attachment to a specific body is explained as natural desire rather than voluntary conception.

Core Idea
The soul attaches to the body by instinct, not by choice.

This passage addresses a key objection: if the soul must conceive a body before attaching, how can it attach to a particular one without prior knowledge? The answer reveals a subtle distinction between voluntary action and natural inclination, which resolves the difficulty without requiring prior cognition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection carefully: why does the soul need to conceive a specific body? Notice the claim about universal conception being insufficient.
The objection assumes attachment requires prior knowledge.
2
Find the answer's key term
The answer uses the term 'natural desire' (شوقي طبيعي) and 'suitability' (مناسبة). Understand how these shift the explanation from cognitive to instinctual.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this answer is significant for the broader discussion of the soul's nature. It avoids making the soul's embodiment a matter of choice.
This aligns with the soul's pre-essential nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنها عند تعلقها بالبدن تتصوره بعينه إذ لا يكفي في ذلك تصور بدن كلي، لأن نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، وكانت قبل استعمال الآلات مدركة للجزئيات. والجواب: أن تعلقها بالبدن شوقي طبيعي بمقتضى المناسبة لا إرادي ليتوقف على تصور البدن بعينه.
The third: when it (the soul) attaches to the body, it conceives the body in particular, because a conception of a universal body is not sufficient, since its relation to all (bodies) is equal; and before using instruments, it was perceiving particulars. And the answer: its attachment to the body is a natural desire due to suitability, not voluntary, so that it would depend on conceiving the particular body.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: when the soul attaches to a particular body, it must conceive that body in its particularity; otherwise, a universal conception would apply equally to all bodies. Also, before using instruments, it must already perceive particulars (since it attaches to one body). The answer explains that the soul's attachment to the body is a natural, instinctual desire due to a pre-established suitability (مناسبة), not a voluntary act that would require prior conception of the body. Therefore, the soul does not need to conceive the body in particular before attachment.

Answering the Fourth Objection: Conception of Instruments
Answer to the fourth objection about the soul's conception of instruments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul can conceive particular instruments without a mediating instrument, through relation and imagination.

Core Idea
The soul conceives instruments via relation to itself, not as independent particulars.

The fourth objection claims that when the soul intends to use instruments for perception or movement, it conceives them in their particularity without any instrument mediating that conception—seemingly contradicting the need for instruments. In this passage, the author provides a twofold answer: first, that the soul conceives instruments as related to itself, and second, that it can do so through imagination, which does not require sensory input.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'والجواب') to understand the objection: the soul conceives instruments in their particularity without an instrument.
Notice the contrast with the need for instruments in perception.
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
The answer begins by explaining that the soul conceives instruments only as instruments for itself, occurring in this body. The specification comes from this relation, not from perceiving them as independent particulars.
The path analogy illustrates how we can identify something by description without seeing it.
3
Examine the imagination route
The answer adds that the soul may also conceive them through imagination, which is not necessarily dependent on the senses.
This shows that imagination can supply particular concepts without sensory mediation.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Consider how the two parts together resolve the objection: the soul does not need a physical instrument to conceive instruments because it either relies on relation or on imagination.
This preserves the general principle that perception of physical objects requires instruments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: فإنها عند قصد استعمال الآلات للإدراكات والتحريكات تتصورها بأعيانها من غير توسط آلة. والجواب: أنها تتصورها من حيث هي آلات لهذه النفوس، حاصلة في هذا البدن المحسوس، فيحصل التخصيص بهذه الإضافة. ولا يلزم إدراكها من حيث كونها جزئيات في ذواتها، كما إذا حاولنا سلوك طريق نعرفه بصفاته بحيث يتعين في الخارج، وإن لم يكن نشاهده بعينه، ويجوز أن تدركها بعينها على سبيل التخيل، فإن التخيلات لا يجب أن تتأدى من طرق الحواس البتة.
The fourth: when it (the soul) intends to use the instruments for perceptions and movements, it conceives them in their particularity without the mediation of an instrument. And the answer: it conceives them insofar as they are instruments for these souls, occurring in this sensible body, so specification occurs through this addition. And it does not necessarily follow that it perceives them insofar as they are particulars in themselves, just as when we try to take a path that we know by its descriptions so that it is determined externally, even if we do not see it with our eyes; and it is possible that it perceives them in their particularity by way of imagination, for imaginations do not necessarily have to be conveyed through the senses at all.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: when the soul intends to use instruments for perception or movement, it conceives those instruments in their particularity without any instrument mediating that conception. This seems to contradict the need for instruments. The answer explains that the soul conceives the instruments not as independent particulars but as instruments related to itself and occurring in this particular body. The specification comes from this relationship. It does not require perceiving them as particulars in themselves, similar to how we can identify a path by its descriptions without seeing it. Alternatively, the soul can perceive them through imagination, which does not necessarily come from the senses.

Key Takeaway
The soul can conceive particular instruments without a mediating instrument by either relating them to itself or by imagining them, both of which bypass the need for sensory input.

Remaining Problem: Where is the Perceived Image?
The author raises a remaining problem about the location of the perceived image.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unresolved issue in the theory of perception: whether the image of a perceived particular exists in the soul, only in the bodily instrument, or is constituted by a relation.

Core Idea
The location of the perceived image raises a deep question about the nature of perception.

The author has explained that the soul perceives particulars through bodily instruments. Now a remaining problem is presented: does the image of the perceived thing come to exist in the soul as well? This passage explores two possibilities and their implications, pushing us to reconsider what perception truly is.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem's context
Recall that the soul perceives particulars by obtaining images in the bodily instruments. The problem asks: where exactly does the image reside?
This is a follow-up question arising from the previous theory.
2
Examine the first horn: image in the soul
If the image is also in the soul, then the old problem returns: how can a material image be imprinted in an abstract soul?
This horn seems problematic because it contradicts the soul's abstract nature.
3
Examine the second horn: image only in the instrument
If the image is only in the instrument (a part of the body), then what is perception? It cannot be just the presence of the image in the instrument.
This forces us to define what mental state constitutes perception.
4
Consider the suggested solution: perception as a relation
The passage hints that perception might be a specific relation (إضافة مخصوصة) or presence of the thing to the soul, not requiring the image in the soul.
This is a key concept to resolve the issue.
5
Reflect on the unresolved nature
The passage ends with a question, showing that this problem is still open. Think about why a relational account might solve the difficulty.
The author leaves it for further investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي هنا إشكال: وهو أنه إذا كان المدرك للجزئيات هو النفس، لكن بحصول الصورة في الآلة، فإما أن تكون الصورة حاصلة في النفس أيضاً على ما يشعر به قولهم ليس الإدراك بحصول الصورة في الآلة فقط، بل بحصولها في النفس، لحصولها في الآلة، وبالحضور عند المدرك للحضور عند الحس، من غير أن يكون هناك حضور مرتين، وحينئذ يعود المحذور. أعني ارتسام صورة الجزئي والمحسوس في المجرد، وإما ألا تكون الصورة حاصلة في النفس، بل في الآلة فقط على ما هو الظاهر من كلامهم، وليست الآلة إلا جزءاً من جسم تدبره النفس. فلا بد من تحقيق أن أي حالة تحصل للنفس نسميها إدراكاً، وحضوراً للشيء عند النفس، ولا يحصل بمجرد تحقق ذلك الشيء في نفسه، وحصول صورته في مادته وإنها إن كانت إضافة مخصوصة، فلم لا يكفي ذلك في إدراك
There remains here a problem: that if the perceiver of particulars is the soul, but by obtaining the image in the instrument, then either the image is also obtained in the soul, as their statement suggests: 'Perception is not by obtaining the image in the instrument only, but by its obtaining in the soul, because of its obtaining in the instrument, and by presence before the perceiver because of presence before the sense, without there being two presences'—and then the problem returns, namely the imprinting of the image of the particular and sensible in the abstract; or the image is not obtained in the soul, but only in the instrument, as is apparent from their statements, and the instrument is nothing but a part of a body that the soul manages. So it is necessary to investigate what state occurs in the soul that we call perception, and presence of the thing before the soul, and it does not occur merely by the thing's existence in itself and the obtaining of its image in its matter; and if it is a specific relation, why would that not suffice for perception?
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage raises a remaining problem. The problem is: if the soul perceives particulars by obtaining the image in the instrument, then either the image is also in the soul or it remains only in the instrument. If the image is also in the soul, then the original problem of imprinting a material image in an abstract soul returns. If the image is only in the instrument, which is just a part of the body managed by the soul, then we need to investigate what constitutes perception. The author suggests that perception is a specific relation or presence of the thing to the soul, which may not require the image to be in the soul. The passage ends by asking why that specific relation would not suffice for perception, implying that the nature of perception needs further investigation.

Key Terms
إشكال
الصورة
الحضور
إضافة مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The image must be in the soul for perception to occur.
Correction: The author questions this assumption, suggesting perception might be a relation or presence without the image being in the soul.

Checkpoint
What is the main unresolved question in the remaining problem?
A) Whether the soul exists.
B) Whether the image of the perceived thing is in the soul, in the instrument, or in a relation.
C) Whether the soul can perceive at all.
D) Whether the body is an instrument.

Key Takeaway
The problem reveals that perception may be a specific relation (إضافة مخصوصة) between the soul and the object, not merely the presence of an image in the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive something—like a beautiful scene or a hurtful word—do you ever think that it is not the thing itself entering your soul, but a relation your soul has to it? How might this change your attachment to sensory pleasures or pains?
?
The passage ends with a question: what state in the soul do we call perception? Have you ever examined your own inner state when you are confident you have truly 'perceived' something? What would it mean for your daily judgments if perception is not just receiving an image but a specific relation?
?
If perception is a relation rather than an image in the soul, then your inner world is not a mirror of the outer world. Does this challenge or comfort you? What would it require you to reconsider about how you know what is real?

The Four Stages of the Theoretical Intellect
Classifies the theoretical intellect into four stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four stages of the theoretical intellect (material, habitual, actual, acquired) and how they represent the soul's progression from potentiality to actual knowledge.

Core Idea
The theoretical intellect progresses through four stages from mere potentiality to full actuality.

In this passage, the author classifies the theoretical power of the rational soul into four distinct stages. This classification is foundational in Islamic philosophy for understanding how the human intellect acquires knowledge. We will explore each stage and the differences among them, as well as the related discussion on the soul's states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall structure. The passage is a classification.
Notice the fourfold division and the description of the soul's states.
2
Identify the four stages
Find the definitions of material intellect (عقل هيولاني), habitual intellect (عقل بالملكة), actual intellect (عقل بالفعل), and acquired intellect (عقل مستفاد).
Each is defined by a different level of disposition or perfection.
3
Understand the progression
Observe how the stages go from weak disposition (material) to full presence (acquired), and how the soul can be in different states.
The passage also notes scholarly differences on whether these are names of states, the soul, or powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المبحث الخامس :
المبحث الخامس
قوة النفس باعتبار تأثرها عن المبدأ للاستكمال عقلاً نظرياً،
وباعتبار تأثيرها في البدن للتكميل عقلاً عملياً.
أما النظرى فمراتبه أربع :
لأنه : إما استعداد ضعيف هو محض قابليتها للمعقولات ويسمى عقلاً هيولانياً.
أو بتوسط هو الاستعداد للنظريات بحصول الضروريات ويسمى عقلاً بالملكة .
أو بقوى هو الاقتدار على استحضار النظريات بلا كسب لكونها مكتسبة مخزونة ويسمى عقلاً بالفعل.
وإما كمال لها في ذلك وهو حضور النظريات عندها مشاهدة ويسمى العقل المستفاد .
وأيضاً النفس. إما خالية، أو متحلية بالضروريات فقط، أو بالنظريات أيضاً بدون الحضور أو معه. واختلفت العبارات (۱) في أن الأربعة أسامي لهذه الحالات، أو للنفس باعتبارها، أو لقوى هي مباديها، وفي أن المعتبر في المستفاد مجرد الحضور، حتى يكون بحسب الوجود مثل العقل بالفعل، وإن كان غاية
He said: The Fifth Investigation: The power of the soul in terms of its being affected by the First Principle for completion as a theoretical intellect, and in terms of its effect on the body for perfecting as a practical intellect. As for the theoretical, its stages are four: because it is either a weak disposition, which is pure receptivity to intelligibles, and it is called material intellect; or an intermediate one, which is the disposition for theoretical matters by the acquisition of necessary matters, and it is called habitual intellect; or a strong one, which is the ability to bring to mind theoretical matters without acquisition, because they are acquired and stored, and it is called actual intellect; or a perfection for it in that, which is the presence of theoretical matters before it as witnessed, and it is called acquired intellect. Also, the soul is either empty, or adorned only with necessary matters, or with theoretical matters as well without presence or with it. And expressions differ (1) as to whether the four are names for these states, or for the soul in its aspects, or for powers that are their principles, and as to whether the consideration in the acquired is mere presence, so that it is like actual intellect in terms of existence, even though it is the ultimate end.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the theoretical power of the rational soul. The author opens the Fifth Investigation by dividing the soul's power: under the aspect of being affected by the First Principle for completion, it is called theoretical intellect; under the aspect of affecting the body for perfecting, it is practical intellect. Then he proceeds to list the four stages of the theoretical intellect: (1) Material intellect: a weak disposition, mere receptivity to intelligibles. (2) Habitual intellect: an intermediate disposition for theoretical matters after acquiring necessary principles. (3) Actual intellect: a strong ability to bring theoretical matters to mind without active acquisition, because they are already acquired and stored. (4) Acquired intellect: perfection in that respect, where theoretical matters are present before it as witnessed. He adds that the soul can be either empty of intelligibles, adorned only with necessary truths, or adorned with theoretical matters either without presence (i.e., stored) or with presence (i.e., witnessed). Finally, he notes that scholars differ on whether these four terms apply to the states themselves, to the soul in those states, or to the powers that are their principles, and also whether the acquired intellect is merely the presence that equals actual intellect in existence or is the ultimate end.

Key Terms
عقل هيولاني
عقل بالملكة
عقل بالفعل
عقل مستفاد
قوة نظرية
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These four intellects are separate faculties of the soul.
Correction: They are actually four stages of the same theoretical intellect's development.

Checkpoint
Which stage of the theoretical intellect corresponds to the actual presence of theoretical matters as witnessed?
A) Material intellect
B) Habitual intellect
C) Actual intellect
D) Acquired intellect

Key Takeaway
The theoretical intellect develops through four stages: material (pure potential), habitual (with necessary principles), actual (stored knowledge ready to recall), and acquired (knowledge actually present).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which stage of the theoretical intellect do you find yourself in most of the time - are you merely receptive (hyulani), or do you have stored knowledge you rarely bring to presence (actual), or have you ever experienced knowledge being directly present (acquired)? What would it take for you to move to the next stage starting today?
?
The soul is described as either empty or adorned with necessary or theoretical matters. Be honest: what is your soul currently 'adorned' with from your daily intake of knowledge, and is that knowledge actually present to you as witnessed, or just stored away?
?
If the acquired intellect is the perfection where theoretical matters are present 'as witnessed', what one thing do you know intellectually that you have never truly witnessed or lived by? How can you turn that knowledge into presence before the end of this week?

The Two Intellects: Theoretical and Practical
Al-Taftazani defines the two intellects based on the soul's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the theoretical and practical intellect according to al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
The soul's power has two aspects: being affected for knowledge and affecting the body for action.

Al-Taftazani explains that the soul possesses a power that can be viewed in two ways: as receptive to higher principles for theoretical completion, or as active upon the body for practical perfection. This lesson defines these two fundamental faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the contrast between the two definitions.
The term 'power' (quwwa) is used for both aspects.
2
Identify the two intellects
Find the phrases that define the theoretical intellect and the practical intellect.
3
Understand the relationship
Notice that the practical intellect is also a perfection of the soul, using the body as an instrument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس قوة النفس باعتبار تأثرها عن المبدأ للاستكمال عقلاً نظرياً، وباعتبار تأثيرها في البدن للتكميل عقلاً عملياً. أما النظرى فمراتبه أربع : يسمى لأنه : إما استعداد ضعيف هو محض قابليتها للمعقولات ويسمى عقلاً هيولانياً. أو بتوسط هو الاستعداد للنظريات بحصول الضروريات ويسمى عقلاً بالملكة . أو بقوى هو الاقتدار على استحضار النظريات بلا كسب لكونها مكتسبة مخزونة ويسمى عقلاً بالفعل. وإما كمال لها في ذلك وهو حضور النظريات عندها مشاهدة ويسمى العقل المستفاد . وأيضاً النفس. إما خالية، أو متحلية بالضروريات فقط، أو بالنظريات أيضاً بدون الحضور أو معه. واختلفت العبارات في أن الأربعة أسامي لهذه الحالات، أو للنفس باعتبارها، أو لقوى هي مباديها، وفي أن المعتبر في المستفاد مجرد الحضور، حتى يكون بحسب الوجود مثل العقل بالفعل، وإن كان غاية بحسب الشرف والكمال، أو حضور الكل يحيث لا يغيب أصلاً حتى يمتنع أو يستبعد جدا حصوله، ما دامت النفس متعلقة والأول أشبه بحصر المراتب. قد سبق أن لفظ القوة، كما يطلق على مبدأ التغيير والفعل، فكذا على مبدأ التغير والانفعال فقوة النفس باعتبار تأثرها عما فوقها من المبادىء للاستكمال بالعلوم والإدراكات تسمى عقلاً نظرياً، وباعتبار تأثيرها في البدن لتكميل جوهره، وإن كان ذلك أيضاً عائداً إلى تكميل النفس، من جهة أن البدن آلة لها في تحصيل العلم والعمل، يسمى عقلاً علمياً. والمشهور أن مراتب النظري أربع : يسمى لأنه إما كمال وإما استعداد نحو الكمال قوى أو متوسط أو ضعيف. فالضعيف وهو محض قابلية النفس للادراكات يسمى عقلاً هيولانياً، تشبيهاً بالهيولي الأولى الخالية في نفسها عن جميع الصور القابلة لها، بمنزلة قوة الطفل للكتابة، والمتوسط وهو استعدادها لتحصيل النظريات، بعد حصول الضروريات عقلاً بالملكة، لما حصل لها من ملكة الانتقال إلى النظريات بمنزلة الأمي المستعد لتعلم الكتابة، وتختلف مراتب الناس في ذلك اختلافاً عظيماً، بحسب اختلاف درجات الاستعدادات، والقوى، وهو الاقتدار على استحضار النظريات متى شاءت من غير افتقار إلى كسب جديد، لكونها مكتسبة مخزونة تحضر الالتفات بمنزلة القادر على الكتابة حين لا يكتب وله أن يكتب متى شاء عقلاً بالفعل، لشدة قربه من الفعل، وأما الكمال فهو أن تحصل النظريات مشاهدة بمنزلة الكاتب حين يكتب، ويسمى عقلاً مستفاداً، أي من خارج وهو العقل الفعال الذي يخرج نفوسنا من القوة إلى الفعل، فيما له من الكمالات.
The fifth research: The power of the soul with respect to its being affected by the principle for completion as theoretical intellect, and with respect to its effect upon the body for perfecting as practical intellect. As for the theoretical, its ranks are four: It is called: either a weak preparedness which is the pure receptivity of the soul for intelligibles, and it is called hyulani intellect; or intermediate, which is the preparedness for theoretical matters by obtaining necessary truths, and it is called intellect by disposition; or strong, which is the ability to recall theoretical matters without acquisition because they are acquired and stored, and it is called intellect in act; or perfection for it in that, which is the presence of theoretical matters before it as witnessed, and it is called acquired intellect. Also, the soul is either devoid, or adorned only with necessary truths, or with theoretical matters also without presence or with it. And expressions differ as to whether the four are names for these states, or for the soul with respect to them, or for powers that are their principles; and whether what is considered in the acquired [intellect] is mere presence, so that it is in existence like the intellect in act, although it is ultimate in honor and perfection, or presence of everything such that it never absents at all, so that its occurrence is impossible or very unlikely as long as the soul is attached [to the body]. And the first is more appropriate for enumeration of ranks. It has already preceded that the term 'power', just as it is applied to the principle of change and action, so also to the principle of change and passion. So the power of the soul with respect to its being affected by what is above it from principles for completion by sciences and perceptions is called theoretical intellect; and with respect to its effect upon the body to perfect its substance (although that also returns to the perfection of the soul, in that the body is an instrument for it in acquiring science and action) is called practical intellect. And it is well known that the ranks of the theoretical are four: It is called because it is either perfection or preparedness toward perfection: strong, intermediate, or weak. The weak is the pure receptivity of the soul for perceptions, called hyulani intellect, by analogy to the primary matter which is in itself devoid of all forms receptive to it, like the capacity of a child for writing. The intermediate is its preparedness for acquiring theoretical matters after obtaining necessary truths, [called] intellect by disposition, because of the disposition it has acquired to transition to theoretical matters, like the illiterate person prepared to learn writing; and people's ranks in that differ greatly according to different degrees of preparedness. The strong is the ability to recall theoretical matters whenever it wishes without needing new acquisition, because they are acquired and stored, present by attention, like the one able to write when he is not writing, and he can write whenever he wishes, [called] intellect in act, because of its strong proximity to act. As for perfection, it is that theoretical matters are present as witnessed, like the writer when he writes, and it is called acquired intellect, i.e. from outside, and it is the active intellect which brings our souls from potentiality to act, with respect to the perfections it has.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the two faculties of the soul — theoretical and practical intellect — and then focuses on the theoretical intellect, enumerating its four ranks. The author begins by stating that the soul has power in two respects: as affected by higher principles for theoretical completion (theoretical intellect) and as affecting the body for practical perfection (practical intellect). For the theoretical intellect, there are four ranks. First, weak preparedness (al-isti'dad al-da'if), which is the pure receptivity of the soul for intelligibles, called hyulani intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani), by analogy with prime matter. Second, intermediate preparedness (al-mutawassit), which is the preparedness to acquire theoretical matters after necessary truths are obtained, called intellect by disposition (al-'aql bi-l-malaka), like an illiterate person ready to learn writing. Third, strong preparedness (al-qawi), which is the ability to recall theoretical matters without new acquisition, because they are already acquired and stored, called intellect in act (al-'aql bi-l-fi'l), like a skilled writer who can write at will. Fourth, perfection (al-kamal), which is the actual presence of theoretical matters before the soul as witnessed, called acquired intellect (al-'aql al-mustafad), like the writer actively writing. The author then notes that scholars differ on whether these four terms refer to states of the soul, to the soul itself in those states, or to the powers that are the principles of these states. There is also a discussion on whether the acquired intellect is merely the presence of known truths (making it existentially like the intellect in act but higher in honor) or a complete presence that never disappears (which is impossible or extremely rare in this life). The first view is deemed more appropriate. Finally, the passage reiterates the definition of power and provides analogies for each rank, emphasizing that people vary greatly in their preparedness. The active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) is identified as the external cause that brings the soul from potentiality to actuality.

Key Terms
قوة
عقل نظري
عقل عملي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the theoretical and practical intellects are separate powers.
Correction: They are two aspects of the same soul's power, distinguished by direction of influence.

Checkpoint
What is the theoretical intellect?
A) The soul's power affecting the body
B) The soul's power being affected by higher principles for knowledge
C) The active intellect
D) The power of writing

Key Takeaway
The theoretical intellect is the soul's receptive power for knowledge, while the practical intellect is its active power for perfecting the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which intellect are you primarily developing? Are you neglecting one? How does this imbalance affect your soul's perfection? What will you do this week to address it?
?
The passage says the practical intellect's effect on the body ultimately returns to the soul's perfection. How often do you see your daily actions – from prayer to work – as instruments for your soul's growth rather than mere routines? What would change if you started seeing them that way from tomorrow?
?
You now know that your soul has both a passive and active power. Are you mostly passive, absorbing knowledge without acting, or active without reflection? What is one concrete step you can take today to integrate both in a way that brings you closer to your Creator?

The Four Ranks of the Theoretical Intellect
Al-Taftazani enumerates the four ranks of the theoretical intellect and describes the corresponding states of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to name and distinguish the four ranks of the theoretical intellect: hyulani, bi-l-malaka, bi-l-fi'l, and mustafad.

Core Idea
The theoretical intellect progresses from pure potentiality to full actuality through four ranks.

After defining the theoretical intellect, al-Taftazani enumerates its four stages of development. These ranks describe the soul's preparedness or perfection regarding intelligibles, from the lowest potential to the highest actuality.

1

2

3

علمياً. والمشهور أن مراتب النظري أربع : يسمى لأنه إما كمال وإما استعداد نحو الكمال قوى أو متوسط أو ضعيف. فالضعيف وهو محض قابلية النفس للادراكات يسمى عقلاً هيولانياً، تشبيهاً بالهيولي الأولى الخالية في نفسها عن جميع الصور القابلة لها، بمنزلة قوة الطفل للكتابة، والمتوسط وهو استعدادها لتحصيل النظريات، بعد حصول الضروريات عقلاً بالملكة، لما حصل لها من ملكة الانتقال إلى النظريات بمنزلة الأمي المستعد لتعلم الكتابة، وتختلف مراتب الناس في ذلك اختلافاً عظيماً، بحسب اختلاف درجات الاستعدادات، والقوى، وهو الاقتدار على استحضار النظريات متى شاءت من غير افتقار إلى كسب جديد، لكونها مكتسبة مخزونة تحضر الالتفات بمنزلة القادر على الكتابة حين لا يكتب وله أن يكتب متى شاء عقلاً بالفعل، لشدة قربه من الفعل، وأما الكمال فهو أن تحصل النظريات مشاهدة بمنزلة الكاتب حين يكتب، ويسمى عقلاً مستفاداً، أي من خارج وهو العقل الفعال الذي يخرج نفوسنا من القوة إلى الفعل، فيما له من الكمالات.
The fifth research: The power of the soul with respect to its being affected by the principle for completion as theoretical intellect, and with respect to its effect upon the body for perfecting as practical intellect. As for the theoretical, its ranks are four: It is called: either a weak preparedness which is the pure receptivity of the soul for intelligibles, and it is called hyulani intellect; or intermediate, which is the preparedness for theoretical matters by obtaining necessary truths, and it is called intellect by disposition; or strong, which is the ability to recall theoretical matters without acquisition because they are acquired and stored, and it is called intellect in act; or perfection for it in that, which is the presence of theoretical matters before it as witnessed, and it is called acquired intellect. Also, the soul is either devoid, or adorned only with necessary truths, or with theoretical matters also without presence or with it. And expressions differ as to whether the four are names for these states, or for the soul with respect to them, or for powers that are their principles; and whether what is considered in the acquired [intellect] is mere presence, so that it is in existence like the intellect in act, although it is ultimate in honor and perfection, or presence of everything such that it never absents at all, so that its occurrence is impossible or very unlikely as long as the soul is attached [to the body]. And the first is more appropriate for enumeration of ranks. It has already preceded that the term 'power', just as it is applied to the principle of change and action, so also to the principle of change and passion. So the power of the soul with respect to its being affected by what is above it from principles for completion by sciences and perceptions is called theoretical intellect; and with respect to its effect upon the body to perfect its substance (although that also returns to the perfection of the soul, in that the body is an instrument for it in acquiring science and action) is called practical intellect. And it is well known that the ranks of the theoretical are four: It is called because it is either perfection or preparedness toward perfection: strong, intermediate, or weak. The weak is the pure receptivity of the soul for perceptions, called hyulani intellect, by analogy to the primary matter which is in itself devoid of all forms receptive to it, like the capacity of a child for writing. The intermediate is its preparedness for acquiring theoretical matters after obtaining necessary truths, [called] intellect by disposition, because of the disposition it has acquired to transition to theoretical matters, like the illiterate person prepared to learn writing; and people's ranks in that differ greatly according to different degrees of preparedness. The strong is the ability to recall theoretical matters whenever it wishes without needing new acquisition, because they are acquired and stored, present by attention, like the one able to write when he is not writing, and he can write whenever he wishes, [called] intellect in act, because of its strong proximity to act. As for perfection, it is that theoretical matters are present as witnessed, like the writer when he writes, and it is called acquired intellect, i.e. from outside, and it is the active intellect which brings our souls from potentiality to act, with respect to the perfections it has.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the two faculties of the soul — theoretical and practical intellect — and then focuses on the theoretical intellect, enumerating its four ranks. The author begins by stating that the soul has power in two respects: as affected by higher principles for theoretical completion (theoretical intellect) and as affecting the body for practical perfection (practical intellect). For the theoretical intellect, there are four ranks. First, weak preparedness (al-isti'dad al-da'if), which is the pure receptivity of the soul for intelligibles, called hyulani intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani), by analogy with prime matter. Second, intermediate preparedness (al-mutawassit), which is the preparedness to acquire theoretical matters after necessary truths are obtained, called intellect by disposition (al-'aql bi-l-malaka), like an illiterate person ready to learn writing. Third, strong preparedness (al-qawi), which is the ability to recall theoretical matters without new acquisition, because they are already acquired and stored, called intellect in act (al-'aql bi-l-fi'l), like a skilled writer who can write at will. Fourth, perfection (al-kamal), which is the actual presence of theoretical matters before the soul as witnessed, called acquired intellect (al-'aql al-mustafad), like the writer actively writing. The author then notes that scholars differ on whether these four terms refer to states of the soul, to the soul itself in those states, or to the powers that are the principles of these states. There is also a discussion on whether the acquired intellect is merely the presence of known truths (making it existentially like the intellect in act but higher in honor) or a complete presence that never disappears (which is impossible or extremely rare in this life). The first view is deemed more appropriate. Finally, the passage reiterates the definition of power and provides analogies for each rank, emphasizing that people vary greatly in their preparedness. The active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) is identified as the external cause that brings the soul from potentiality to actuality.

Key Terms
عقل هيولاني
عقل بالملكة
عقل بالفعل
عقل مستفاد
استعداد
كمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the ranks are separate types of intellects rather than stages of the same intellect.
Correction: They are states of preparedness or perfection of the theoretical intellect, not different intellects.

Checkpoint
What is the second rank of the theoretical intellect called?
A) العقل الهيولاني
B) العقل بالملكة
C) العقل بالفعل
D) العقل المستفاد

Key Takeaway
The theoretical intellect progresses through four ranks: hyulani, bi-l-malaka, bi-l-fi'l, and mustafad, from pure potentiality to full actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your intellect has the potential to reach the rank of acquired intellect, but do you actively seek knowledge and reflect to actualize that potential? What specific habits or distractions keep you at the level of mere disposition?
?
The soul can be adorned only with necessary truths. Are you satisfied with just the basics of your faith, or do you pursue deep understanding (tadabbur) of the Qur'an and authentic knowledge of Allah? What would it take for you to move toward higher ranks?
?
When you learn, do you aim for the knowledge to become present before you as witnessed, or do you store it without reflection? How can you make your knowledge more alive and transformative in your daily life and worship?

Detailed Analogies for the Four Ranks
Al-Taftazani elaborates the four ranks with analogies and discusses individual differences in preparedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogies used by al-Taftazani to illustrate each rank of the theoretical intellect.

Core Idea
Each rank of the intellect is like a stage in learning writing.

Al-Taftazani provides vivid analogies to clarify the four ranks of the theoretical intellect. By comparing the soul's progression to a child learning to write, he makes abstract concepts accessible.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogies
Read the passage and match each rank to its analogy: child, illiterate, able writer, writing writer.
The analogies are: hayulani = child's capacity, bi-l-malaka = illiterate ready to learn, bi-l-fi'l = skilled writer not writing, mustafad = writer actively writing.
2
Note the progression
Observe how the soul moves from pure potential to actuality, just as a child becomes a writer.
3
Understand individual differences
The passage notes that people differ greatly in their preparedness, just as in learning ability.

المبحث الخامس قوة النفس باعتبار تأثرها عن المبدأ للاستكمال عقلاً نظرياً، وباعتبار تأثيرها في البدن للتكميل عقلاً عملياً. أما النظرى فمراتبه أربع : يسمى لأنه : إما استعداد ضعيف هو محض قابليتها للمعقولات ويسمى عقلاً هيولانياً. أو بتوسط هو الاستعداد للنظريات بحصول الضروريات ويسمى عقلاً بالملكة . أو بقوى هو الاقتدار على استحضار النظريات بلا كسب لكونها مكتسبة مخزونة ويسمى عقلاً بالفعل. وإما How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four ranks do you honestly see yourself in regarding your understanding of your faith? What would it take for you to reach the 'intellect in act'—the ability to recall and apply theological knowledge at will?

?

Commit now to actively studying it until you can explain it clearly to someone else, as if you are the writer composing the answer.

Logic and Technical Terms

Scholarly Disputes on the Four Ranks and the Nature of Acquired Intellect
Al-Taftazani presents differing views on the referral of the four terms and the nature of the acquired intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly interpretations of the four ranks and the debate over the acquired intellect's nature.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree whether the four ranks are states, soul, or powers, and whether acquired intellect is mere presence or permanent presence.

After presenting the four ranks, al-Taftazani notes that scholars have different interpretations of what these terms actually refer to. There is also a critical debate about whether the acquired intellect is simply the presence of knowledge or a state of continuous, never-absent presence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the three different interpretations of what the four terms are names for.
The three possibilities: states, soul with respect to states, or powers that are principles.
2
Understand the debate on acquired intellect
There are two views: 1) mere presence, making it existentially like intellect in act but higher in honor; 2) presence that never absents, which is impossible in this life.
Al-Taftazani favors the first view as more appropriate for enumeration.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding the perfection of the soul.

Commentary
This passage defines the two faculties of the soul — theoretical and practical intellect — and then focuses on the theoretical intellect, enumerating its four ranks. The author begins by stating that the soul has power in two respects: as affected by higher principles for theoretical completion (theoretical intellect) and as affecting the body for practical perfection (practical intellect). For the theoretical intellect, there are four ranks. First, weak preparedness (al-isti'dad al-da'if), which is the pure receptivity of the soul for intelligibles, called hyulani intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani), by analogy with prime matter. Second, intermediate preparedness (al-mutawassit), which is the preparedness to acquire theoretical matters after necessary truths are obtained, called intellect by disposition (al-'aql bi-l-malaka), like an illiterate person ready to learn writing. Third, strong preparedness (al-qawi), which is the ability to recall theoretical matters without new acquisition, because they are already acquired and stored, called intellect in act (al-'aql bi-l-fi'l), like a skilled writer who can write at will. Fourth, perfection (al-kamal), which is the actual presence of theoretical matters before the soul as witnessed, called acquired intellect (al-'aql al-mustafad), like the writer actively writing. The author then notes that scholars differ on whether these four terms refer to states of the soul, to the soul itself in those states, or to the powers that are the principles of these states. There is also a discussion on whether the acquired intellect is merely the presence of known truths (making it existentially like the intellect in act but higher in honor) or a complete presence that never disappears (which is impossible or extremely rare in this life). The first view is deemed more appropriate. Finally, the passage reiterates the definition of power and provides analogies for each rank, emphasizing that people vary greatly in their preparedness. The active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) is identified as the external cause that brings the soul from potentiality to actuality.

Correction: Scholars disagree on whether they refer to states, the soul, or powers.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, which view on the acquired intellect is more appropriate for the enumeration of ranks?
A) Presence that never absents
B) Mere presence, like the intellect in act

D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The four ranks are subject to different interpretations, and the acquired intellect is best understood as the presence of theoretical matters, not a permanent unceasing presence.

?
If the acquired intellect is only a presence that can leave, how does that change your view of knowledge as something that must be constantly renewed? What specific steps will you take this week to ensure that the truths you hold are not forgotten?
?
The passage suggests that permanent presence of all knowledge is impossible while the soul is attached to the body. Does that thought humble you or drive you to seek more? How will you respond to the reality that your intellectual grasp is always incomplete?

The Four Ranks of the Theoretical Intellect
The author defines the theoretical intellect and enumerates its four ranks with analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four ranks of the theoretical intellect and the analogy of writing used to explain their progression from potentiality to actuality.

Core Idea
The theoretical intellect progresses through four ranks from pure potentiality to acquired perfection.

This passage from al-Maqasid lays the foundation for understanding how the human intellect acquires knowledge. The author distinguishes the theoretical intellect from the practical intellect and then enumerates the four stages of the theoretical intellect, each with a vivid analogy to writing. This classification is essential for later discussions on the acquisition of knowledge and the role of the active intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the author's progression from defining the two intellects to detailing the four ranks.
2
Understand the two types of intellect
Notice how the author distinguishes theoretical (receptive) from practical (active) intellect based on the soul's relation to principles above it and its effect on the body.
The theoretical intellect is about perception, while the practical intellect is about action.
3
Identify the four ranks
Locate the four ranks: material (hayulani), dispositional (bil-malaka), actual (bil-fi'l), and acquired (mustafad). Pay attention to the analogies with writing.
Each rank represents a degree of preparedness or actuality.
4
Examine the metaphors
Reflect on how the analogies (child, illiterate, capable writer, writing writer) illustrate the progression from pure potentiality to perfect actuality.
The analogies make abstract concepts concrete.
5
Note the role of the active intellect
In the final rank, the author equates the acquired intellect with the active intellect, using the sun-sight metaphor to explain how it brings souls from potentiality to actuality.
This connects the human intellect to a transcendent principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد سبق أن لفظ القوة، كما يطلق على مبدأ التغيير والفعل، فكذا على مبدأ التغير والانفعال فقوة النفس باعتبار تأثرها عما فوقها من المبادىء للاستكمال بالعلوم والإدراكات تسمى عقلاً نظرياً، وباعتبار تأثيرها في البدن لتكميل جوهره (وإن كان ذلك أيضاً عائداً إلى تكميل النفس، من جهة أن البدن آلة لها في تحصيل العلم) والعمل، يسمى عقلاً علمياً. والمشهور أن مراتب النظري أربع : يسمى لأنه إما كمال وإما استعداد نحو الكمال قوى أو متوسط أو ضعيف. فالضعيف وهو محض قابلية النفس للادراكات يسمى عقلاً هيولانياً، تشبيهاً بالهيولي الأولى الخالية في نفسها عن جميع الصور القابلة لها، بمنزلة قوة الطفل للكتابة، والمتوسط وهو استعدادها لتحصيل النظريات، بعد حصول الضروريات عقلاً بالملكة، لما حصل لها من ملكة الانتقال إلى النظريات بمنزلة الأمي المستعد لتعلم الكتابة، وتختلف مراتب الناس في ذلك اختلافاً عظيماً، بحسب اختلاف درجات الاستعدادات، والقوى، وهو الاقتدار على استحضار النظريات متى شاءت من غير افتقار إلى كسب جديد، لكونها مكتسبة مخزونة تحضر الالتفات بمنزلة القادر على الكتابة حين لا يكتب وله أن يكتب متى شاء عقلاً بالفعل، لشدة قربه من الفعل، وأما الكمال فهو أن تحصل النظريات مشاهدة بمنزلة الكاتب حين يكتب، ويسمى عقلاً مستفاداً، أي من خارج وهو العقل الفعال الذي يخرج نفوسنا من القوة إلى الفعل، فيما له من الكمالات، ونسبته إلينا نسبة الشمس إلى أبصارنا.
It has preceded that the word 'quwwa' (power/force) is used for the principle of change and action, and likewise for the principle of change and passion. So the power of the soul, by virtue of its being affected by what is above it of the principles for perfection through sciences and perceptions, is called 'theoretical intellect' ('aqlun nazariyyun), and by virtue of its effect on the body to perfect its substance (even if that also returns to perfecting the soul, insofar as the body is an instrument for it in acquiring knowledge) and action, it is called 'practical intellect' ('aqlun 'amaliyyun). And the well-known (view) is that the ranks of the theoretical (intellect) are four: because it is either perfection (kamal) or preparedness (isti'dad) towards perfection, strong or intermediate or weak. The weak – which is pure potentiality (qabiliyya) of the soul for perceptions – is called 'material intellect' ('aqlun hayulaniyyun), by analogy to prime matter (hayula) devoid in itself of all forms receptive to it, like the child's power for writing. The intermediate – which is its preparedness for acquiring theoretical (sciences), after acquiring necessary (sciences), as intellect by disposition ('aql bil-malaka), because it has acquired the disposition (malaka) of transition to theoretical (sciences) – is like an illiterate person ready to learn writing. And the ranks of people differ greatly in that according to the different degrees of preparedness. And the strong (quwwa) – which is the ability to bring to mind theoretical (propositions) whenever it wishes without needing a new acquisition, because they are acquired and stored, present upon attention, like the one who is able to write when he is not writing and can write whenever he wishes, as intellect in actuality ('aql bil-fi'l), due to its extreme closeness to actuality. As for perfection (al-kamal), it is that the theoretical (propositions) are obtained as witnessed, like the writer when he writes, and it is called 'acquired intellect' ('aqlun mustafadun), i.e., from outside, and it is the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) which brings our souls from potentiality to actuality with respect to the perfections it possesses. And its relation to us is like the relation of the sun to our sight.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This is a classification passage. The author begins by distinguishing between theoretical and practical intellect based on the soul's receptivity to principles and its effect on the body. He then presents the well-known division of the theoretical intellect into four ranks: weak (material), intermediate (dispositional), strong (actual), and perfection (acquired). Each rank is explained using an analogy to writing: the child's power to learn, the illiterate person's readiness, the capable writer who is not currently writing, and the writer actually writing. The fourth rank, acquired intellect, is further described as the active intellect that actualizes our souls, with a metaphor of the sun illuminating sight. The author follows the argumentative order of the source, first defining terms, then enumerating the four ranks with their characteristics and analogies.

Differences in Defining the Intellect's Stages
Differing scholarly definitions of the intellect's stages and their interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars differed in defining the stages of the intellect and what each definition emphasizes.

Core Idea
The intellect's stages are defined differently by scholars, reflecting deeper disagreements about the soul's faculties.

The passage presents a survey of conflicting definitions for the four stages of the intellect (material, disposition, actual, acquired). Scholars disagree whether these stages refer to preparedness themselves, the soul qualified by them, or specific faculties. This lesson walks through each position, including Ibn Sina's and al-Razi's distinctive views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the variety of opinions presented.
The passage is dense with different views; do not worry about memorizing each one yet.
2
Identify the three levels of disagreement
First, scholars disagree on what the terms refer to (preparedness, soul, or faculties). Then, specific definitions vary for each stage. Finally, there is disagreement about the acquired intellect's nature.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's equivalence of actual and acquired intellect
Ibn Sina says the actual intellect and acquired intellect are one in essence but differ in aspect. This is a key nuance.
This view is from his book al-Mabda' wal-Ma'ad.
4
Understand the debate about presence of sciences
Some say the acquired intellect requires constant presence of all possible sciences (impossible in body), while others say mere presence suffices, allowing it to precede actual intellect in existence.
Al-Razi's position is mentioned on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتختلف عبارات القوم في أن المذكورات أسام لهذه الاستعدادات والكمال، أو للنفس باعتبار اتصافها بها، أو لقوى في النفس هي مباديها، مثلاً يقال تارة : إن العقل الهيولاني، هو استعداد النفس لقبول العلوم الضرورية، وتارة أنه قوة استعدادية، أو قوة من شأنها الاستعداد المحض، وتارة أنه النفس في مبدأ الفطرة من حيث قابليتها للعلوم، وكذا في البواقي وربما يقال إن العقل بالملكة هو حصول الضروريات من حيث تتأدى إلى النظريات. وقال ابن سينا هو صورة المعقولات الأولى، تتبعها القوة على كسب غيرها بمنزلة الضوء للأبصار والمستفاد هو المعقولات المكتسبة عند حصولها بالفعل. وقال في كتاب المبدأ والمعاد. إن العقل بالفعل، والعقل المستفاد واحد بالذات مختلف بالاعتبار، فإنه من جهة تحصيله للنظريات عقل بالفعل، ومن جهة حصولها فيه بالفعل عقل مستفاد . وربما قيل : هو عقل بالفعل بالقياس إلى ذاته ، ومستفاد بالقياس إلى فاعله . واختلفوا أيضاً في أن المعتبر في المستفاد هو حضور النظريات الممكنة للنفس، بحيث لا تغيب أصلاً حتى قالوا إنه آخر المراتب البشرية، وأول المنازل الملكية، وأنه يمتنع أو يستبعد جداً ما دامت النفس متعلقة بالبدن، أو مجرد الحضور حتى يكون قبل العقل بالفعل بحسب الوجود على ما صرح به الإمام، وإن كان بحسب الشرف هو الغاية، والرئيس المطلق الذي يخدمه سائر القوى من الإنسانية، والحيوانية، والنباتية، ولا يخفى أن هذا أشبه بما اتفقوا عليه من حصر المراتب في الأربع . نعم حضور الكل بحيث لا يغيب أصلاً، هو كمال مرتبة المستفاد . وذكر الإمام في بيان المراتب . أن النفس إن خلت عن العلوم مع أنها قابلة لها، سميت في فلك الحالة عقلاً هيولانياً، وإلا فإن حصلت الضروريات فقط سميت حينئذ عملاً بالملكة، وإن حصلت النظريات أيضاً، فإن لم تكن حاصلة بالفعل، بل لها
And the statements of the people (scholars) differ concerning whether the aforementioned (terms) are names for these preparednesses and perfection, or for the soul by virtue of its being qualified by them, or for faculties (quwa) in the soul that are their principles. For example, it is sometimes said that the material intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani) is the preparedness of the soul for receiving necessary sciences, and sometimes that it is a preparatory faculty (quwwa isti'dadiyya), or a faculty whose characteristic is pure preparedness, and sometimes that it is the soul at the beginning of innate disposition (fitra) from the aspect of its potentiality for sciences. And similarly for the rest. And perhaps it is said that the intellect by disposition (al-'aql bil-malaka) is the obtaining of necessary (sciences) insofar as they lead to theoretical (sciences). And Ibn Sina said: it is the form of the primary intelligibles (suwar al-ma'qulat al-ula), followed by the faculty to acquire others, like light for eyesight, and the acquired intellect (al-mustafad) is the acquired intelligibles when they are obtained in actuality. And he said in the book 'al-Mabda' wal-Ma'ad' that the intellect in actuality (al-'aql bil-fi'l) and the acquired intellect are one in essence (dhat) but different in consideration (i'tibar): for from the aspect of its acquisition of theoretical (sciences) it is intellect in actuality, and from the aspect of their obtaining in it (actually) it is acquired intellect. And perhaps it was said: it is intellect in actuality with respect to itself, and acquired with respect to its agent (fa'il). And they also differed concerning whether what matters in the acquired (intellect) is the presence of all possible theoretical (sciences) to the soul, such that they never absent (so much so) that they said it is the last of human ranks and the first of angelic stations, and that it is impossible or very far-fetched as long as the soul is attached to the body; or (whether it is) mere presence such that it precedes the intellect in actuality according to existence, as the Imam (al-Razi) stated, even though according to honor it is the goal, and the absolute ruler (al-ra'is al-mutlaq) which all other powers—human, animal, and vegetative—serve. And it is not hidden that this is more similar to what they agreed upon of restricting the ranks to four. Indeed, the presence of all such that they never absent at all is the perfection of the acquired rank. And the Imam mentioned in the explanation of the ranks that if the soul is empty of sciences while being receptive to them, it is called in that state material intellect; if only necessary (sciences) are obtained, it is then called intellect by disposition; and if theoretical (sciences) are also obtained, if they are not (yet) obtained in actuality, but (only) in potentiality...
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents the differing scholarly definitions of the intellect's stages. The author first notes the points of disagreement: whether the terms refer to the preparedness and perfection themselves, the soul qualified by them, or faculties in the soul. He then gives examples for the material intellect and intellect by disposition. Next, he reports Ibn Sina's view: the intellect by disposition is the form of primary intelligibles, and the acquired intellect is the obtained intelligibles. Ibn Sina also equates the actual intellect and acquired intellect in essence, differing only in aspect. The author then discusses a further disagreement about the acquired intellect: whether it requires the constant presence of all possible theoretical sciences (which may be impossible while embodied) or merely their presence in principle, allowing it to precede the actual intellect in existence, as stated by Imam al-Razi. The passage ends with Imam al-Razi's own classification, which aligns with the standard four ranks. The author follows the source's structure, presenting each differing opinion in sequence and explaining the nuances.

The Four States of the Soul and Three Stages of the Intellect
Classification of intellectual states and critique of a later definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four states of the theoretical intellect and how they correspond to the three stages described by Ibn Sina, as well as a nuanced critique of a later definition.

Core Idea
The intellect moves from potentiality to actuality through four distinct states.

This passage introduces a fundamental classification of the intellect's stages in Islamic philosophy and theology. The author builds on Ibn Sina's three-stage model but adds a fourth state to capture the difference between possessing theoretical knowledge and actively recalling it. A brief critique of al-Mawaqif's definition shows the author's dedication to precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four States
Read the passage carefully and list the four states of the soul mentioned: emptiness, having necessary knowledge, having theoretical knowledge without active recall, and having theoretical knowledge with active recall.
2
Map to Ibn Sina's Stages
Notice how the author correlates the four states to Ibn Sina's three stages: potential intellect, actual intellect, and acquired intellect. Consider which states collapse into which stage.
The first two states roughly correspond to potential and actual, while the third and fourth both relate to the acquired intellect.
3
Understand the Critique
Pay attention to the author's disagreement with al-Mawaqif's definition of actual intellect as a faculty of derivation. The author claims this definition is not found in earlier authorities.
This shows the author's commitment to the original terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوة الاستحضار بمجرد التوجه سميت النفس حينئذ عقلا بالفعل، وإن كانت النفس (۱) حاضرة سميت النفس عقلاً مستفاداً. فالحالات أربع لا غير حالة(٢) الخلو، وحالة حصول الضروريات وحالة حصول النظريات بدون الحضور وحالة حصولها مع الحضور والمراتب هذه (۳) النفس باعتبارها وهو موافق لما قال ابن سينا، ان النفس تكون عقلاً بالملكة، ثم عقلاً بالفعل، ثم عقلاً مستفاداً، والمعنى أن حالتها مستفادة ، وأما ما ذكر في المواقف من أن العقل بالفعل هو ملكة استنباط النظريات من الضروريات. أي ضرورة العقل، بحيث متى شاء استحضر الضروريات، واستنتج منها النظريات فلم نجده في كلام (٤) القوم .
The power of recollection by mere attention: then the soul is called actual intellect; and if the soul (1) is present, it is called acquired intellect. So the states are four, no more: the state of emptiness, the state of possessing the necessary [elements], the state of possessing the theoretical [elements] without presence, and the state of possessing them with presence. And these ranks (3) of the soul in consideration of it are in agreement with what Ibn Sina said: that the soul is intellect in potentiality, then actual intellect, then acquired intellect, and the meaning is that its state is acquired. As for what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that actual intellect is the faculty of deriving theoretical [knowledge] from necessities—i.e., the necessity of the intellect, such that whenever it wills it summons the necessities and deduces theoretical [knowledge] from them—we did not find it in the speech of the people (4).
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines the four states of the theoretical intellect as they relate to the soul. The author begins by describing two specific conditions: when the soul has the power of recollection through mere attention, it is called 'actual intellect'; when the soul actually possesses knowledge, it is called 'acquired intellect'. Then the four states are enumerated: (1) the state of emptiness (no knowledge), (2) the state of having necessary knowledge (axioms), (3) the state of having theoretical conclusions without presence (i.e., not actively recalled), (4) the state of having theoretical conclusions with presence (actively recalled). The author notes that these correspond to Ibn Sina's three-stage model: intellect in potentiality (al-ʿaql bi-l-malaka), actual intellect (al-ʿaql bi-l-fiʿl), and acquired intellect (al-ʿaql al-mustafād). The text then critiques a definition from al-Mawaqif, which claimed that actual intellect is the faculty of deriving theoretical knowledge from self-evident necessities. The author states that this definition is not found in the works of the earlier authorities ('the people'), implying a disagreement with al-Mawaqif's formulation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the four states are identical to the three stages, but the author clarifies that the four states are a finer division, and the three stages are a summarized version following Ibn Sina.
Correction: The fourth state (theoretical with presence) corresponds to acquired intellect, but the three stages are a condensed model; the author's classification includes a distinction between possessing knowledge and actively recollecting it.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which of the four intellectual states do you find yourself most often regarding your knowledge of Allah and the unseen? What would it take for you to reach the state of presence (حضور) with that knowledge?
?
The author rejects a definition from al-Mawaqif because it was not found in earlier authoritative texts. Are there aspects of your faith that you accept solely from later sources without verifying against the Qur'an and Sunnah? How can you begin to verify them today?
?
Ibn Sina's model describes the intellect's progression from potential to acquired. Does your own pursuit of Islamic knowledge reflect this movement, or are you content with merely possessing information without actively bringing it to mind? What is one practical step you can take this week to move from theoretical knowledge to conscious presence?

The Practical Intellect: Definition and Operation
Definition and explanation of the practical intellect with its relations and an example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the practical intellect, its function in deriving crafts and distinguishing good from evil, and how it relates to the theoretical intellect and other faculties through a concrete example.

Core Idea
The practical intellect is the engine of ethical and productive human action.

This passage from al-Maqasid defines the practical intellect, a key concept in Islamic psychology and ethics. It explains how this faculty handles our daily actions, crafts, and moral choices by deriving particular judgments from universal principles. The author then illustrates its operation with a vivid example.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its structure. Pay attention to the opening definition and the concluding example.
Notice the footnote markers; they indicate additional commentary in the original.
2
Understand the definition and its elaboration
Identify the definition of the practical intellect. Then see how the author elaborates on it, mentioning derivation of crafts and discrimination of interests.
The definition is given in the quoted part within brackets.
3
Examine the three relations
The passage lists three relations: to the appetitive faculty, internal senses, and theoretical faculty. For each, note how the practical intellect interacts with them.
The theoretical faculty provides universal principles, while appetitive and internal senses contribute to action.
4
Follow the example step-by-step
The example shows the derivation from universal premise to particular judgment, desire, and action. Trace each step to see the practical intellect in operation.
This illustrates the practical intellect's dependence on theoretical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (وأما العملي) : وهو قوة التصرف في الموضوعات واستنباط الصناعات، وتمييز الصالح الفاسد لانتظام أمر المعاش والمعاد، فيستعين بالنظري من جهة أن أفاعيله(٥) تنبعث عن آراء جزئية مستنبطة عن الآراء الكلية ] . من يعني أنها قوة بها يتمكن الإنسان من استنباط الصناعات والتصرفات في موضوعاتها، التي هي بمنزلة المواد كالخشب للنجار، وتمييز مصالحه التي يجب الإتيان بها من المفاسد التي يجب الاجتناب عنها، لينتظم بذلك أمر معاشه، ومعاده . وبالجملة هي مبدأ حركة (بدن (٦) الإنسان الى الأفاعيل الجزئية، الخاصة (٧) بالروية، على مقتضى آراء تخصها صلاحية ولها نسبة الى القوة النزوعية، ومنها يتولد الضحك والخجل والبكاء، ونحوها ، ونسبة الى الحواس الباطنة، وهي استعمالها كما (۱) في استخراج أمور مصلحة وصناعات وغيرها، ونسبة الى القوة النظرية. وهي أن أفاعيله. أعني أعماله الاختيارية تنبعث عن آراء جزئية، تستند الى آراء كلية تستنبط من مقدمات أولية، أو تجريبية، أو ذائعة، أو ظنية، تحكم بها القوة النظرية، مثلا يستنبط من قولنا بذل الدرهم جميل، والفعل الجميل أن يصدر عنا، إن بذل الدرهم ينبغي أن يصدر عنا، ثم تحكم بأن هذا الدرهم ينبغي أن أبذله لهذا المستحق، فينبعث من ذلك شوق وإرادة الى بذله، فتقدم القوة المحركة (۲) على دفعه الى المستحق .
[He said: As for the practical [intellect]: it is the power of managing subject matters, deriving crafts, and distinguishing good from corrupt for the ordering of livelihood and afterlife. It seeks help from the theoretical [intellect] in that its acts (5) arise from particular opinions derived from universal opinions.] That is, it is a power by which a person is able to derive crafts and actions in their subject matters, which are like materials, such as wood for the carpenter, and to distinguish his interests that he must perform from corruptions that he must avoid, so that his livelihood and afterlife are ordered by that. In summary, it is the principle of movement of the body (6) of man towards particular actions, specific (7) to deliberation, according to opinions that have fitness for them, and it has a relation to the appetitive faculty, from which arise laughter, shyness, crying, and the like, and a relation to the internal senses—their use (1) in extracting beneficial matters and crafts and others—and a relation to the theoretical faculty: namely, that its acts, I mean his voluntary actions, arise from particular opinions that depend on universal opinions derived from primary, empirical, well-known, or probable premises, which the theoretical faculty judges. For example, one derives from our saying 'Giving a dirham is beautiful' and 'A beautiful action should come from us' that 'Giving a dirham should come from us'; then he judges that 'This dirham should be given to this deserving person', from which arises desire and will to give it, so the motive power (2) proceeds to give it to the deserving person.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines the practical intellect (al-ʿaql al-ʿamalī) and explains its operation. It begins with a quoted definition: the practical intellect is the faculty that manages objects (subject matters), derives crafts, and distinguishes beneficial from harmful for the ordering of worldly life and the afterlife. It relies on the theoretical intellect because its acts arise from particular judgments that are derived from universal judgments. The author then elaborates in his own words: it is a power that enables a person to derive crafts and to manage their subject matters (like wood for a carpenter), and to discern interests to pursue from corruptions to avoid. In summary, it is the principle behind bodily voluntary movements that are specific to deliberation, based on appropriate opinions. The practical intellect has multiple relations: to the appetitive faculty (from which emotions like laughter, shyness, crying arise), to the internal senses (used to extract beneficial things and crafts), and to the theoretical faculty (its voluntary actions arise from particular opinions that are based on universal opinions derived from various types of premises—primary, empirical, well-known, or probable). The author concludes with a concrete example: from the premise that giving a dirham is good and that a good action should come from us, one derives that giving a dirham should come from us; then one judges that a particular dirham should be given to a particular deserving person, which generates desire and will, leading to the motive power performing the act.

Key Terms
العقل العملي
القوة النظرية
القوة النزوعية
القوة المحركة
آراء جزئية
آراء كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the practical intellect operates independently of the theoretical intellect.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that it seeks help from the theoretical faculty and that its actions are based on judgments derived from universal premises.

Checkpoint
What is the primary function of the practical intellect according to the passage?
A) To contemplate abstract truths
B) To manage actions, derive crafts, and distinguish good from corrupt
C) To perceive sensory objects
D) To deduce necessary truths from axioms

Key Takeaway
The practical intellect is the faculty that guides voluntary actions by deriving particular judgments from universal principles, relying on the theoretical intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the example, the practical intellect uses the universal premise 'giving is good' to produce a specific action. Think of a recent ethical decision you made. What universal principle (e.g., honesty is good, patience is virtuous) did you consciously or unconsciously apply? Are you living in alignment with that principle, or do you compromise it? What would it cost you to fully commit to it today?
?
The passage says the practical intellect orders both 'livelihood and afterlife'. When you plan your day or your life, how much attention do you give to the afterlife compared to worldly gain? Is there an imbalance? What one change could you make this week to ensure your actions serve both?
?
The practical intellect relies on the theoretical intellect to provide sound premises. When was the last time you critically examined the 'universal premises' you act upon—like 'success means wealth' or 'comfort is happiness'? Could these premises be flawed? What would it mean to rebuild them on truth?
