Definitions of Theoretical and Practical Wisdom
Sanusi defines theoretical and practical wisdom and gives a unifying definition of wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of theoretical and practical wisdom and the general concept of wisdom as encompassing both knowledge and action.

Core Idea
Wisdom is the soul's perfection through knowledge and right action.

This passage opens by distinguishing the two main branches of wisdom derived from the intellect. The author provides precise definitions that will serve as the foundation for the subsequent classification of sciences and virtues.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the two definitions
Locate the definitions of theoretical and practical wisdom and note how they differ.
Theoretical wisdom is about knowing, practical wisdom is about doing.
3
Understand the general definition
The author then gives a single definition of wisdom that combines knowledge and action. Why do you think he does this?
This shows wisdom is both theoretical and practical.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these definitions are the starting point for the entire discussion on wisdom.
They set the stage for the detailed classification to come.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويتفرع على النظري) : الحكمة النظرية المفسرة بمعرفة الأشياء كما هي بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وعلى العملي. الحكمة العملية المفسرة بالقيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي بقدرها . فمن ههنا يقال : إن الحكمة هي خروج النفس من القوة الى الفعل في كمالها الممكن، وأن اسم للعلم والعمل جميعاً(۳) وقد يقال : الحكمة العملية لمعرفة الأمور المتعلقة باختيارنا وتخص النظرية بما ليس كذلك فإن تعلقت بما يستغنى عن المادة ذهنياً، وخارجاً فما بعد الطبيعة. أو ذهناً فقط. فالرياضي، أو يحتاج فيهما، فالطبيعي والعملية إن تعلقت بإصلاح الشخص فتهذيب الأخلاق أو المشاركين في المنزل، فتدبير المنزل، أو المدنية، فسياسة المدن الفاضلة، اعتدال القوة الشهوية وهي العفة والغضبية وهي الشجاعة والنطقية، وهي الحكمة ومجموعها العدالة ولكل طرف إفراط وتفريط هما رذيلة، فللعفة الخمود والفجور، وللشجاعة التهور والجين وللحكمة الجريزة والغباوة ] . هذه التعريفات خاصة بعلماء الكلام وإلا فإن للحكمة تعريفات عدة عند علماء اللغة، وكذلك الفقه. وفي كتاب الله تعالى: ﴿ ومن يؤتي الحكمة فقد أوتى خيراً كثيراً) ويقول الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم من يرد الله به خيراً يفقهه في الدين»
He said: And there branch from the theoretical [intellect]: theoretical wisdom, interpreted as knowing things as they are to the extent of human capacity; and from the practical [intellect]: practical wisdom, interpreted as performing affairs as they ought to be to their extent. Hence it is said that wisdom is the soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality in its possible perfection, and that it is a name for knowledge and action both (3). And it is also said: practical wisdom is for knowing matters related to our choice, and theoretical wisdom is specified for what is not like that. For if it is related to what is independent of matter mentally and externally, it is metaphysics; if only mentally, it is mathematics; if it requires [matter] in both, it is natural [philosophy]. And practical [wisdom] if related to the rectification of the individual, it is ethics; or to those sharing in the household, it is domestic economy; or to the city, it is the governance of virtuous cities. The moderation of the appetitive power is chastity, of the irascible power is courage, of the rational power is wisdom, and their combination is justice. Every extreme has excess and deficiency, both of which are vices: for chastity, [the vices are] apathy and licentiousness; for courage, recklessness and cowardice; for wisdom, cunning and stupidity. These definitions are specific to the scholars of kalam; otherwise, wisdom has several definitions among linguists, and similarly for jurisprudence. In the Book of God Almighty: 'Whoever is given wisdom has been given much good' (Quran 2:269), and the Prophet said: 'Whomever God desires good for, He makes him understand the religion.'
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the branches of theoretical and practical wisdom derived from the theoretical and practical intellects. It begins with a statement: from the theoretical intellect branches theoretical wisdom, defined as knowing things as they are to the extent of human capacity; from the practical intellect branches practical wisdom, defined as performing affairs as they ought to be to their extent. Then a general definition of wisdom is given: it is the soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality in its possible perfection, encompassing both knowledge and action. The passage then provides a detailed classification: theoretical wisdom is subdivided by its relation to matter—independent in mind and reality (metaphysics), independent only in mind (mathematics), and requiring matter in both (natural philosophy). Practical wisdom is subdivided by the object of rectification: the individual (ethics), the household (domestic economy), or the city (politics of virtuous cities). The virtues corresponding to the powers of the soul are then explained: moderation of the appetitive power is chastity, of the irascible is courage, of the rational is wisdom, and their combination is justice. For each virtue, there are two vices: for chastity, apathy and licentiousness; for courage, recklessness and cowardice; for wisdom, cunning and stupidity. Finally, the author notes that these definitions are specific to the scholars of kalam, and he references Quranic and prophetic sayings about wisdom.

Key Terms
الحكمة النظرية
الحكمة العملية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of theoretical wisdom?
A) Performing affairs as they ought to be
B) Knowing things as they are to the extent of human capacity
C) The soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality
D) The combination of knowledge and action

Key Takeaway
Wisdom is defined as both knowing reality and acting rightly, and it represents the soul's perfection through actualizing its potential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that wisdom requires both knowledge and action. In which area of your life are you content with just knowing, without taking the step to act? What specific action will you take today to move from potential to actuality?
?
Consider your daily routine: how much of your knowledge about God and the afterlife actually translates into your choices? Where is the gap between what you know and what you do, and can you honestly say you are wise according to this definition?
?
The passage defines wisdom as the soul's perfection. If your soul were to be judged right now, would it be in a state of potential or actuality in terms of obedience and character? What is one concrete habit you will change to actualize your potential?

Subdivisions of Wisdom: Sciences and Disciplines
The author classifies theoretical wisdom into three sciences and practical wisdom into three disciplines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theoretical wisdom is divided into metaphysics, mathematics, and natural philosophy, and how practical wisdom is divided into ethics, domestic economy, and politics.

Core Idea
The branches of wisdom are determined by their subject's relation to matter and human choice.

After establishing the basic definitions, the author now provides a detailed classification that shows how each branch of wisdom deals with a specific aspect of reality or human action. This classification is based on the object's dependence on matter and whether it concerns voluntary actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the passage that begins with 'وقد يقال' and traces the subdivisions.
Pay attention to the criteria used for division.
2
Understand the criteria for theoretical wisdom
Theoretical wisdom is subdivided based on whether its objects are independent of matter mentally and externally, only mentally, or require matter in both.
3
List the three theoretical sciences
Identify metaphysics, mathematics, and natural philosophy and match them to the criteria.
4
Understand the criteria for practical wisdom
Practical wisdom is subdivided based on the scope of rectification: individual, household, or city.
5
List the three practical disciplines
Identify ethics, domestic economy, and politics.

Virtues and Vices of the Soul's Powers
The author enumerates the three powers of the soul, their moderate virtues, the extreme vices, and adds a note on the specificity of these definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main powers of the soul, the virtues that result from their moderation, the corresponding vices, and the note that these definitions are specific to kalam scholars.

Core Idea
Virtue is the mean between two extremes of excess and deficiency.

The passage now turns to the moral dimension of wisdom, explaining how the cardinal virtues are products of balanced powers. It then concludes with a reminder that these definitions belong to the kalam tradition, not necessarily to other fields.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on virtues
Read from 'اعتدال القوة الشهوية' to the end, noting the three powers and their virtues.
2
Identify the three powers
The appetitive, irascible, and rational powers are mentioned. Note the virtue for each.
3
List the vices for each virtue
For each virtue, there are two vices: one of excess and one of deficiency.
E.g., for chastity: apathy (deficiency) and licentiousness (excess).
4
Understand the role of justice
Justice is the combination of all three virtues.
5
Note the concluding remark
The author states these definitions are specific to kalam scholars and then quotes Quran and hadith.
This highlights the contextual nature of the classification.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ويتفرع على النظري) : الحكمة النظرية المفسرة بمعرفة الأشياء كما هي بقدر الطاقة البشرية، وعلى العملي. الحكمة العملية المفسرة بالقيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي بقدرها . فمن ههنا يقال : إن الحكمة هي خروج النفس من القوة الى الفعل في كمالها الممكن، وأن اسم للعلم والعمل جميعاً(۳) وقد يقال : الحكمة العملية لمعرفة الأمور المتعلقة باختيارنا وتخص النظرية بما ليس كذلك فإن تعلقت بما يستغنى عن المادة ذهنياً، وخارجاً فما بعد الطبيعة. أو ذهناً فقط. فالرياضي، أو يحتاج فيهما، فالطبيعي والعملية إن تعلقت بإصلاح الشخص فتهذيب الأخلاق أو المشاركين في المنزل، فتدبير المنزل، أو المدنية، فسياسة المدن الفاضلة، اعتدال القوة الشهوية وهي العفة والغضبية وهي الشجاعة والنطقية، وهي الحكمة ومجموعها العدالة ولكل طرف إفراط وتفريط هما رذيلة، فللعفة الخمود والفجور، وللشجاعة التهور والجين وللحكمة الجريزة والغباوة ] . هذه التعريفات خاصة بعلماء الكلام وإلا فإن للحكمة تعريفات عدة عند علماء اللغة، وكذلك الفقه. وفي كتاب الله تعالى: ﴿ ومن يؤتي الحكمة فقد أوتى خيراً كثيراً) ويقول الرسول - صلى الله عليه وسلم من يرد الله به خيراً يفقهه في الدين»
He said: And there branch from the theoretical [intellect]: theoretical wisdom, interpreted as knowing things as they are to the extent of human capacity; and from the practical [intellect]: practical wisdom, interpreted as performing affairs as they ought to be to their extent. Hence it is said that wisdom is the soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality in its possible perfection, and that it is a name for knowledge and action both (3). And it is also said: practical wisdom is for knowing matters related to our choice, and theoretical wisdom is specified for what is not like that. For if it is related to what is independent of matter mentally and externally, it is metaphysics; if only mentally, it is mathematics; if it requires [matter] in both, it is natural [philosophy]. And practical [wisdom] if related to the rectification of the individual, it is ethics; or to those sharing in the household, it is domestic economy; or to the city, it is the governance of virtuous cities. The moderation of the appetitive power is chastity, of the irascible power is courage, of the rational power is wisdom, and their combination is justice. Every extreme has excess and deficiency, both of which are vices: for chastity, [the vices are] apathy and licentiousness; for courage, recklessness and cowardice; for wisdom, cunning and stupidity. These definitions are specific to the scholars of kalam; otherwise, wisdom has several definitions among linguists, and similarly for jurisprudence. In the Book of God Almighty: 'Whoever is given wisdom has been given much good' (Quran 2:269), and the Prophet said: 'Whomever God desires good for, He makes him understand the religion.'
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the branches of theoretical and practical wisdom derived from the theoretical and practical intellects. It begins with a statement: from the theoretical intellect branches theoretical wisdom, defined as knowing things as they are to the extent of human capacity; from the practical intellect branches practical wisdom, defined as performing affairs as they ought to be to their extent. Then a general definition of wisdom is given: it is the soul's emergence from potentiality to actuality in its possible perfection, encompassing both knowledge and action. The passage then provides a detailed classification: theoretical wisdom is subdivided by its relation to matter—independent in mind and reality (metaphysics), independent only in mind (mathematics), and requiring matter in both (natural philosophy). Practical wisdom is subdivided by the object of rectification: the individual (ethics), the household (domestic economy), or the city (politics of virtuous cities). The virtues corresponding to the powers of the soul are then explained: moderation of the appetitive power is chastity, of the irascible is courage, of the rational is wisdom, and their combination is justice. For each virtue, there are two vices: for chastity, apathy and licentiousness; for courage, recklessness and cowardice; for wisdom, cunning and stupidity. Finally, the author notes that these definitions are specific to the scholars of kalam, and he references Quranic and prophetic sayings about wisdom.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any form of anger is bad, but the passage shows that anger can be moderated into courage.
Correction: Courage is the balanced state of the irascible power; recklessness and cowardice are its vices.

Checkpoint

A) Apathy
B) Licentiousness
C) Cowardice
D) Stupidity

Key Takeaway
Virtue arises from moderating the soul's powers, and each virtue has two extreme vices; these ethical concepts are part of the kalam tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine your own soul. Which of the three powers—desire, anger, or reason—do you struggle most to keep balanced? In that area, do you tend toward excess or deficiency? What is one specific action you will take this week to move toward the moderate virtue?
?
When you use your intelligence to get what you want at the expense of truth, you are falling into the excess of wisdom: cunning. Can you recall a recent moment when you did this? How can you repent and realign your reason toward wisdom?
?
Justice (عدالة) requires all three virtues to be present. Is there a virtue in your life that is overshadowing others? For example, are you so courageous that you neglect reason? Or so focused on chastity that you become cowardly? What would a just soul look like for you, and what is the first step to achieving it?

The Many Meanings of Hikma
Compilation of early opinions on the meaning of 'hikma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse interpretations of the Quranic term 'hikma' (wisdom) as presented by early Islamic scholars.

Core Idea
Hikma is not a single concept; scholars saw it as fear of God, intellect, Sunnah, and more.

In this passage, the author compiles various early scholarly opinions on the meaning of 'hikma' as it appears in the Quran. Each scholar offers a different perspective, revealing the richness and depth of the term. As you read, pay attention to the diversity of interpretations and the evidence each scholar brings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the passage reports multiple sayings without explicit commentary.
The passage is a compilation of transmitted opinions, not a single argument.
2
Identify each scholar's interpretation
List the different meanings of 'hikma' mentioned: knowledge, fear of God, understanding, Sunnah, intellect, fiqh, prophethood. Notice the variety.
Some scholars offer multiple opinions, like Abu al-'Aliyah.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why early scholars differed. What does this diversity teach us about Quranic interpretation and theological method?
This shows the richness of Islamic intellectual tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرى بعض العلماء أنها ليست النبوة ولكنه العلم والفقه والقرآن، وقال أبو العالية: الحكمة: خشية الله فإن خشية الله رأس كل حكمة، وقد روى ابن مردويه من طريق بقية عن عثمان بن زفر الجهني عن أبي عمار الأساسي عن ابن مسعود مرفوعاً (رأس الحكمة مخافة الله) وقال أبو العالية في رواية عنه: المحكمة الكتاب والفهم. وقال إبراهيم النخعي: الحكمة: الفهم، وقال أبو مالك: الحكمة السنة، وقال ابن وهب عن مالك. قال زيد بن أسلم الحكمة العقل. قال مالك، وإنه ليقع في قلبي أن الحكمة هو الفقه في دين الله وأمر يدخله الله في القلوب من رحمته وفضله. وقال السدي الحكمة النبوة. وجاء في بعض الأحاديث: من حفظ القرآن فقد أدرجت النبوة بين كتفيه غير أنه لا يوحي إليه. رواه وكيع بن الجراح في تفسيره عن إسماعيل من رافع.
And some scholars see that it is not prophethood but rather knowledge, fiqh, and the Quran. Abu al-'Aliyah said: Wisdom is the fear of God, for the fear of God is the head of all wisdom. And Ibn Mardawayh narrated via Baqiyya from 'Uthman ibn Zufar al-Juhani from Abu 'Ammar al-Asadi from Ibn Mas'ud as a marfu' hadith: 'The head of wisdom is the fear of God.' And Abu al-'Aliyah said in another narration: al-Muhkamah is the Book and understanding. Ibrahim al-Nakha'i said: Wisdom is understanding. Abu Malik said: Wisdom is the Sunnah. Ibn Wahb narrated from Malik: Zayd ibn Aslam said: Wisdom is intellect. Malik said: And it occurs to my heart that wisdom is understanding in the religion of God and a matter that God enters into the hearts from His mercy and bounty. Al-Suddi said: Wisdom is prophethood. And it comes in some hadith: 'Whoever memorizes the Quran, prophethood has been inserted between his shoulders, except that he is not inspired.' Narrated by Waki' ibn al-Jarrah in his Tafsir from Isma'il ibn Rafi'.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of various early interpretations of the term 'hikma' (wisdom) as it appears in the Quranic verse. It begins by noting that some scholars distinguish it from prophethood, identifying it instead with knowledge, fiqh, and the Quran. Then it presents the opinion of Abu al-'Aliyah that wisdom is fear of God (taqwa), supporting it with a hadith. Other opinions include understanding (al-fahm), the Sunnah, intellect ('aql), and even prophethood itself. A hadith is quoted linking memorization of the Quran to the station of prophethood. These diverse views illustrate the richness of the term and its centrality in Islamic thought.

Classification of Dreams in Sleep
Sanusi classifies dreams into three types based on the imaginative faculty's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul perceives during sleep and the three categories of dreams as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Dreams are classified into true, interpretable, and confused based on image alteration.

The author begins by explaining that in sleep, the senses become still, allowing the soul to connect to the unseen world. He then distinguishes three types of dreams depending on whether the perceptual image remains unchanged, is altered but analyzable, or is distorted beyond recovery. This passage introduces the foundational classification that shapes the Islamic understanding of dream interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the mechanism of perception
Read the passage from the beginning through the classification. Note how the soul connects to the unseen due to stillness of senses.
The imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila) plays a key role.
2
Identify the three dream types
Find the three types: true dream, dream requiring interpretation, and confused dreams. Note the condition for each.
The author uses conditional phrases: 'if it remains...' for true, 'if altered but traceable...' for interpretable, 'otherwise...' for confused.
3
Consider physical causes of confused dreams
Read the example of yellow bile. Understand that some dreams arise from bodily humors, not from spiritual connection.
This is important to distinguish meaningful dreams from those caused by temperament.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقالوا في إدراكاتها المتعلقة بالنوم :
إنها تتصل بعالم الغيب لركود الحواس، فيحصل لها صورة إدراكية جزئية في نفسها، أو بجعل المتخيلة، فإن بقيت على حالها، بحيث لا تتفاوت في المجعولة إلا بالكنية والجزئية، وتأدت إلى الحس المشترك فرؤيا صادقة، وإن تصرف فيها المتخيلة بتبديل الصور، فإن أمكن أن تعاد إلى الأصل بضرب من التحليل، فرؤيا تعبير، وإلا فأضغاث أحلام، ومن الأضغاث ما يرد على الحس المشترك من الصور المرتسمة في الخيال بالإحساس أو بالانتقال إليه من المتخيلة في النوم، حاصلة كانت قبل أو حادثة فيها عند النوم، لتغير أفعالها بتغير مزاج الروح الحامل إياها، كما يرى عند غلبة الصفراء من الأشياء الصفر مثلا].
إشارة إلى القسم الثاني، وبيان ذلك أن النفس لاشتغالها بالتفكر فيما تورد عليها الحواس، قلما تفرغ للاتصال بالجواهر الروحانية، فعند ركود الحواس بسبب انحباس الروح الحاملة لقوة الحس عنها، تتصل النفس بتلك الجواهر وينطبع فيها ما فيها من صور الأشياء، سيما ما هو أليق بتلك النفس من أحوالها، وأحوال ما يقرب منها من الأهل والولد والمال والبلد ، وتلك الصور قد تكون جزئية في نفسها وقد تكون كلية تحاكيها المتخيلة بصور جزئية، ثم تنطبع في الخيال وتنتقل إلى الحس المشترك فتصير مشاهدة فإن كانت الصورة المشاهدة
He said: (And they said regarding its perceptions related to sleep: It connects to the world of the unseen due to the stillness of the senses, so an individual perceptual image occurs in it itself, or by the action of the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila). If it remains as it is, such that it does not differ in the fabricated (image) except by content and particularity, and it reaches the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), then it is a true dream (ruʾyā ṣādiqa). If the imaginative faculty acts upon it by changing images, then if it is possible to return it to the original through some analysis, it is a dream requiring interpretation (ruʾyā taʿbīr); otherwise, it is confused dreams (aḍghāth aḥlām). Among the confused dreams are those that come to the common sense from images imprinted in the imagination by sensation or by transmission from the imaginative faculty during sleep, whether they existed before or occurred during sleep, due to change in its actions by change of temperament of the spirit (al-rūḥ) carrying it, as one sees when yellow bile predominates, yellow things, for example.) This is an indication of the second division, and an explanation that the soul, because it is occupied with reflecting upon what the senses bring to it, rarely has leisure to connect with the spiritual substances (al-jawāhir al-rūḥāniyya). So when the senses are still due to the confinement of the spirit carrying the power of sensation from them, the soul connects with those substances and imprints in itself the images of things that are in them, especially what is most suitable to that soul of its states and the states of those close to it such as family, child, wealth, and country. Those images may be particular in themselves or universal, which the imaginative faculty imitates with particular images, then they are imprinted in the imagination and transmitted to the common sense, becoming observed. If the observed image is...
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the soul's perceptions during sleep and a classification of dreams. It begins by stating that the soul connects to the world of the unseen (ʿālam al-ghayb) when the senses are at rest, producing an individual perceptual image either directly or through the imaginative faculty (al-mutakhayyila). The author then classifies dreams into three types: (1) A true dream (ruʾyā ṣādiqa) occurs when the image remains unchanged as it reaches the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak). (2) A dream requiring interpretation (ruʾyā taʿbīr) occurs when the imaginative faculty alters the image, but it can be traced back to the original through analysis. (3) Confused dreams (aḍghāth aḥlām) are those that cannot be traced back, including images that arise from the imagination due to physical conditions (e.g., seeing yellow things when yellow bile predominates). The passage then elaborates on the second division, explaining that when awake, the soul is preoccupied with sensory input, so it rarely connects to spiritual substances (al-jawāhir al-rūḥāniyya). In sleep, the sensory spirit is confined, allowing the soul to connect and imprint images of things, especially those relevant to itself and its surroundings. These images may be particular or universal, and the imaginative faculty represents universals as particular images, which then reach the common sense and become visible. The text ends mid-sentence, continuing the explanation.

المتخيلة
الحس المشترك
الرؤيا الصادقة
رؤيا تعبير
أضغاث أحلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All dreams carry meaningful messages or prophetic insights.
Correction: The author distinguishes true and interpretable dreams from confused dreams that arise from physical conditions like bile imbalance.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes a dream requiring interpretation (ruʾyā taʿbīr) from a confused dream (aḍghāth aḥlām)?
A) The image is altered but can be traced back to an original through analysis
B) The image remains unchanged
C) The image originates from the soul directly
D) The image is caused by physical humors

Key Takeaway
Dreams are classified into three types based on how the imaginative faculty processes the image: true, interpretable, and confused, with the latter often arising from physical conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think back to a recent dream that felt significant. Using Sanusi's classification, which category does it likely fall into? Be honest—does it pass the test of being unchanged, or is it more likely a product of your physical state or random imagery? What does that reveal about your tendency to assign spiritual meaning to ordinary experiences?
?
The lesson explains that confused dreams can come from physical conditions like yellow bile. How often do you consider your diet, stress levels, or sleep hygiene before interpreting your dreams as spiritual messages? What would it mean for you to take more responsibility for the physical factors that shape your inner life?
?
If you were to discard every dream that could not be traced back to an original image through analysis, how many of your dreams would you consider 'true' or 'interpretable'? Does that question unsettle you? Why is it hard to let go of the idea that every dream carries a hidden meaning?

The Soul's Connection to Spiritual Substances in Sleep
Sanusi explains the soul's connection to spiritual substances and image formation in the second division of dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul connects to spiritual substances during sleep and how images are formed from them.

Core Idea
In sleep, the soul imprints images from spiritual substances, which become visible dreams.

The author continues by explaining the second division, focusing on the soul's interaction with spiritual substances (al-jawāhir al-rūḥāniyya). He describes how the soul, when not occupied with sensory input, connects to these substances and receives images that are either particular or universal. The imaginative faculty then converts universals into particular images, which are imprinted in the imagination and transmitted to the common sense, becoming observed as dreams. This passage details the mechanism behind meaningful dreams.

Guided Path
1
Understand why the soul needs sleep to connect
Read how the soul is preoccupied with sensory input when awake, preventing connection to spiritual substances.
The confinement of the spirit carrying sensation allows connection.
2
Identify the source of images
The soul imprints images from spiritual substances, particularly those relevant to its own states and surroundings.
Images may be particular or universal.
3
Trace the image formation process
Follow the path from spiritual substance to imagination to common sense, noting the role of the imaginative faculty in converting universals.
The final step is the image becoming observed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the soul rarely connect to spiritual substances during waking hours?
A) The soul is too busy with sensory reflection
B) The spiritual substances are inactive
C) The common sense is blocked
D) The imaginative faculty is dominant

Key Takeaway
Meaningful dreams arise when the soul, freed from sensory input in sleep, imprints images from spiritual substances, which are then processed by the imaginative faculty and made visible via the common sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sleep tonight, your soul will connect to spiritual substances. Are you prepared for what images it might imprint? Reflect on the state of your heart and the attachments you have to family, wealth, and country – what do your dreams reveal about your soul's preoccupations? Commit tonight to purify your waking thoughts so that your soul's connection in sleep is with what pleases Allah.
?
The passage says the soul rarely has leisure to connect with spiritual substances due to being occupied with sensory reflections. What sensory distractions occupy your soul during the day that prevent it from ascending to higher realities? What specific step will you take tomorrow to create 'stillness' for your soul?
?
Your imaginative faculty converts universal concepts into particular images in dreams. What universal truths has Allah revealed to you in the Qur'an and Sunnah, and have you let them take shape as particular images in your life? If not, what is holding you back?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Objections to Perpetual Celestial Motion
A series of objections against the necessity of perpetual celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and content of the objections raised against the necessity of perpetual celestial motion.

Core Idea
Objections do not deny motion; they challenge its necessity and proposed cause.

In this passage, the objector systematically challenges key premises of the proof for perpetual celestial motion. Each objection begins with 'We do not concede' (laa nusallim), aiming to show that the proof's conclusions do not necessarily follow. Understanding these objections is crucial for evaluating the strength of the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the flow of objections.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لا نسلّم' (we do not concede).
2
Identify each objection
Go through each 'لا نسلّم' clause. There are at least five distinct points. List them briefly.
The objector also gives alternative possibilities starting with 'لم لا يجوز'.
3
Understand the final requirement
The objector concludes that even if some points are rebutted, the proof fails unless all are rebutted. Note why this is a strong rhetorical move.
This shows the cumulative nature of the objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه. أنا لا نسلّم وجوب دوام حركة السماء، وامتناع انقطاعها، ولا نسلّم أن طلب المحسوس، لا يكون إلا للجذب أو الدفع. لم لا يجوز أن يكون لمعرفته أو التشبه به أو غير ذلك. ولا نسلّم استحالة الشهوة والغضب على الأفلاك. ولا يلزم من تشابه أجزائها في الحقيقة تشابه أحوالها. ولا نسلّم أنه يلزم من عدم نيل ذات المعشوق، أو حاله، حصول اليأس ولا من نيله انقطاع الطلب. لم لا يجوز أن يدوم الرجاء، أو يكون المعشوق، أو حاله أمراً غير قار، يتحفظ نوعه، بتعاقب الأفراد كما ذكرتم في الشبه. ولا نسلّم أن المعشوق الموصوف بصفات كمال غير متناهية هو العقل، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان ذلك على الاجتماع دون التعاقب، وبعض هذه وإن أمكن دفعه، لكن لا يتم المطلوب إلا بدفع الكل.
And the objection against it: We do not concede the necessity of the perpetual motion of the heaven and the impossibility of its cessation; nor do we concede that the desire for the sensible object is only for attraction or repulsion. Why might it not be for knowledge of it, or for assimilation to it, or something else? Nor do we concede the impossibility of appetite and anger in the celestial spheres. Nor does the similarity of their parts in essence necessitate similarity of their states. Nor do we concede that from the failure to attain the beloved's essence or state despair necessarily follows, nor that from attaining it pursuit ceases. Why might hope not continue, or the beloved or its state be something non-permanent, whose type is preserved through the succession of individuals, as you mentioned concerning similars? Nor do we concede that the beloved described with infinite perfect attributes is the intellect; that would only follow if those attributes were united simultaneously, not successively. Even if some of these objections can be rebutted, the desired conclusion is not achieved unless all are rebutted.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is an objection to a previous proof that the heavens are perpetually in motion due to a love for an infinite beloved. The objector begins by stating that he does not concede the necessity of perpetual motion or the impossibility of its cessation. He then denies the premise that desire for a sensible object is only for attraction or repulsion, suggesting it could be for knowledge, imitation, or other reasons. Next, he does not concede that appetite and anger are impossible for celestial spheres, and he argues that similarity of parts does not imply similarity of states. He further challenges the connection between attaining the beloved and the cessation of pursuit: despair need not follow from non-attainment, nor cessation from attainment, as hope could continue or the beloved could be non-permanent, preserved by succession. Finally, he denies that the beloved with infinite perfections must be the intellect, unless those perfections are all simultaneously present. He concludes that even if some points are rebutted, the overall argument fails unless all are rebutted.

Key Terms
لا نسلّم
الجذب والدفع
الشهوة والغضب
المعشوق
التعاقب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a denial of celestial motion itself.
Correction: The objection is not a denial of celestial motion, but a challenge to the necessity of its perpetuity and the specific cause proposed.

Checkpoint
What does the objector deny about the desire for the sensible object?
A) It exists at all.
B) It is only for attraction or repulsion.
C) It is for knowledge of the beloved.
D) It leads to despair.

Key Takeaway
The success of the original proof requires rebutting every single objection, not just some.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What are you unwilling to concede in your own beliefs about God? Could it be that your reluctance stems from a fear of having to change your entire worldview? This lesson shows that even a single unaddressed objection can weaken an entire argument. Are you willing to examine your most cherished assumptions with the same rigor the objector applies here? Before you finish this reflection, write down one belief you hold that you've never seriously doubted, and ask yourself: what would it take for me to reconsider this?
?
The objector suggests that desire for the beloved might be for knowledge or imitation, not just attraction or repulsion. What is the ultimate object of your desire? Is it truly God, or are you using Him as a means to something else? If your desire is for Him, does it lead you to perpetual pursuit or to rest? Are you satisfied with a static relationship with God, or do you yearn for deeper knowledge and imitation? Commit to a specific action today that reflects a desire for God's attributes, not just His gifts.
?
The passage ends by warning that the desired conclusion is not achieved unless all objections are rebutted. When you face doubts about your faith, do you tend to brush aside the hard ones and focus on the easy answers? What would it look like for you to honestly confront the one objection you find most troubling? This week, set aside time to write down that objection and seek a thoughtful response, rather than avoiding it.
